frequent occurrence, especially in children. In the treat-

remedies failed in many oases to give relief, and I was very

great benefit.—JAMES SMEAL, Surgeon, &c., Ararat

THE RED PAGE.
RONDEAU: LOVE’S ONWARD DAT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Love’s onward day ; as sum of morn
Kissing in dew Spring’s budded thorn,
Flooding the verdure osf Earth’s breast—
Pearly the East, purpfle the West—
So wakes new life when love is born.
As noon of day—one cloud forlorn
Straying about the mountain crest—
Full trees, rich bio,bins and fields of corn,
Love’s onward day.
As noon to sober eve has worn,
And birds arfe silent in the nest,
And winds ?.re so unmov’d in rest
That eve’s lig(ht cloud-woof scarce is torn
Such restfulness, such peace, adorn
Love’s onward day.
M.L.
AHAD.
A New Theory of Poetry.
M~R-/ LIDDELL, an English scholar, an
I Wfi editor of Chaucer, and recently Professor
pf English at the University of Texas,
is dissatisfied (says London Daily
Chronfele) with our prosody and our criticism of
poetry. The former dates from the sixteenth
centpry, when, in Mr. Liddell’s words, “ Chaucer
was/ read as Elizabethan English. . . . English
models and English rules were therefore out of the
question as the basis of a.normal theory of poetry.”
He regards that prosody as being still “merely
the application, with some modification, of classic
orosody to English rhythms.”
') Our criticism is still more antiquated, he thinks,
<thd.still more absurd. It is full of phrases which
describe poetry as “ a thing of God,” “ the inner
thought of things, ”or “ something divine;” Words-
worth has called it “the finer spirit of knowledge.”
Even at this day,” says the Professor of English at
Texas, the poet is comparatively rare ; we need
not, therefore, wonder that the Greeks and
Romans, “who brought the supernatural into
almost every relation of human life,” should make
poetry divin® ; and we are the posthumous
victims of this ridiculous notion. Mr. Liddell
would change all this and enter upon poetry as a
field for study, “much like.that of Economics and
Ethics.”
He does this with a sympathetic and analytic
skill quite astonishing in an English critic,
and compares the methods of study with
that of biology rather than of ' meta-
physics ; for, in his own words, “the develop-
ment of poetry is intimately associated with
the subtlest activities of the most complex
arrangement of life-material known to man,” a
phrase not superior by much to those which he
disdainfully quotes above. He shows occasionally
an English critic’s fondness for metaphor and
imaginative generalisation, and a German critic’s
fondness for an extravagantly technical vocabu-
lary ; but in the development of his scheme these
weaknesses stand alone, if we ignore a few slight
faults of detail. The most important of the
earlier chapters deals with the fundamental ele-
ments of verse form. He examines Hebrew and
Old English poetry with a view to establishing
the fact: —
That the aesthetic arrangement of the thought-moments
is as vital an element of it (English poetry) as are the
aesthetic arrangements of stressed and unstressed sylla-
bles which mark these moments off for us.
He speaks of “ thought-moments ” instead of
“ phrases,” because “ it is important here to disso-
ciate them from words and to think of them as
groups of notions.” By way of illustration, he
quotes a from Beowulf, which exhibits
“ cartaingvvell-defined moments of more or less
length, ” sfven in a rough translation. In a chapter
on the punctuating elements of verse form, he
points but that English quantity is bf “ too shift-
ing Tend uncertain a character for poetry to
build on”; an experiment with the metronome
proves that “ proportionate time equivalents
between long and short vowels in English is a
mere fiction of the imagination. ” The importance
of “ the thought-moment structure ” is nowhere
more ingeniously shown than in an analysis of
three lines of Walt Whitman :
Blow | Blow | Blow,
Blow up | sea winds | aiong Paumonock’s shore,
I wait 1 and I wait | till you blow my mate to me.
Here thought-moments are marked by a printer’s
device. Again, he quotes :
Our birth is but a sleep and a forgetting, ...
And fade into the light of common day,
and, we believe, he proves that those nineteen
lines “stripped of their rhythmic cadence as well
as of their rhyme, and reduced to a form like that
of Whitman’s verse, would still be a thing of
beauty ” ; certainly, at least, if unassisted by a
division into lines, they would equally impress
the sensitive student of poetry. Ur. Liddell goes
further:
The English mind will not recognise a series of rhythmic
impulses as a verse Jorm unless they are applied to an
already .esthetic thought-moment structure, though it
will vaguely receive rhythmic successions ol thought-
moments, punctuated only by emotion-pulses as an essen
tially sesthetic, though unusual, form of poetic expression.
Discussing the punctuating system of English
verse, he examines the lines—
So shalt thou feed on Death that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there’s no more dying then.
The only naturally and absolutely accented
syllable is “ dy.” If we judge by quantity, the
first/ line is a “ trochaic-spondaic-—iambic—
pyrrhie.” line of ten syllables. If we judge by
natural emphasis, , the line is made up of a trochee,
two spondees, an iambus, and a spondee. Only a
very young child could make it thoroughly satisfy
our prosody and read it thus—
So shalt thou feed on Death that feeds on men,
And Death once dead, there’s no more dying then.
Mr. Liddell concludes that “the characteristic
which punctuates English thought-moments into
sesthetic form is neither quantity nor accent, nor
yet accentually determined quantity.” The prin-
ciple which takes the place of these, in everyone’s
practice, and in Mr. Liddell’s theory, is that of
stress. Quite naturally, without education, we
distribute stress “with unerring accuracy of
position, and deftly shaded precision of inten-
sity. ” He considers its importance in these words
from Othello :
My lord is not my lord.
If we can only make use of accent ana lack of
accent, the thing is meaningless. Stress applied
di , ffer f rlt (e.g., 1 the stronger,
and 2 the weaker) makes it intelligible •
1 1 2
My lord is not my lord.
The writer is then at pains to divide primary
stress under ten different heads, which we mention
V n ° rder t 0 illu strate the

We confess that we think that the line

Kv \°n Sa ! ing , s that his book is more
Hi.* 0 ™ tereBfc and serve the student of
Lnglish poetry than any we have seen before
it it were necessary to have a standard of
poetry, we should say that there is an excel-
lent one between these covers. It is not a
new system of prosody, though it makes the old
one ridiculous. It affords no basis for a classifica-
tion of metres ; it leaves blank verse, as before,
an infinitely varied line usually of ten syllables.
Its strongest quality is that it states the grounds
for the appreciation of verse which have been used
mthil 'T cr \ tlc ? of , evei T it states them
subtly and amply for the first time, and in such a
way as not to offend those who are far from
wishing to study poetry as they study economics.
Australian National Character.
AN article on The Beginnings of an Aus-
tralian National Character ” in the Nine-
teenth Century for September is mostly
misleading Instancies;—
iJlj becoming an Australian, the Englishman in Aus
Austoalian. me yPer ' SenSltlV6 60 Criticisni of eve O'thing
The proof given of this is that Max O’Rell’s
El HX f iTed . a better reception in
England than his work on Australia received here
Notable proof, truly ! As a matter of fact, the
-t- 1? ’ °" u the a , ver^? e ’ much less suscept-
ible to criticism than the Englishman, because of
the extra touch of cynicism in his character. It
is not that he is les3 vain-glorious; but he
doesn t care to a greater degree.
Another astounding statement :
in the Australian landscape is stranger to the
Northern visitor than its lack of half-tones, of the subtle
MMances, of a thousand hues that give their beautv to
Welsh hills or English lakes. Therels little reticence in
the Australian landscape. If Nature will have a tint
yellow, it lsyellow, and no doubt of it; whereas in Ene-
J yf ld K I U S 0f l e " thou ffh. after first suggesting yellow
she bethought herself that some faint shade of green were
purple mtlue enWaS alteri “ g ain
Yet nearly everybody is agreed that the essential
quality of the Australian landscape is its subtle
colour and its lightness.
The article proceeds to find the same lack of
character— tlCenCB reserve in the Australian
~Jio e A raCe 1 ° f ,nystc T y t . hat half revea -is and half con-
ceals the charm and mind of an English girl will be
found comparatively seldom in the' more downright
-beauties of the southern continent. '
A crowning absurdity is the statement that
Australians have no great gift of hunior, and that
Mr. tx. H. Retails our only humorist as yet.”
Mr. Reid our only humorist! What about Mr
Harry Rickards and Mr. Bland Holt ? Hoes the
Nineteenth Century writer know the meaning of
humor . Conclusion of his article shows that he
knows nothing of the demands of his subject,
hor, atter giving a few more or less correct but
always trifling observations, he says nothing at all
in discussion of the real forces which go to make
up national character.
M. J. K Huysmans (of “ En Route”) on Zola :
I was once his ardent disciple, Hut, although severed
from him, I must give him that tribute which a good man
deserves. He was generous and ever ready to render
services, especially to young authors. He "liked to be
surrounded by his friends, and never posed. But, above
all, he was absorbed by his work, although he wrote com-
paratively little each day. When he began the Rougon-
Macquart series he amazed us by a forecast of the whole
work, with the genealogical tree which afterwards ap-
peared in his “ Docteur Pascal.” He was haunted b\ r an
ambition to be a playwright, and few people now remem-
her “ Bouton de Rose,” which was produced at the
Palais Royal, and was a black failure. Of late years his
physical appearance quite ebanged. He used to be obese,
and fond of good living. He suddenly became as abste-
mi us as a Carthusian, and seemed to gradually melt
whils. the wrinkles upon his forehead became more
piominent.
Mr. Wilson Dobbs writes :
In “Answers to Correspondents” (B. 8/11/02) this
occurs, inter alia : —“ The Celtic Irishman and the High-
land Scot are vaguely supposed to represent a Spanish
br Belgic invasion,” and “ Erse, in any case, is Gaelic, not
Irish.” It is certainly conceded by ethnologists that an
Iberian or Basque population, spread over the whole Of
what is now called Great Britain and Ireland, in the
Neolithic age ; and that the ancient Irish and Scotch
were closely connected together. It is curious, there-
fore, to note in the lastr edjtion (1902) of Brewer’s
( Reader’s Handbook,” this comment tinder “Erse” :
‘ Whether the Irish came from Scotland, or the Scotch
from Ireland, is one of those knotty points on which the
two nations will never agree.” But surely, if any ques-
tion of the kind is settled, it is that the Scoti (ancient
Irish) crossed from Scotia (Ireland) into Arg.vle (Scotland)
m the 4th century a.d. Becoming the dominant race, they
called the land they had taken Scotia Minor, afterwards
Scotland, and the island they had left, from being
termed Scotia Major, got eventually called Ireland.
And thus Joannes Scotus, who died in 875 a.d., was
also known as “ Erigena ” —that is “ Irishman ” as well as
Scot.” .As to the word “ Erse,” however, you seem to
be under a misapprehension. For it is admittedly an
early Scotch version of the word “ Irish,” many variant
forms being found, some simply variations in spelling,
such as “ Yrische.” “ Erse ”is then considered to either
reproduce the Old English (Anglo-Saxon) “Irish,” or Old
Norse (Old Icelandic), “Irskr,” or possibly descending
*?£<2 a Parallel form, retaining the-vowel of the Old Irish
,o?r n ’.”J reland - Its Scotch use can be traced back to
1 ” 7 > 5 ’ A.p., in Barbour’s “Bruce.” The Gaelic dialect
ot the Highlands being’ of Irish origin, “ Erse ” was the
word applied thereto by the Scotch Lowlanders, also to
the people speaking that dialect, and to their customs.
Hence, in the 18th century', “ Erse” was used in literary
England as the ordinary designation of the Eaeliq of
Scotland. But besides this, it was even then occasionally
extended to the Irish Gaelic, and, at the present time, the
practice is to limit the word “ Erse ” to the ancient
Irish language, and the word “Gaelic” to the
ancien t language of the Scotch Highlanders. T 6 that end
" d '^i' onar ‘ es and philological works, the word
Gadhehc” is employed, to mean that branch of the
Celtic class of the northern division of the Aryan
language, which comprises the Erse (Irish), the Gaelic
(Highland-Scotch), and Manx (Isle of Man). Even the
word Irish ” itself also signifies the Scottish Highlands,
or the Gaelic inhabitants of them, and Halliwell calls
Highlanders and Islesmen “ Irishry.” As late as 1542
A.D. those in the Lowlands who spoke Gaelic were
known as “Erse” or “Irish,” and in its written form
Scotch Gaelic was not clearly distinguished from Irish
till about 1760 A.D. So, too, it is that while the word
Gaelic first became familiar to English readers as denot-
ing the language of the Scottish Highlanders, yet in more
recent times it has been applied to the language of the
Irish Celts also.
“Cash”: Isn’t the “agin’the churches” idea
in The Bulletin in danger of becoming a craze,
or a pose ? I > know the original idea, a tilt
against humbug, not against religion. But it has
got beyond that, and any casual reader would think
the churches in Australia were all humbug ; which
(as a long-deceased scientist was fond of remarking)
“is Absurd.” The other day a very minor poet
was given over half-a-column to explain that he
would rather stay out on the cliffs with a girl than
go to church. This is quite uninteresting
to anyone but himself and the girl ; and
even the value it might have as an expression
of opinion was spoiled by evident underlying
malice. He assumed that if he went to church he
would hear a “fat priest,” who would “mumble,”
which is much as though I should call his girl
(without knowing her) a fat wench with a squint.
I don’t want to bore him with my reasons for
going to church. Why should he be given half-a-
colurnn to bore me with his for not going, especi-
ally when they are on they face of them not
reasons at all, but gratuitous assumptions ? And,
finally, why shouldn’t The Bulletin let all
theology slide, anti as well as pro?
[Which calls for some explanation: The
Bulletin doesn’t look to the “politics” of its
poets. In that department it is the merit of the
expression which alone counts ; and if another
Shelley should send a poetic sample of
Atheism, and another Newman another “Dream
of Gerontius, ” they’d both be gladly welcomed.
If poems in glorification of going to church are
rare, it is because the people who go to churc 1 '
don’t often send us in good verses on that subr
The B. (don’t smile) awaits with an eager we’
a really poetic hymn.]

. But there are some failures more beautiful and

H.G. : Where is the logic in spelling words of
Greek derivatives, such as phonograph, with the
ph ? The F sound in Greek was indicated by a
single letter, and, simply because the language
came to England through the Latin, the English
have followed the Latin system of translating the
Greek F into the Latin Ph, while it should pro-
perly be translated into the English F. I contend
that the correct spelling, according to derivation,
is the phonetic : fonograf, telegraf, fonetic, etc.
[There is precious little “ logic” in any English
spelling. But Greek “phi’’.sound is more sono-
rous and full than English f ; that the Latins
(most exact in matters of language) preserved the
Greek Ph, and used it in addition to their own
“ F,” is sufficient proof of the fact. “F” is just'
as much Latin as “Ph.”]

No Heart. No Energy
Tortured for a Year.
Weakness and Weariness Replaced by
Health and Vigour.
The Case of Mr. I{. TREMAHi
(BY OUR reporter).
Australians are neither short-sighted nor
reckless, and they always think it well to
brush aside irresponsible rumour and gossip
and to enquire without prejudice into the
history of the affair in question. Hearing
that investigation was courted on a certain
matter, a reporter called on Mr. Richard
Tremain, of No. 131 Macquarie-street
South, Surry Hills, who answered a question
by saying
“ When I was living in the northern
district of New South Wales, about five
years ago, my health appeared to be drift-
ing away. I felt that I had neither the
heart nor the energy to do my work, and
the gloom and depression which hung over
me took every pleasant thought out of my
mind. Nearly all day I felt as if I wanted
to be lying down, the drowsiness which
affected me being quite overpouring. It is
awful to have work to do and not have the
necessary vitality to get it out of the way,
and that is exactly what was *the matter
with me. breams at night whenever I
slept took away all the refreshing influence
which rest generally produces, and when I
left my bed in the mornings it was with a
feeling of being utterly exhausted and worn
out, while my limbs ached to an extent
which was almost unbearable. It was
absurd to think of eating any breakfast, for
as soon as I sat down my stomach revolted
at the smell of the food, and I often got up
without touching it. The nasty taste in
my mouth had sometKTng to do with this,
I believe, because later in the day it was
not quite so bad, and then I sometimes ate
a little, but I even felt worse after taking
nourishment than if I had let it alone.”
“ That is remarkable,” asserted the man
with the note-book.
“Not at all, under the circumstances,”
was Mr. Tremain’s reply. “You see, the
trouble was that I could not digest what I
had eaten, and the suffering of pain was
only what was to be expected. I felt it
very much just a little below my neck,
where the shoulder-blades are situated, and
the stomach became distended with gases
which seemed to rise to my chest and
prevent my breath from escaping freely. I
could hardly move myself about for the
terrible aches in my loins, and if I stooped
for any purpose my head became quite
giddy. The wretched headaches which dis-
tressed me seemed to produce a feeling
about my eyes as if they had swollen, and
my nervous system was altogether run
down. It was the wretched condition of
iny nerves which made my life a misery, for
[ became afflicted with the maddening pains
af neuralgia, from wh.ich I suffered for
twelve solid months., .1 was nearly dis-
tracted with it, and as I now got less sleep
than ever I gradually wore away to a com-
parative shadow, and with scarcely an ounce
&f strength in my body. Prom eleven stone
t>dd I came down to nine stone, so that will
tell you how greatly reduced I was. It was
»nly natural that such an alarming state of
iffairs should prey upon my mind, but,
ihank goodness ! when everything looked as
alack as could be, a happy thought sud-
lenly came to my mind. * I will try some
elements Tonic,’ I said to myself.”
“ Why was Clements Tonic suggested to
pour mind ? ”
“ Because people coming to my store had
10 frequently spoken in praise of it, and I
>hought when so many folks agreed on the
fame subject there must be truth in what
they said,- And what a blessing it was that
remembered their conversations, for I can
honestly state 'that several bottles of
Clements Tonic thoroughly cured the
neuralgia which had tortured mejor a year,
and I knew that I was now towed to safety
bo far aa my digestive organs were con-
cerned, as the systems of indigestion soon
began to leave me. To feel a sense of ease
after my meals was a treat which I enjoyed
through Clements Tonic removing the dis-
comforts arising from flatulence, and I was
glad to get rid of the nasty bilious taste of
a morning, for I could now eat heartily of
anything that was put before me After
experiencing weeks and weeks of sleepless-
ness you can understand how grateful I was
to find that the taking of Clements Tonic
meant plenty of sound rest every night, the
mornings bringing feelings of and
animation, which had deserted me for many
months. I made up my mind not to part
company with Clements Tonic so long as
there was anything for it to do, and a final
obliteration of all my aches and discomforts
resulted. Indeed, it was a complete release
from painful captivity brought about solely
by Clements Tonic, which also toned and
strengthened my system so well that all
nervousness vanished. Before this I had
been treated by a doctor, and had also
bought various medicines from chemists,
but I did not get the slightest relief from
any of these physics, the only one to wh ch
I am indebted being Clements Tonic, which
was a perfect success, as it went to all parts
of my body and left health and vigour
where weakness and weariness had dwelt so
long. What I have already told you is
something which the most ungrateful mortal
in the world would be thankful for ; but
there is part of my experience which you
have not yet heard.”
“ I would like very much to hear it, if
you do not mind,” interrupted the reporter.
“ Well, this is it: From the time that I
was sixteen years of age I was subject to
rheumatism. It had me crippled so much
that I could not get out of bed, and for
three years I was almost helpless, although
I had some of the best doctors in the city
attending me. This will help you to form
an idea of the great pressure that had been
placed on my constitution previous to' my
taking Clements Tonic, and I think that
medicine is entitled to all the more credit
for making me as sound as lam to-day. It
was when I was taking Clements Tonic for
my other ailments that I discovered its in-
trinsic value as a remedy for rheumatic
pains, for, as I said before, it found its way
to all parts of my body and gave me relief,
and as this came after trying all sorts, of
medicines to no purpose, it is easy to under-
stand why I regard Clements Tonic as the
most effective physic. Mine was a com-
plication of complaints of a most obstinate
and distressing character, and as Clements
Tonic proved itself to be master of them all
I think I would be doing a wrong to the
public if I did not place my experience on
record, so that anybody in a similar position
can act as he thinks fit. In any case, these
facts may prove serviceable to many
sufferers, so you may publish them in any
way you like.”

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Incurable.
The long, hot, dull day is nigh over,
And cut like the blade of a sword ;
A red bar of sunshine is hanging
High on the white wall of the ward.
The woman from Mitchell’s is dozing,
The nurses have gone t« their tea,
And Granny’s asleep in her corner—
The ward is for Alice and me.
We lean by the window together,
And watch the gaunt, grey shadows push
Long arms reaching east to the dead-house,
And south to the roll of the bush.
“ Years ago —three last September—•
They carried me here in the spring ;
They said I should go ere the summer”—
She touches the band of my ring ;
‘ ‘ I shall never wear one on my finger—
Not that it will matter for much ! ”
But she turns her bright head to the sundown,
And leans on the arm of her crutch.
So I talk of old days on the ranges,
And show her the snow-saddled peak
I climbed in a long-faded summer,
Till brightness comes back to her cheek ;
And she laughs with an easy forgetting—
A light nature deepened by pain !
And slowly the last of the sunshine
Dies out on the bush-broken plain.
Will they carry her east to the dead-house
In the grey of some rain-ridden dawn ?
And for some is it life to be buried ?
And for some is it death to be born ?
The sunset is red on her tresses,
That glitter like bubbles on wine,
As the wind blows them in from the window
Against the dull sable of mine.
And even out there in the dead-house—
As light draweth light to its fold —
I know a stray sunbeam would find them,
And nestle there —gold to the gold !
LOLA.
An Affair of Outposts.
The cocks were crowing for the light,
The dogs howled at the rising moon :
But well he knew the dreary night
Was scarce outside its laggard noon.
And he was told to hold a post
Against an army weird and vague
A i that proud-b -mered phantom host,
Which ’leaguered once the walls of Prague.
The drink was dead in all his blood :
Brain burned, and eyes rheum’d tearfully ;
Whilst tremors not to be withstood
Shook soul and body fearfully.
Gnarled scrub and twisted roots arouncl
Took life and movement in his eyes,
His ears were full of horrid sound —
The stars were corpse-lights in the skies.
A hell-dog like a ball of soot
Coursed through the scrub, and glared and went;
A shapeless horror at his foot,
Grew o’er him chill and passion-spent.
If sleep, e’en for a moment’s space,
Sought horror’s features to conceal,
A foul fiend smote him in the face
And bade him see, and know, and feel.
’Twas but an outpost lightly touched
(The legions otherwheres had drawn).
He damned his soul for getting bushed
In cold reality of dawn.
The shanty half-a-mile away
Silent and shapeless made no sign,
Out north and west the plain showed grey
To the black river timber-line.
God ! for a drink ! What use ? Dead broke—
Kicked out when utterly put through.
Spite snakes and shakes, we’ll raise a smoke,
Up bluey, boys, and battle through !
F.M.
The Western Wife.
She lives “in hopes,” though Fortune strange
Ignores her prayers and tears;
She’s waiting for her luck to change—
She’s waited now for years.
Where Famine and Despair .abide,
Where Drought’s drear curses rest,
She lives—and laughs—but just to hide
The anguish in her breast.
The dust-storm beating at her door,
The white and with’ring sun
She has to bear, and something more—
The bag of flour is done.
The teething child she soothes at night,
Makes gray eyes wet and dim;
Says she (and she, perhaps, is right):
“ Oh, what a world for him ! ”
She lives “in hopes,” and through her grief
Looks for a fairer day—
But that the heavens may give relief
Her faith alone can say.
V.
WILL JARRATT.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 29, 1902.
Price 6d
THE LIVERPOOL (N.S.W.) “TUBERCLE FARM.”—A MIDNIGHT DEPUTATION.
LEADER OF THE DEPUTATION : “As the most recent death at the institution, I have been asked, JOHN SEE, to introduce to you this deputation of the 1901 and 1902 victims of the N.S. W. Govern-
ment’s Liverpool Tubercle Farm. There are 76 of us killed in 1901, 55 so far in 1902, and we expect a couple more every hour. So we claim this to be a large and influential deputation.
What we wish to point out is that a consumptives hospital that would cure, not kill, would be really a permanent and reproductive work, and might, with much more advantage certainly
than your tombstone to soldiers, be charged to loan votes. The men who get consumption, and are cut off in the prime of life, are often men cf brains, often skilled mechanics Fat politicians
don’t get consumption; but I assure you that some men very useful to the State do. Save them, cure them instead of killing them, and you will have a valuable asset in the shape of an
increased body of taxpayers. It won t cost much—less than a ducal celebration. You will excuse the absence of the usual compliments: Dead men know not how to flatter John See—
What do you mean to do?"

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 29, 1902.
The Removal-of-the-Food-Duties
Campaign.
The savage animosity of the foreign import-
ing class which lives on the restriction of
Australian industry (certain members of this
class are just now being publicly sued by a
person who claims that he was instrumental
in destroying an Australian industry, and
that the full price agreed upon for doing
this injury to his country wasn’t forthcoming)
has broken out afresh over the ques
tion of the grain and fodder duties. The
financial arrangements between the Federal
and State Governments are of a com-
plicated character the Braddon clause
makes that inevitable, and the jealousies
of the States, and especially their re-
fusal to hand over the debts to the
Federal authority, made the Braddon
clause inevitable. Nine-tenths of the public
utterly fail to realise the nature of these
financial arrangements, therefore there is
extra-special scope for the importing crowd
—which wants, at any cost, to get rid of the
Government that seeks to encourage Aus-
tralian industries —and the Freetrade papers
—which are specially mad at having lost
their own 50 per cent. Protection—-to lie on
the subject. And to do both these parties
justice, a capacity for plausible, venomous
and blushless lying where their own interests
were concerned is a thing they have never
lacked.
* * • *
The case which they persistently lay
before the public is that the drought is bad,
the stock perishing for want of fodder, the
crops insufficient to meet the country’s
demands, the price of food high—and that,
amid all these woes, the Barton Govern-
ment refuses to abolish the food duties.
Also it is claimed that the abolition of the
food duties would be of immense benefit to
the people. To quote Importer M'Mildan :
We expected this year that we should have to import
about 9,000,000 bushels of wheat. This would probably
be quoted at about ss. per bushel. If there had been no
duty— at present the Customs impost was equal to about
Is. per bushel—the wheat would be cheaper by the
amount of the duty. This duty would create the price
for the whole of Australia. A price of 4s. per bushel
would give the fanner a good profit in this time of dis-
tress. The duty would probably £400,000 to the
revenue of Australia, but the extra taxation to the people
of Australia would be equal to about £2,400,000.
It is thus claimed that if there were no duty
the price of wheat would go down to 4s. per
bushel—in other words, that the farmer
would get Is. per bushel less, and his dis-
tresses would be proportionately increased.
Wheat was sold in N.S.W. during 1896, when
there was no duty, and when the harvest
was better than it is expected to be this
year, at an average of 4s. 3d. ; and in 1897,
when there was no duty, and the har-
vest was better still, it averaged 4s. s|d.
Therefore Mr. M‘Millan’s bare assurance
that, but for the duty, it would be only
4s. this year wants a great deal of cor-
roboration. And bread is cheaper in
Sydney to-day than it was when M‘Millan
was N.S.W. Treasurer, and there was no
drought, and the blessings of Freetrade were
in full blast in the land. Also, flour is as
cheap as it was under McMillan’s Treasurer-
ship and M‘Millan’s Freetrade policy ; and
it is cheaper than it was under Reid’s
beneficent Freetrade policy in 1897. There-
fore it is by no means so certain as it looks
that there is any very extra or unusual food
trouble (barring that which exists in the
diseased imagination of the importer) to
alleviate, and certainly M'Millan, as a man
with a ghastly capacity for putting up the
price of food, is the last person to look to
for alleviation. But this is a digression.
Let the unusual food trouble be taken for
granted.
9 * * •
Assuming that, the price of bread having
got up almost as high as it did under Free-
trade, it is time to take violent steps, who
should take them ? Assuming that the Aus-
tralian Government will collect £400,000
extra this year in food duties, which may, or
may not, be true, all this money will go back
to the States. The Australian Government
doesn’t take its authorised 25 per cent., even
of the present customs revenue, and any
extra revenue will be returned to the
States bodily. If the Australian Govern-
ment and the Australian Parliament
were to abolish the food duties the
States would lose that £400,000, and their
already hard-up condition would become
still worse. They would have to cut off a
lot of the relief they are now extending to
the distressed farmer—this, too, at a time
when, M'Millan asserts, the fall in the price
of what little grain he had would add to his
distresses ; the States would also have to cut
down more wages, and sack more em-
ployees ; and for these things the Australian
Parliament would be cursed with bell, book,
and candle. The M‘Millan gang already de-
nounces it for two things : (1) For not re-
turning enough revenue to the States to
keep them solvent ; (2) for being callously
blind to the sufferings of the people in not
abolishing the food duties. If the Australian
Government keeps on the food duties it will
still be denounced for its callousness in not
taking them off; and if it takes them off it
will be still more violently denounced for
not returning sufficient revenue to the States
to keep them solvent. The M'Millan party
gets there both ways.
* * * *
The Australian Government wisely decides
to leave things to the States. There is no
need for Federal interference, and the whole
yarn on which all the denunciation of the
Barton Government is based is the meanest
and most unmanly lie that this country has
possessed since the days of the Kiama Ghost.
As a matter of fact, any and every State can
help itself in the matter. Any and every
State can abolish its own rood duties if
IT RECKONS SUCH A COURSE NECESSARY OR
ADVISABLE. Supposing N.S.W. really wants
these duties abolished. Its share of the
income from the food duties may be £lOO,OOO
or any other sum. The State Parliament has
only to intimate that it will pay, by its own
cheque on its own Treasury, the duties on
all food imported into N.S.W., and, so far
as N.S.W. is concerned, the thing is done.
The State Treasurer hands to the Federal
Treasurer his cheque for the duties on all
grain imported. He gets the money back at
the division of the surplus, which takes place
at the end of each month. Consequently,
he pays nothing, and, in the matter of food
for the supply of N.S.W., Sydney becomes
an absolutely free port. Of course, the
State Treasurer does not get the revenue he
would have received if Sydney had not been
made a free port in this respect, but, if the
Australian Parliament instead of the N.S.W.
Parliament suspended the duties in N.S.W,,
the result would be the same. The State
Parliamentsof N.S.W., Victoria, Queensland,
Tasmania and Westralia are all sitting, and
any one of them, if it feels that its own State
requires the abolition of the food duties, and
if it is willing to sacrifice the revenue
obtained from them, can suspend them, so
far as its own State is concerned, in a night.
The Federal Parliament isn’t sitting, so it
can’t suspend them. No one knows these
facts better than the M‘Millan gang, and
its persistent suppression, not to say falsifi-
cation, of them suggests axe-grinding rather
than patriotism.
* * *
If all the State Parliaments passed a reso-
lution that they regarded the suspension of
the grain and fodder duties as so essential
that they were willing to sacrifice the re-
venue derived from them, the Australian
Parliament would probably grant a general
suspension at once. But either the State
Parliaments don’t want the suspension, or
they don’t regard the alleged benefit to be
derived as anything like commensurate with
the loss of revenue, or they want to get the
alleged benefit, while leaving the Aus-
tralian Parliament to bear the obloquy of the
loss of revenue —which means that they
want to get the alleged benefit while reserv-
ing the right to say that the consequent
deficit isn’t their fault, but is all owing to
the wickedness of the incompetent Barton
Ministry. In either case, the Barton
Ministry rightly refuses to act. It has no
warrant to act except the advice of certain
venomously hostile papers which want to see
it ruined, and the counsel of certain interested
politicians who will inherit its billet if it
takes their advice and thereby destroys itself.
There is no word in any of the speeches of
Reid, M'Millan, Bruce Smith and co. to
the effect that they won’t take a mean
advantage of the Barton Ministry by
assuming its billet if it loses office by
following their advice. Various politicians
have moved in various State Parliaments
that the grain and fodder duties should be
abolished, but not one of them has added to
his resolution that “ this Parliament, so far
as its own State is concerned, will take full
responsibility for, and make ail necessary
provision for, any loss of revenue which may
ensue.” So long as any State that is willing
to sacrifice the revenue derived from the
food duties can absolutely suspend these
duties, and the suspension by the State
Parliament would cost no more than would
suspension by the Federal authority, the
interested parties who want the Barton
Government’s billet have no case. The
demand of these parties that the Barton
Government shall commit suicide by imposing
on the six States a policy which they can
adopt themselves if they regard it as worth
while, but which not one of them has adopted
all the same, is about the finest specimen of
the political confidence trick that this coun-
try has evolved up to date.
Belief for the Distressed Farmers.
Premier See lately decided to hold a conference
of prominent members of N.S.W. Assembly, in-
cluding the leaders of the Opposition and the
Labor party, to consider what should be done for
the settlers in the drought-stricken regions of the
State. Such a conference should represent the
Legislature’s united wisdom or its united inca-
pacity, and if any part of N.S.W. Assembly has
any idea as to what should be done, that idea
should get a fair chance to come to light. It is a
foregone conclusion that a good part of any such
conference will suggest floating a big loan and
supplying the ruined settlers with employment on
public works, but that sort of thing is inevitable.
* * • •
The drought is part of N.S.W.’s trouble; the
gradual crumbling away of a monstrous system of
credit is another and almost equally disastrous
part. Even last year, bad as things were, they
might have been much worse ; how they will
exactly pan out this year it is impossible to say
until the Government Statistician holds his annual
stock-taking. During the year which ended
March, 1901, N.S.W. had the largest wheat crop
it ever produced ; during the year ended March,
1902, it had the second largest. The last five
wheat harvests were the five largest on record.
The four years when the mineral production of
N.S. W. was the largest on record were 1891, 1899,
1900 and 1901, and in the way of mineral pro-
duction 1900 was the best year of all. The years
when N.S.W.’s exported wool crop fetched the
biggest amount of money were, putting them in
order of value :
1891 .. £11,036,018 1893 .. £9,676,061
1889 .. 10,620,636 1883 .. 9,598,761
1892 .. 10,211,456 1888 .. 9,089,776
1899 .. 10,020,495 1901 .. 9,035,437
Last year, therefore, was only £2,000,000 below
the best one in the State’s record, and was better
than 1894, 1895, 1896, 1897 or 1898. It was also
the year when the shipping business reached its
maximum, when the revenue was at its highest,
and when borrowing was also at its highest and
the public works policy going strongest. During
the last three years N.S.W. both exported and
imported more than during any other three years in
its history. A great deal of this apparent pros-
perity must be bogus, if a few months of really
hard drought has brought N.S.W. to such _ a
condition that it is necessary to hold a special
conference to decide how to save it from falling
through. No doubt, things are vastly worse than
they were last year, but a State that stood on a
sound basis shouldn’t totter so quickly, or so
badly.
* * » *
So far as concerns the agricultural settler his
trouble seems based on the fact that the agricul-
tural boom of recent years has been built, to a great
extent, on the foundations of a huge mistake. The
western slope isn’t a region of reliable rainfall, and
it never has been. A dozen years ago the fact was
little noticed, for there was very little agriculture
thereabouts; this year there are many agricul-
turists, and the conditions that didn’t matter then
are sheol and ruin now. In 1893 the area
under crop in N.S.W. was 1,010,727 acres. In
1901 the area under alleged crop was 2,445,564
acres. And this was where the increase mostly
lay;

When drought comes, sheep die or are killed, and
the loss ends there; the State is under no obliga-
tion to feed the sheep indefinitely, and that
animal has no Parliamentary representation, and
doesn’t write to the papers. The selector and his
family can’t be killed in drought time. So land-
settlement in drought regions means not only the
immediate loss of crops in bad seasons, but the
absolute necessity of finding some means of keep-
ing a large section of the population alive at any
cost.
• • * •
The area that was settled, in the agricultural
sense, previous to ’93 is not very much worse
this year than it was then. The area that was
settled since—the great burst of agricultural settle-
ment that N.S.W. was so proud of—is this year
mostly wasted effort, and a good part of it has been
wasted effort all the time. There are over 18,000,000
acres in the well-watered coastal district of
N.S.W., of which oidy 306,329 acres are under
crop. There are nearly 40,000,000 acres on the
comparatively well-watered tableland of which
590,026 acres are under crop. But in some parts
of this region, owing to rivers, heavy timber
mountains, and other obstacles, it was diwieult
and expensive to open up the country with roads
and railways. In other parts there were huge
landowners, whose properties would have to be
resumed to make settlement possible. It cost less
trouble to rush settlers out on to the easy, almost
level, almost treeless country, where railways were
cheap and roads almost made themselves, for
several years the great ambition of the Govern-
ment was to increase the cultivated and settled
area on paper by dumping anybody down any-
where —and anybody was dumped down any-
where. The Freetrade party had a stupendous
contempt for manufactures. They interfered with
the softgoods importing fetich. Its one idea, was
to put people on the land—any people, and tor
preference the land where they could be dropped
with least trouble and expense. The 1* reetrade
party was so gloriously successful that while the
area under cultivation increased nearly 250 per
cent, in eight years, the value of the products of
cultivation increased by less than 50 per cent.
Now that the first pinch has come the question is
how to get many of these extra people off the land
again. The land they were settled on yielded a
meagre return in decent years, and this year it
bids fair to yield none at all, and to leave some
8000 beggared families on the hands of the State.
Every acre of good land in the comparatively
well-watered coastal districts should be turned
to the best account before the land-settlement
craze again attacks the Western slopes. The
big estates should be resumed and cut
up, and it should be made absolutely
illegal for any person or company or syndicate to
own more than a certain area —this to prevent
any future aggregation of big estates. The coun-
try should be opened up fully with roads and
railways—built out of revenue, not out of loan.
N.S.W. has sufficient revenue for the purpose with
decent administration. Everything should be
done that can be done to facilitate the removal to
the betterwatered regionsof those amongthe ruined
settlers west of the mountains who want to remove.
There is great talk of irrigation in what is now the
worst of the drought country, and the idea is a
good one. When there are two or three or six
million people in the coastal districts, then N.S.W.
will have a solid body of taxpayers to put up the
money for irrigation works. At present the
great north coast district of N.S.W. has no rail-
way north of Maitland, barring a few miles of an
isolated section close to the Queensland border
vet this is the best-watered region in N.S.W. It
is also ill-supplied with roads. The N.S.W. Par-
liamentary conference might think hard over these
matters. Also it should think still harder over
the fact that no agricultural population will ever
amount to much that hasn’t a manufacturing
population to supply it with a market. A manu-
facturing population is nearly independent of
drought, it doesn’t want seed-wheat, free water
trains, free carriage for starving stock, and it asks
for little in the way of relief works. Also a manu-
facturing population of 25,000 requires just about
as much expenditure for roads, _ bridges and rail-
ways as an agricultural population of 1000
The Beer Party and the Bethel Party.
The Liquor Bill, introduced to N.S.W. Assembly
last week, is of importance as indicating the trend
of political thought on the drink question rather
than as a serious effort at legislation. It may be
predicted, with almost absolute certainty, that
the measure will not get to the statute book.
Introduced at the fag end of a session, the busi-
ness paper already cumbered with many vexed
questions, it can hardly be hoped, or feared, that
it will pass both Houses before Christmas. Talk
will be plentiful at all stages ; there is nothing so
efficacious in loosening the tongue as liquor legis-
lation, in which so many vested interests are at
stake. The brewers and the publicans are very
strongly represented, both directly and indirectly,
in Parliament; and almost equally strong are the
representatives of the anti-liquor interest—the men
whose livelihood depends more or less completely
on publicly cursing the demon drink and striving
to exorcise it from Society. It is a curious, and for
the general public an unfortunate, fact that the
question of alcoholic drink has become a religious
one in Anglo-Saxon countries. Possibly the
development is a by-product of Puritanism. It is
certainly not noticeable to any extent in Latin
countries, where wine is unhesitatingly accepted
as an orthodox gift of Providence, is drunk at all
ages and at all meals by all classes, and is respon-
sible for very little drunkenness. But in gloomier
lands, with gloomier ideas, were evolved darker
and more potent drinks, full of invitations to
excess and causing insane and melancholy
drunkenness ; they, in time, by a natural
revulsion of feeling caused the “ Temperance
movement.” Australia, with her bright, warm,
cheery climate, should have been heir to the light
drinks and the almost total absence of drunken-
ness of Southern Europe ; but Fate willed other-
wise, and she acquired the fire-water, the capacity
for gloomy excess, and the Prohibition religion of
Britain.
» • * •
So the position is that Australia, which should
be drinking light wines and light beers with un-
questioned cheerfulness, and dreaming neither of
drunkenness nor of total abstinence —the Scylla
and Charybdis of diet—is most unhappily burdened
with the drink problem of England. A big sec-
tion of her population wallows in beer, the strength
of which is as the strength of ten because its
make is impure, and soaks in spirits and fortified
wines of much evil potency; and this section
flaunting its blue devils in public shocks another
big section into the scare policy of total abstin-
ence and prohibition. The while, there is a great
middle class, which wishes neither to be extra-
spirituous nor extra-spiritual, whose taste is
neither for Gin delirium nor the New Jerusaleni
of the W.C.T.U., and which desires to be left in
undisturbed enjoyment of a mild stimulant and
narcotic which, in these days of high-pressure
civilisation, is almost necessary to a pleasant life.
But these people are never considered when the
Legislature comes to deal with the liquor question.
The extreme anti-alcoholic fanatics are con-
sulted ; the men who at heart desire to pro-
mote to as great a degree as possible the
consumption of poor-quality alcohol have their
representatives in the lobby. But the great
general public, desirous only of honestly getting
a modicum of good quality alcoholic liquor— its
voice is rarely heard.
* * * *
The most recent N.S.W. Liquor Bill does,
strangely enough, give some little consideration
to the great bidk of the community in one or two
clauses ; but, poor as is the chance of any of the
Bill getting on to the statute book, the hope of
those parts surviving is even poorer ; they will be
eagerly torn out by the fanatics on both sides;
to destroy them the Bung and Bethel parties will
combine. Yet in the light of common sense they
appear as the only really valuable parts of the
measure —the only provisions designed to meet
the convenience of the truly ‘ ‘ temperate ” people.


Briefly, those parts of the N.S.W. Liquor Bill
which offer to help the public convenience are the
more stringent provisions against the adulteration
of liquor ; the withdrawal of the prohibition
against music in public houses (this will be
eagerly fought by the prohibition fanatics, who
insist that, if you drink at all, you must drink in
melancholy gloom and under such circumstances
as promote sodden drunkenness); the liberalising
of the conditions under which makers of wine and
cider may sell their products ; the grading of
license fees, so that the backblocks and suburban
publican won’t be in a measure forced to
adulterate his liquor by being levied on for the
same license fee as the big metropolitan hotel-
keeper ; and the suggestion that occupiers of
oyster-saloons and fish-shops should be allowed to
obtain licenses to sell beer or porter in connection
with their restaurant business. This proposal,
the most sensible in the whole Bill, is already
threatened with fierce opposition by the Beer
party and the teetotallers. Yet, in the public in-
terest, it should be carried through, and indeed
extended so that all restaurant-keepers snould be
allowed to sell wine and lager-beer, to be con-
sumed on their premises with meals. It would be
a good thing for most people if no alcoholic liquor
were consumed, except with meals; and it would
probably be a good thing if some light alcoholic
liquor were taken by all adults at meals, in place
of that vilest of national beverages, over-drawn
tea.
• * • *
The points scored by Bung in the Bill are: a
proposal to charge a license fee to clubs and co-
operative societies selling liquor ; a clause to pre-
vent the sale of liquor by ship-masters in port;
and some concessions in regard to licenses. The
Prohibition party is offered a series of concessions
of which some are merely make-believe: no woman
under 21 to be employed as a barmaid (very rarely
would that clause be needed); persons under 18
not to be served with liquor; and more stringent
provisions against Sunday trading and trading
after hours.
* * • *
Summed up, the Bill is mainly a series of tem-
porising concessions to both sides of the bitter
liquor controversy, with just a little consideration
for the general public. A bold measure dealing
with the question would recognise that, in
moderation, good liquor is a good thing, and would
not hesitate to tolerate its general use ; would
takeintoeonsideration the fact that the present sys-
tem is radically bad, with its tied houses, its adul-
terated drinks, and its many sordid concomitants ;
and would probably find a way out of the maze of
difficulty by making the sale and, as far as possible,
the manufacture of alcoholic drinks a State
monopoly. But there is no hope of bold measures
from the See Government; indeed, the present
Bill —timid and temporising as it is—is palpably
only an “advertising” measure, not a serious
effort at legislation.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Importers’ Agent.
The remarkable case of 0. Aloysio's Fox, against
certain large and wealthy importing firms in Mel-
bourne, hangs fire. Not that that fact matters
much, for the amount at dispute, and the legal
merits of the dispute, and the possible decision,
have no public concern whatever, and the case
may hang fire longer than did the coffin of the late
Mahomet without the matter interesting any save
the parties involved. The matter which does
interest the public is that a man is suing certain
importers in an Australian court for money
allegedly due to him for bringing about injury to
an Australian industry, and helping to destroy
employment, and wreck Australian capital, and
for doing something to increase Australia’s
distresses. According to plaintiff’s statement, lie
was hired by the defendant importers to get the
excise duty on Australian-made spirits fixed at
13s. per gallon, instead of the lower rate previ-
ously suggested. It is claimed that, by means of
publishing pamphlets, by lobbying, and by
judiciously talking in the great aggregate ear
of the Legislature, he was instrumental in
doing this. A letter is put in as evidence in
which he demanded his pay—there was some dis-
pute about the pay—alleging that he had succeeded
in utterly crippling the Australian distilling
business, and pointing out that the laborer who
did such good work was worthy of his
hire. The rest of the case doesn’t in-
terest the public. Any mere money questions
between the man who would do sucn work,
and the men who would hire him to do such
work are their own affair. The real matter
at issue is that the importer is set forth un-
disguisedly as a public enemy and a wilful
wrecker of Australian industry. There is nothing
said, when he and his alleged employee come into
court, about any desire to reduce prices, to lift a
burden off the people, to deliver them from the
yoke of the manufacturer, or any of the other
usual high-faluting moral principles. Plaintiff, in
his letter, didn’t demand his full pay on the
ground that he had assisted to deliver the
people from the incubus of Protection. No
doubt the Federal members were approached
on different lines, but, in private, plaintiff appears
to have posed simply as a man who was hired by
certain foreign importers to do all possible damage
to a certain local business, for the importers’
sordid personal profit, and who had been remark-
ably successful in doing so, and, consequently, in
depriving the Australian workman of his job and
his wages, or of his chance of getting these two
desirable things. What the Federal members,
who were doubtless approached in quite a different
tone, and who listened to the voice of the charmer,
think now of their share in the business is another
question.

Selling' the Railways.
Queensland Treasurer Cribb has discovered how
his country, which is now financially lost, may be
saved. All the trouble may be put right by
selling the railways. “In our railways,” says
Cribb, “we have a valuable asset which could, if
found desirable, be disposed of at its capital cost,
thus relieving us of an annual drain of about
£600,000.” And the saving of that £600,000 would
put the finances straight, arid then all would be
well. There is something touchingly credulous
about Cribb’s faith in private railway enterprise,
yet he is only a sample of a considerable class,
and that class is a branch of the great fool com-
munity, which sends its pound to the turf-tipster,
and gives a seedy, down-at-heelsman Is. for a copy
of a book entitled “How to Become a Millionaire,”
and practises faith-healing, and discovers that the
Pope is the eause of the high price of bread, and
pays 10s. to be told by a medium that the spirit
of its deceased aunt is feeling quite well and
happy, Yet quite a large section of the people of
Queensland evidently believe that there are capi-
talists who would pay cost price—over £20,000,000
—for a neglected railway system that doesn’t yield
2 per cent, on that sum, for nobody laughed to
any great extent over Cribb’s puerile statement.
If Queensland actually put its railways on the
market, and stipulated that the purchasing syndi-
cate was to maintain freights and fares at the
present level, it is probable that the best offer it
would get would be about £5,000,000. If any
syndicate gave £20,000,000, it would want prac-
tically unlimited power to increase the railway
charges ; and it is doubtful if £20,000,000 would
be offered even then. Syndicates that build
railways at the rate of a mile of line for every 175
people generally demand a land grant equal to the
whole cost of the line ; in other words, they want
the railways for nothing. However the deal was
effected, the syndicate would want to see a reliable
5 per cent, in view—probably it would want to
see 6 per cent. And the Queensland people would
have to find the money just as they do now—the
difference being that they have now to find only
per cent.; therefore, the change would mean
that Queensland would have to pay £250,000 or
£450,000 more than it does at present. The new
system might make things easier for Treasurer
Cribb, for he would no longer have to collect the
money, but Queensland would have to pay it, and
Queensland is more important than Cribb. That
painfully futile person says he doesn’t absolutely
commit himself to recommending the sale of the
railways, but mentions the idea “as a means
whereby it would be possible to avoid burdensome
taxation.” Whether Cribb is grinding any
definite axe, or only getting the grindstone ready
in case he has an axe by-and-bye, is uncertain,
but these points arc certain: A pound paid in
extra railway rates is burdensome taxation, just
as much as 10s. paid in extra taxes. No syndicate
will give £20,000,000 or half of £20,000,000 for
the Queensland system on the present table of
rates and charges ; the syndicate which wants to
invest millions at 2 per cent, when it can get 3 per
cent, in British consols isn’t born yet. And when
the syndicate, if it ever arrives, has cut off the free
Parliamentary railway passes, the free water trains,
and the reduced rates for fodder and starving
stock, and has put up charges 50 or 100 per cent.,
and when it demands a land grant of 75,000 acres
a mile whenever a new line is suggested to it, even
Treasurer Cribb may find that he hasn’t got rid
of so very much of his deficit after all. The
“ burdensome taxation,” which it is proposed to
avoid by selling the railways, is, of course, the
land-tax. If Treasurer Cribb could name any
country where private railway ownership has
averted a land-tax he would throw some light on
a very dark argument.

The Vampires on the Raft.
Horrible allegations begin to trickle out as to the
happenings on the Elingamite raft between the
time when, with 16 souls aboard, she left the sink-
ing steamerfoodless, waterless and gearless, and her
discovery by the Penguin with eight survivors—
talk of human vampires sucking the blood of a
helpless woman (who afterwards died), whispers
of vigils, such as the companions of Cannibal
Gabbett kept on a memorable journey of the old
convict days. The Elingamite stories are too re-
volting to be more than hinted at ; but what has
been printed in one or two papers is enough
to conjure up before any of the public
contemplating a sea trip the vision of a vam-
pire on a raft. It is for the Federal Parlia-
ment to take prompt steps to banish that horror.
The shipowners as a body will, it is certain, do little
or nothing, for experience shows that too many of
them know nothing of precaution, save what the
law absolutely enforces, and even that many of
them will neglect, if they can neglect it safely. It
should not be necessary for the law to force ship-
owners to provide effective boat-accommodation
in case of disaster, and to equip and provision the
boats, any more than it is necessary to pass a law
that coach-proprietors should equip their vehicles
with brakes on mountain roads. But it is ; bitter
experience proves that. Why it should be so, why
so many shipowners should preserve in their
calling so much of the moral callousness of
the old days when the shipping interest was
mostly piratical, it is impossible to even theorise.
The Legislature need not seek out the cause : all
it has to do is to consider the patent, obvious fact,
and to deal with it. The shipowning class con-
tains so large a percentage of callous adventurers,
that it must be legislated for as a class which
knows nothing of the precautions necessary for
making its business reasonably safe, and which
cares nothing of the results to others of the
neglect of precaution. The Australian Navigation
Act should set forth ample precautions, leave
nothing at all to the good-sense of the shipowner,
and provide a large, commodious dock with the
full complement of spikes for those who depart
from the rules prescribed.

The Case of Major Lenehan.
After many months, an intimation appears in
the daily press that the Australian military depart-
ment ‘ ‘ is considering whether Major Lenehan is
to be allowed to remain in the Commonwealth
Forces.” By this it may be inferred that Major-
General Hutton finds Minister Forrest much
more amenable to his influence than was Minister
Lyne. When acting as head of the Defence
Department, Mr. Lyne refused to concur in the
dismissal of Lenehan without a full inquiry into
the merits of the matter. Mr. Forrest seems
now to propose to do so, in spite of the
existence of a growing public feeling that
Major Lenehan’s case calls for redress, not
punishment. But Lenehan cannot be dismissed
without inquiry, except by an act of arbitrary
tyranny perpetrated by an Australian Government
in obedience to the representations of an Imperial
officer. It is equally true that he cannot, consis-
tently with military discipline, remain in the
Defence forces in defiance of the opinion of the
officer commanding. The position is one that can
only be relieved by an inquiry. If that establishes
the fact that Lenehan is a wronged man, he
deserves redress ; if it shows him a wrong-doing
man, the Imperial demand for his dismissal is
justified. The circumstances in regard to
his ease are now fairly familiar. He was
officer commanding the Bush veldt Carbineers, but
had no part in the atrocities committed by
some officers of that corps. He was, however,
arrested with the others, and, it is claimed, was
illegally imprisoned before and after trial, with
no other motive, seemingly, than to prevent him
from communicating with the Australian Govern-
ment in regard to the invidious punishment which
was about to be meted out to Australian scape-
goats at the demand of Germany, There is very
strong presumptive evidence that this was so ;
and that subsequently, in order to smother Aus-
tralian indignation, the acts of the Bush veldt Car-
bineers were blackened and represented as even
more infamous than they were. It would seem,
from the as yet imperfect evidence available—
(l) That the Australian officers of the Bushveldt Carbi-
neers committed murders, but committed them under
English orders, and were countenanced in their action by
scores of English officers, who committed similar murders
and no word said.
(2) That the execution of two Australians and the im-
prisonment of another were due, not (altogether) to
English disapproval cf Boer murder, but to the English
necessity of finding scapegoats to satisfy German
demands.
(3) That Major Lenehan, an innocent man, was illegally
imprisoned to prevent him from communicating with the
Australian Government until the executions had taken
place.
In the language of the law courts, a prima facie
case has been made out on these points, and it is
claimed that an inquiry will establish them fully
as facts. Naturally, the Imperial Government
does not wish for an inquiry. But, just as
naturally, the Australian Government should
be firm in instituting an inquiry. Major-General
Hutton, who, of course, represents. the
Imperial and not the Australian interest, has
worked strenuously to burke the question and to
crush Major Lenehan’s requests for an investiga-
tion ; he has definitely refused a court-martial,
and now is striving to defeat the agitation for the
trial of the case by a civil tribunal. That’s his
business. The Australian Cabinet’s business is to
see that an Australian citizen gets justice, no
matter who is offended thereby.
Treasurer Bird’s New Chicken.
Though Tas. Treasurer Bird’s new taxation pro-
posals lost some of their gaudiest feathers during
the prorogation, they still carry a good many
frills. Mr. Bird estimates that he will begin 1903
with a deficiency of £219,000. This amount, he
explains, would have been reduced oy £30,000 or
so but for the House of Dodery naving cast out
the taxation proposals of .ast session. The esti-
mated expenditure for 1903 is £804,000, and
revenue from all sources is not expected to exceed
£774,000. To make up the balance of £90,000
there is proposed (subject to certain exemptions)
a company tax of Is. m tne £, a tax of 6d. on
incomes derived from business and of Is. on
incomes derived from property. The tax is to be
paid on all incomes of £lOO and over, the exemp-
tions being fixed on a sliding scale, thus: On
income of £lOO an exemption of £BO is allowed,
with gradually diminishing exemptions until £4OO
is reached, when 6d. in the £ will be collected on
the full amount. An income of £lOO will thus
pay 10s.; the proposals of last session were to take
255. This alteration, in view of the approaching
elections, was very necessary to the life of Bird
and co. A similar system of graduated exemp-
tions is provided in the Land Tax Bill. No
owner of less than £5OOO worth of land is to be
asked for more than |d. in the £—the same old
inadequate ha’penny. Land valued at £lO,OOO
will pay -fd. in the £, and so on gradually, until
estates over £55,000 are reached with a tax of Id.
There is a stamp tax or Id. on receipts for £1
and 2d. on ditto for £5; a probate duty or
from three to five per cent., with a
£5OO exemption on property willed to a
widow, widower, ancestor or descendant,
double those rates on property left to any other
relative, and 10 per cent, on property left to a
stranger in blood. And Tattersall is not for-
gotten :
In respect of every lottery ticket, coupon, or certificate
of a share in any lottery sanctioned by law in Tasmania :
Where the face value of the ticket does not exceed 55.,
Id. ; where the face value of the ticket exceeds 55., for
every ss. or fraction of 55., Id.
From all these sources Treasurer Bird expects to
rake in £90,000 or thereabouts. The Opposition
is being implored by a tearful Bird not to interfere
with the proposed exemptions, but an election is
not far off, so the Propsting party will probably
interfere all it knows. To oppose taxation is as
cheap a way of catching votes as any other.
«
The Reactionary Crowd is going strong in S.
Australia. Having got the land-grant horror
safely through, it turns now to the education
system and makes a dead set against free schools.
It knows that the present Parliament is almost
weak enough to pass anything if enough row is
engineered outside, and it engineers a row
accordingly. It cunningly starts by giving the'
idea that Parliament has already disapproved
of the system, although all that Parliament
has really done so far is to cut down the
£150,000 vote by £l4OO. As a matter of fact,
members did not have the free education system
in their minds at all when they made that small
cut. The cost per average child, after steadily
falling for years, had taken a turn and gone
steadily up from £3 4s. lid. in 1890 to £3 Bs. sd.
in 1901, and it was that at which retrenching
members struck. Anybody who knows much
about S.A. Education Department knows that
its control by a “board of inspectors,” lately
abolished, was ineffective and costly. Better
things had been promised under the new
system, introduced a few months back, and the
House took the Minister at his word and
indicated that it expected the improvement
to actually take place. To pretend that Par-
liament has declared against the whole system,
and to make that the excuse for preparing the
way for a change is smart, but it isn’t con-
spicuously honest. And, anyhow, the cost m
S.A. is not, comparatively, high. In N.S.W.,
where fees are still collected, the lowest cost to
the State per child in average attendance for many
years has been £3 11s. lid.; last year it was over
£4, notwithstanding that the N.S.W. youngster
is scattered over a smaller area than the S.A.
youngster. The thing couldn’t, indeed, be done
for the money in S.A. if it were not for the whole-
sale employment of “provisional teachers,” at a
wage which would pinch even a dairy-farm laborer.

The South Australian land revenue has dropped
from £635,487 in 1882 to £148,420 in 1902. In the
days of the rush of agricultural settlement the
State resumed, for agricultural purposes, 13,200
acres of pastoral land in the dry country, for which
land the squatter had been paying 225. 4d. per
square mile per annum. It paid the dispossessed
squatters £243,276 (of loan money) for their im-
provements, interest on which represents 275. 2d.
per annum for each square mile. The agri-
cultural population that is on the land now pays
about 275. per square mile per annum on an
average, and gets the improvements thrown in, so
the State doesn’t get enough to pay interest on the
money spent on improvements, and gets rather less
than nothing for the land itself. Also, it supplies
the farmers with seed wheat occasionally. In L 894,
the pastoral country—the portion which, luckily,
was not resumed so that the unhappy farmer might
be put on it—brought in, on the average, a rent of.
10s. 3d. per square mile per annum. It now
realises ss. sd. The State, under a specially foolish
law, paid outgoing squatters about £1,000,000 (out
of loans) for their improvements. New squatters
who took up the land are supposed to repay this,
but about £500,000 is supposed to be lost, and the
other £500,000 is hard to collect. The cultivated
area is a trifle less than it was 18 years ago. Dust,
Germans and green parrots are the chief products
of the S. A. country districts. It is a great thing
to go on the land, but over most of Australia most
of the time it is better •to stay in the city and.
make blucher boots.

By dint of telling the sa?me falsehood everyday,,
with monotonous iteration, the importers’ party
and their newspapers _ have really created an
impression that there is some wide discrepancy
between the Barton Government’s tariff and the
policy which Premier Barton outlined at Mait-
land. On that celebrated occasion Barton pro-
mised that the object of the tariff would be “re-
venue without destruction” —meaning thereby
destruction of Australian industries. The tariff
produces revenue right enough, and the one
industry that has been destroyed—the distilling
industry—was killed by a conspiracy among the
Freetraders, who hired a man to beguile Federal
members into raising the excise for the purpose.
(That man is now suing for his alleged money, on
the ground that the importers hired him to
destroy an Australian industry, and didn’t pay up
the price agreed upon.) Under these circum-
stances, the Barton tariff and the Barton mani-
festo square well enough, though the tale about
the imaginary discrepancy will doubtless appear
in Sydney Daily Telegraph till Premier Barton
is in his grave, and will be mentioned in his
obituary.
“ Shellback ” writes
Melb. Argus, discussing the Elingamite wreck, says :
“ Men who are familiar with the whole routine on hoard
ship say that it is remarkable, yet still true, that officers
and men are, for the most part, rather indifferent as to
the equipment, upon which their own safety, in common
with that of the passengers, will, in case of accident,
depend.” Wrong; and a cowardly libel on a hard-
working and much abused class. A mate myself I know
what lam talking about. Kindly bear in mind, you lion-
seagoing public, the fact that the effective crew of the
average coaster is limited to eight sailors (for colliers only
six). Now the ordinary lifeboat as usually provided
requires from 6 to 8 men at each fall to even lift it out of
the chocks preparatory to swinging out for lowering.
Cooks and stewards, firemen, greasers, etc., are all told
off to make up a boat’s crew. But what they know of
sailoring is practically nil. This, mind you, is no dis-
paragement of them as men. No wonder, therefore, that
it takes an undue time to launch the boats in case of
disaster! The average chief-officer, as I know him,
would be only too glad to devote one day a week to the
overhauling and working of the boats. But from the
scantiness of labor provided he is either compelled to
forego it or else do it in odd half-hours snatched from
cargo work. In port, which is the best place to put this
job through, his eight men are usually disposed thusly :
Night-watchman, 1 (off all day); winches, 3 or 4 ; gang-
way, 1; plus, perhaps, two hands told off to look after
holds, sweeping up, etc. In colliers it is even worse,
especially in loading ports, where the men have to prac-
tically “stand-by” the mooring lines for hours at a
stretch. Again, should a more than usually energetic
mate manage to put his boats, etc., in thorough order,
he probably does so at the expense of the appearance of
some portion of the vessel, and on arrival in home port
gets hauled over the coals by his co.’s marine superin-
tendent for coming in with a dirty ship. It is no excuse
for him to plead that his hands were otherwise engaged
and on more important business ; occasionally he ge: s the
sack or is disrated for it—in any case a black mark is
scored against his name. Maiine inspectors whose busi-
ness it is to find out the weak spots often slur their
work. Less than six months ago I saw a lifeboat
on a coaster passed as seaworthy with a lead patch Ift.
square on her side. To the credit of the co. concerned, it
must be added that, on her arrival at the home port, this
crazy craft was replaced by another in better order ; still,
the ship had to make a 500-mile passage before she got it,
and who was to say that the boat would not be wanted
en route t This brings me back to an assertion made some
weeks ago, in your columns, that the system of appoint-
ing our inspectors in Victoria is radically wrong. In
N.S.W., the marine surveyors owe their appointment to
no shipowning influence, and consequently their word is
law, in addition to having an absolutely free hand. The
sooner a Federal Navigation Act puts, the surveyorship
in all States on a similar basis the sooner will the present
general feeling of mistrust be dispelled.

A bit of well-founded gall from “L.E.” :
The bald announcement that the already-enrolled
women voters exceed the males by 20,000 in the Sydney
metropolitan area is loaded far below the Plimsoll line
with indications of a coming revolution. Various tony-
minded policemen have expressed to the kitchen wenches’
organ their deep regret it was “ the wrong class of
woman ” that was enrolling. The well-to-do, tastefully-
dressed matrons and missies of the Potts Point end of
Darlinghurst “ merely smiled and said they left all that
sort of thing to the men and didn’t know anything about
it,” and refused to give their names ; and the deplorable
fact moved the “ coppermen ” almost to tears. The news
seems too good to be true. Apparently it is the women who
have felt the rough edge of life who are taking an interest in
their country’s affairs, and, judging by their numbers, are
taking it to some purpose. The sweated factory-girl, the
underpaid shop-girl struggling to dress decently and live
somehow on 15s. per week ; the ground-down boarding-
house keeper, who has to board and feed the shop-girl
for 12s. 6d. per week because the poor creature can pay
no more ; and all that great army of women one meets in
city boarding houses who know not where next week’s
board money is to come from, and who keep up a brave
but tremulous under-lip because they are afraid to
let their hearts go while they tremble on the edge of the
precipice. Apparently these are the women who are
enrolling ; and a good thing, too. Next election should
see an overwhelming increase in the Labor vote. The
yell that the vote is being taken advantage of by the
drab and the demi-rep i? rather joyful, news than other-
wise : the drab and the demi-rep are quite right to try
and smash the order of things that has made them what
they are ; and the classes from which they are recruited
will do well to destroy it before it makes them likewise.

Another new departure of the most Tory charac-
ter in the new Victorian ‘ ‘ Reform ” Bill is that
restricting the issue of voters’ certificates.
Premier Irvine argues that a man ought to know
the votes on which he seeks election to Parlia-
ment, and that he cannot do so if voters are
allowed to take out certificates up to a day before
the election. As a good Tory, living on Tory
votes in a shockingly Tory House, Irvine was
bound to forget the little fact that the same thing
applies to the property-owner who has votes in
several districts. Irvine says the voters’ certifi-
cates are responsible for a shifting vote, which he
denounces as dangerous. If the democratic shift-
ing vote is dangerous, so also is the Tory shifting
vote. The Tory who has a vote in half-a-dozen
districts is only allowed to vote in one, but he
always votes in the one where there is a demo-
cratic candidate to be suppressed. No democratic
candidate can know what his prospects are, for if
in an adjoining electorate there is no democratic
candidate to be put down, any number of Tories
may shift into his electorate to oppose him. And
Irvine doesn’t object. The Tory yote can go on
shifting till further notice.

Among the many Imperialistic dreams and ideas
of to-day nobody seems to remember perhaps the
most remarkable one Australia ever produced—
that of Charles Gavan Duffy. Thirty years
ago Duffy’s Royal Commission in Victoria pro-
jected a scheme of Australian Federation which
aimed also at solving the main difficulty of
Imperial Federation, and for a time both Duffy
and Henry Parkes believed they had succeeded.
The key was Australian neutrality, wherever
Australia was not legislatively consulted. Duffy
argued that union with Britain without a neutrality
agreement would drag Australia into wars that
did not concern it, and would make all Britain’s
foes the foes of Australia. By the ratification
of a relationship of neutrality Australia would, on
the other hand, only participate in British war
operations when it formally and legislatively re-
solved to do so. It was intimated at the time that
foreign nations would respect such a neutrality,
Britain and Hanover maintained such a relation
at one time, and Britain and the lonian Isles at
another. But the public mind gradually fell away-
from the idea, and Parkes and others veered round
against it, the impression being that Neutrality
r Teally meant Separation.
