“If I Were King,” staged at Sydney
Royal last Saturday, is a fine, vigorous drama
with much feathers and millinery and clanking
mail in it, and with a certain pretence of being
historical, though if history awoke and saw itself
as Justin Huntly M’Carthy has presented it, it
would be a very much surprised branch of litera-
ture. The story lies, in more senses than one, in
the reign of Louis XI., and that aged monarch
creeps like a malicious black caterpillar through
it all. It is a stage tradition that “ Father Louis”
was always old, silent, dressed in black, and
hunched with long stooping at keyholes and
seeking for dropped coppers and dropped king-
doms, and other property. When the story opens,
the Burgundians are besieging Paris, the Con-
stable of France has turned traitor, the army is
disaffected, and the king is going about in a silent,
malignant, doubled-up fashion, as usual. He is
disguised in a wine-shop with his executioner, try-
ing to find out how popular he is, and deciding to
hang nearly everybody next morning; when Villon,
the poet, drinks at his expense, slaps him cordially
on the back, and calls him old rooster, or words to
that effect. Louis resolves to hang Villon next
morning. Villon tells how he has fallen madly in
love with a lady of rank, and Louis, who is shocked
at this immorality, resolves to hang him something
extra next morning. But Villon, in a rough-and-
tumble fight, badly wounds the traitorous Con-
stable, and Louis relents so far as to make him a
temporary nobleman, and appoint him Constable
of France and commander of his army for seven
days—he to be hanged at the end of that period.
Louis XI. nearly dies laughing as he thinks of
this joke, and even forgets that he is besieged in
his own capital.
• • • •
Later, as he crawls like an apologetic
black worm round his palace, another joke occurs
to Louis XI., and this time he laughs so much
that he has almost to be slapped on the back by
his inseparable companion, the executioner, to pre-
vent him choking. He tells the new Constable that
if the lady whom he loves will marry him in hi 3
own capacity as a beggar-poet, and go back with
him to his garret, his life will be spared. For six
days, under the new Constable, Paris is a well-
governed city, On the seventh day, the Constable
puts on his armour and scatters Charles the Rash
and his Burgundians to the winds. He comes
back in time to be hanged, and the King, more in-
tensely doubled-up than ever, sees his way to two
more jokes. He postpones the hanging for as
long as a candle will burn, and while the grease
thereof dribbles on to Paris he offers to put the
execution off altogether if some one else will be
hanged instead. Then the lady offers to take
Villon’s place on the gallows or to marry him. The
disgusted King starts to confiscate her property ;
the idea of a high-born beauty of his court having
to take in washing to help support her newly
married state being one that strikes him favor-
ably. But, owing to the hostile attitude of the
army and other circumstances, Louis XI. sud-
denly rises to state that he is an old man of the
best intentions, and pardons everybody, and lets
the Constable retain his billet, and the lady her
estates, and bids them be happy ever after.
Probably he hanged them both at a quiet oppor-
tunity later on.
• * * *
Three characters, all very well played,
constitute the bulk of the drama. Cuyler
Hastings is more attractive as Francois Villon
than in his previous capacity of Sherlock Holmes.
He is a manly poet, with a long sword, and
a tall swagger, and a mighty thirst, and the
Bohemian abandon with which he smacks the
King on the back appeals to the public. He
is a manly Constable also, and fills both
parts for all they are worth—which is
less than it might be. Atholwood’s Louis XI.
is an excellent piece of character acting, though
the mean, wise, hunchy little King, with the
rusty black suit, and the leaden, far-seeing eye,
and the long, withered hand that clawed at all
Europe, grows rather stereotyped. He is always
old in drama, like Uncle Tom ; yet, at the time
when the Burgundians besieged Paris, he was
only middle-aged, and his father’s left-off suits,
which he had inherited, were still comparatively
new ; and instead of hiring stray drunks to defend
him, he fought like a demon at Mont l’hery, and
stained his clothes with so much blood that they
never looked decent again. Miss May Chevalier,
who was rather disappointing as Sherlock
Holmes’s best girl, is vastly improved as Villon’s
best girl. Roland Stavely makes Oliver le Daim
another Louis XI., only blacker, and wormier
and more apologetic. Hamilton Stewart’s Tristan
l’Hermit is a rather vigorous and picturesque
ruffian, and Mrs. D’Arey Reid and Miss Mabel
Lane are especially commendable. A quantity
of thieves, vagabonds, executioners, nobles, pub-
licans, and other trash will possibly be sorted out
next week. One or two persons who require a
certain amount of reproof are among them.
** * *
At Sydney Lyceum, “The Derby Winner”
will run his last race on Friday, and on Saturday
Bland Holt revives “White Heather”—a play
that can stand a great deal of reviving. Until
Friday, little Mary, the housemaid, will continue
to stray about the gilded halls to say goodbye to
the earls and countesses, as is the habit of the
peerage’s housemaid when leaving her job with
suspicions on her character. The scenery in
the “D. W.” is much better than the hero.
The mists rise yard by yard from the Downs
at daybreak, and then the right horse wins the
Derby, retrieving the characters of all the good
people in a vague but highly picturesque sort of
way.
»* * *
“ Uncle Tom’s Cabin ” goes more smoothly
than before at Sydney Criterion, but the gloom is
about as great, though brightened with Jean
Drummond’s singing and other irrelevant touches.
By the way, _ St. Clair boasts a most curious col-
lection of piebald lady niggers, most of whom
possess octoroon or quadroon faces, set off with
European hands (pearl powdered at that). Why
they weren’t bought up by an enterprising dime
museum manager nobody explains. A cheerful
Emmeline is sold into dreadful soul bondage to
Legree without much sign of woe, and the chorus
firls state that they are yearning for-the Golden
_ bore just as they yearned last week. Also, Eva
is allowed to die in the usual fashion. They are
always going to send round to the doctor or
the chemist about Eva’s cough, but, while they are
thinking of it, the piebald niggers start singing
about Canaan’s happy shore, or Daniel or Jonah,
etc., and everybody forgets about the chemist.
“ A Trip to Chicago” is promised for next week.
** * *
“The Worst Woman in London” will
definitely take her bad character away from
Sydney Palace this week (Nov. 28). From
present appearances this theatre will take a rest
for a month or so. Times are somewhat dull
theatrically in Sydney just now, owing, perhaps,
to the advent of the hot weather —perhaps to the
shadow cast before of the coming depression; and
the removal of one theatre from the competition
for audiences will not be unwelcome to other
managers.
** * *
To the Sydney Tivoli programme, which
has just now a somewhat greater than ordinary
amount of violent pink leg, the Nawns last week
brought another artistic sketch of Irish life—
“ Major Bourke.” The comedy is mingled with
a good deal of sentiment—the Major is an
old Irish soldier, who, though almost starving,
keeps faithful to a big monetary trust reposed
in him, and refuses to get food, at the price of
a harmless embezzlement—and shows Mr. Tom
Nawn favorably in a new type of character.
Reception of the little piece was decidedly en-
thusiastc. Another new item was supplied by
the Schenk Bros., acrobats, whose agile strength
recalls Cinquevalli. One Schenk, for instance,
walks up and down stairs with the other Schenk
balanced on him head to head. The Wiltons,
Winton, and A. G. Spry are other good features
of the programme.
** * *
Miss Madeline Royle, a pupil of Herr
Kretschmann’s, gave a pianoforte recital of
difficult or beautiful (or both) compositions at
the Centenary Hall last week to a good audience.
She was assisted by Miss Julie Davies and Mr.
Cyril Monk.
** * *
Melba in grand opera came to pass at
Melbourne Princess’s last Saturday, and was not
such a notable event as she would have been a
month earlier. The house was not crowded, ex-
cept in the dress circle. In the amphitheatre,
where the attendance was only moderate
for a Saturday night, the broad expanses
of bare bench amounted to a satire on
the management’s suspension of the free list.
Pressmen, who are ordinarily engaged in
working for George Musgrove’s interests, under
sufferance, were frozen out of the theatre. All
the pressmen in Melbourne might have been com-
fortably seated in 10s. 6d. seats at the Princess’s
last Saturday. So it seems that the little weeklies
which responded to the management’s recent
contempt, by ignoring all the Melba performances,
have at last got the sneer on their side. In this
fickle country the sneer never stays long on any
side.
••* • •
What is the matter with Melba, a singer
of undoubted celebrity, that she didn’t even get
an “ovation,” or half an ovation, on making her
first Australian appearances in two of her most
famous characters ? Tall, graceful and bonnie, she
looked her young girl parts well enough ; she
acted them prettily ; she sang them as they have
never been sung in Melbourne since Signora
Cuttica’s time. Probably the plump, unimposing
Signora was nearly equal to Melba in voice and
vocalism, although she never had a great market
value. Impossible to preserve a distinct re-
collection of a Voice for many years. One can
only remember its effect upon oneself. Present
scribe thinks Melba surpasses the Cuttica of yore
as Gilda in “Rigoletto” and Marguerite in
“ Faust.” Her tones have a peculiar quality of
youthfulness. The fine, honest, unaffected singing
is in itself most admirable, whilst in the voice
there is enchantment for anyone who wants to
enjoy the good things of this world without con-
sidering whether they might be more wonderful, or
less expensive, or not so wearisomely “ boomed.”
People who now doubt the beauty—the wai-mth,
color, freshness —of Melba’s voice ate the same
who were more or less deaf and blind to
the attractions of Dolores until circumstances
swayed their judgment. Melba was great as
Gilda, the hunchback’s daughter. Her “Caro
nome ” air was a dream of sweet melody. And
the Marguerite performance (garden scene act and
last act) brought out all her art of expressing
emotion with the least possible theatrical display.
Melba is often accused of coldness as an artist, but
this scribe would call her remarkable for
undemonstrative warmth. The prima-donna’s
share of the limited entertainment deserved more
enthusiasm than it got. For the rest, there
was a company of three assistant principals (plus
Miss Maud Harrington for a minute or two) and
no chorus, the acts being chosen to admit of
“grand opera” on this extraordinary scale of
economical completeness. Madame Slapoffski
appeared ’ as Siebel, instead of a contralto, and
subsequently did a quick-change turn as Martha.
Lempriere Pringle was an incidental hired assassin
in the “Rigoletto” scene, and a powerful
Mephisto.
• • * *
Bensaude, the baritone, made a brief,
weak appearance as Gilda’s humpy parent. Louis
Arens was an unpleasing Duke before he came
along ohce again as Faust, in which part he failed
conspicuously as of yore in his principal air, and
was a poor partner for Marguerite in the duets.
The four artists sang the “Faust” selections in
French —un-rapid French which appealed to the
Australian understanding in a way that no native
of France could imitate. Madame Slap.’s pro-
nunciation was especially British. The third and
last appearance of Melba in selections from operas
will take*place on Saturday next (29th).
** * *
“Moths,” in the hands of Musgrove’s
dramatic co. is a moderately pleasing reminder of
two previous productions which Charles Cart-
wright staged in Melbourne at two way-back
periods. Ouida’s lurid drama of immorality
among the dukes and duchesses has grown old for
its age, and Fuschia Leach is an out-of-date cari-
cature these times. Nevertheless, Fuschia and
her tame Duke of Mull appeared to impress a ma-
tinee audience at the Princess’s last Saturday.
The success of the cheerful couple (Miss Edith
Stewart and Sydney Stirling) was very marked,
especially when the lady got out of temper with
the surrounding aristocracy and clean forgot her
cheap ’Murkan accent. ‘ ‘ Moths ” might be better
presented by a co. no stronger in acting capacity
than the crowd at the Princess’s, but more
suited to their parts. Miss Athena Claudius,
as Prince Zouroff’s sister, fills her place
naturally. None of the other sombre characters
is happily played, yet the performance
all round is intelligent. Harcourt Beatty,
as Zouroff, is a profligate of no stage importance,
built on the same lines as Dot Boucicault’s comic
Frenchmen. Miss Farleigh, as the beauteous
heroine, plays spiritedly in the last two acts, and
excites about as little romantic interest as the
Correze who adores her. Albert Grau is a strong,
chubby, husky Jura, not a bit like the calm
personage he ought to be ; Miss Sadler is a color-
less wicked duchess; and Miss Levettiz makes a
pardonable attempt to play Lady Dolly.
#• # *
Her Majesty’s (Melb.) was crowded on
Saturday for a revival of “ Mikado ” ; moreover,
the bulky audience took kindly to this ancient
libel on Japan. In some respects, the revival
beats previous “Mikado” records, or runs them
very close. The girl chorus has never been
younger and brighter than now. Reginald
Roberts scores, freely as Nanki Poo, in spite of a
coarseness in his speaking accents. The singing
voice, which he kept under subjection in “The
Toreador,” turns out to be a healthy, mus-
cular tenor. Florence Young, formerly a
passable Katisha, puts more “make up” and
humor into the sketch this time, and her
enunciation of her song words is excellent.
Carrie Moore, Maud Chetwynd, and the new lady
Lulu Evans are three chippy little maids from the
Jap. seminary. Bathurst improves as Pooh Bah,
and Lauri will be all right as Ko-Ko if he subordi-
nates low comedian Lauri to librettist W. S.
Gilbert. In the name part Thorley doesn’t shine,
but the orchestra and scenic effects are everything
the new generation can reasonably demand.
“Mikado” may be expected to go along until
“ Paul Jones ” is ready to take the stage of the
Williamson temple.
** * *
When the Musgrove dramatic co. was im-
ported nine months ago it had a leading lady
(Miss Lucy Wilson) for the dramas other than
“Sweet Nell.” Now that the other dramas—
“ Sorrows of Satan” and “Moths”—are being
played in Melbourne, there is no Lucy Wilson.
The principal girl-parts fall to Miss * Farleigh,
and they fall rather flat.
** * *
“Little Lord Fauntleroy,” at Melbourne
Bijou, promises to be interesting. The play was
new-fashioned 13 years ago, and has not been imi-
tated or revived meanwhile, so its chance of
success is as good as it was, or better, or worse,
according to how public taste has altered. Then
there is the question re the effect of a bona fide
boy, instead of a precocious damsel disguised as a
boy in the name part. Little Syd. Hallidav is a
handsome, sturdy kiddie of not more than 10. He
got his first stage coaching from Mrs. G. B.
Lewis, and was produced by Hawtrey in the
curtain-raiser “ Counsel’s Opinion.” Women, and
some of the men, were immensely taken by
young Hallidav’s performance in that weak
playlet, although it was very uneven. Sometimes
he talked like an intelligent parrot, but now and
then he would seem absolutely natural and spon-
taneous in speech and action. He’s a very smart
boy, otherwise Hawtrey wouldn’t be pegging
away at the task of teaching him the part of
Fauntleroy, with hundreds of lines and any
amount of ‘ ‘ business ” in it. The trouble is to give
the necessary tone of effeminate prettiness to the
elocution and style of a boyish boy. If Hawtrey,
who plays the old Earl, can bring his young friend
along successfully the play will take care of itself.
The child and the old-man characters are all
important.

Fitzgeralds’ buildings was packed every-
where on Saturday night, when a tank act was
added to the Circus’ aerial and terrestrial “ turns.”
So far, the Fitz repertoire traverses Earth, Air,
and Water. Probably, they’ll soon invade the
fourth Element, and bill a ‘ ‘ Stampede of animals
from the burning prairie.” The “Water Spec-
tacle” has departed from its early melodrama in-
tentions and resolved itself into “Sports on the
Village Arena,” in the course of which sundry
animals undergo (the ordeal of the tank, each
facing the music in its own characteristic way.
The cow is taken unawares by the cold plunge.
She does the unlooked-for trip in an indifferent
way, apparently regarding it as a new scheme
for watering milk. The horses seem excited and
flurried and cause enough splash to make things
nasty for an unseated rider. Elephant Liz. for
sheer sense, is the flower of the swim. With
the cross though conscientious air of an adult
Baptist, she plumps in of her own accord, heaves
out again and waddles off to her warm bed.
Sometimes at rehearsal Lizzie takes possession of
the Tank and bathes at large until the manage-
ment goes distracted, but on Saturday she went
in and out with docility and despatch.

The clever Montrose acrobats have come
along to Melbourne Opera House from Sydney
Tivoli, otherwise the programme is much the
same as last week. Cinquevalli’s last night seems
to have been indefinitely postponed, and Wallace
Brownlow is suspected of an intention to let loose
a new and stirring Australian Anthem next
Saturday. Several previous anthems have flitted
across the mind of this continent like shadows,
but the forthcoming effort is thought to be of an
enduring—not to say sticky—character.

Of the farewell Dolores Recitals at Melb.
Town Hall it is only necessary to remark that the
triumphant little lady will arrive at her fifth
recital to-night (Thursday). Further information
re the Dolores Boom can be obtained from the
advts. Presumably Mdlle. will resume tier fare-
well on Saturday, seeing that last Saturday she
had the Town Hall crammed full, and the shout
of the audience after her “Carnival of Venice”
(with variations) was perhaps the loudest and
most legitimate uproar of its kind in local musical
history.
«* * *
The biff between Badger Purvis and
the Chicken will be withdrawn from Melbourne
Royal after Friday, to the deep regret of all who
admire vigorous forms of dramatic art. Some
nights the ruffled Badger threatens to knock out
the Chicken with an unrehearsed delivery upon the
latter’s jaw, butup to now theprize fight has always
ended in a spurious collapse of Badger aforesaid.
“Saturday Night in London,” taking one con-
sideration with another, deserves a longer run
than a fortnight. It is full of life—and death.
The scene following the Fight starts with
the murder of Miss Guildford by Stirling
White, who subsequently casts Temple Har-
rison from a house-top and throws his
best girl down several flights of stairs,
after which Willard drops White off the
housetop with two bullets in his debased interior,
and the scene closes. Willard’s own death by
violence is reserved for the final chapter, in
accordance with precedent. In nine Anderson
dramas out of every ten Willard is the lucky last
corpse. Next Saturday (29th) “A London Arab”
will shake his grimy tatters at the Royal.
** * *
Adelaide Royal has remained closed, but
Maggie Moore is promised for the end of the
month. Howard Vernon, always a favorite in
Adelaide, has dropped down at the Tivoli with a
co. of his own, and played Planquette’s opera of
20 years ago, “Rip van Winkle.” Otherwise
Melba had the city all to herself, and her two con-
certs crammed the Town Hall as it has, perhaps,
never been crammed before. Melba made herself
very popular in Adelaide, sending £5O each to the
Hospital, Children’s Hospital, and Maternity
Home, and visiting each institution in turn.
Also she visited the fire station, where she offered
to climb an escape if Super. Booker would go
first. But that official carries too much avoir-
dupois to take on any such contract.
The Wild Cat Column.
It is reported that, when the Commonwealth
takes over the State debts and starts to renew them as
they fall due, Treasurer Turner (if he is still in office) will
probably float the early renewal loans in Paris, instead of
London. The idea certainly looks commendable, so far
as concerns that part of the renewal loans which can’t be
taken up locally. The Bank of France has over three
times as much cash in hand as the Bank of England—it
held, at recent advices, about £124,000,000, against the
Bank of England’s £36,000,000. It was lending at 1 per
cent, less than the Bank of England. France is now
almost as large a lender of money to foreign countries as
Britain is, and, at present, it certainly has a great deal
more loose cash to spare. A vast amount of French money
is now being lent out in London, and under these cond i tions
it might be worth while to explain that an honest, indus-
trious, and respectable Commonwealth, which wasn’t
going on any wild jamborees of its own, but merely
wanted to convert existing debts and make them a first
charge on its revenues, desired accommodation. Finan-
cing in Paris should be at least J per cent, cheaper than
in London just now. (N.B. —Dan O’Connor, who has a
beautiful French accent, might be sent over to interview
leading Parisian bankers on the subject.)

,* * • •
Some signs of an increasing scarcity of money
in Australia are visible in the B.N.S.W.’s balance-sheet.
During the past six months, note-circulation, deposits,
bills payable, bills receivable, cash and liquid securities
have all diminished a little. Probably the increasingly
hard-up condition of the N.S.W. Government’s account is
responsible for all these things. The Bank of New South
Wales has a note circulation of £950,370 ; deposits
£21,069,810, and total liabilities to the public, £24,489,557.
Against this it holds £7,462,197 of cash and liquid
securities, equal to nearly 31 per cent. It is a splendidly
solid concern in every way, and looks like the most
durable thing in N.S.W. in these unstable times.

The great success of the Wilfley patent Con-
centrator since its merits became known to the mining
community of Australia, has led to imitations and
piracies under various names being placed upon the
market. The Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate of
London, who own the Australian patents, have decided to
prosecute all users and vendors of concentrating machines
infringing their patents, and action has been commenced
in Victoria. Similar action to protect their rights had to
be taken in America, where the Supreme Court has up-
held Wilfley’s patents, granting an injunction and
damages against the users and vendors of the infringing
machine. The sole manufacturers in Australia of Wilfley's
patent concentrator are the Austral Otis Engineering
Company of South Melbourne, who supply customers
direct or through any of their accredited agents, in the
various mining centres.*

Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W and
J. Lempnere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*

Last year the Temperance and General’s Ordi-
nary Life Department earned £12,017 in interest and
rents, equal to £4 9s. 4d. per cent, per annum on the
average funds. This is a falling away from the £4 10s. 3d.
per cent, earned in 1901, but better than the £4 6s. Id.
per cent, of 1900 : and, in any case, it is a good average
rate. The Industrial Department earned £1326,
equal to £4 9s. 2d. per cent, as compared with £4 10s. 2d.
per cent, in 1901, and £4 ss. 7d. per cent, in 1900 —
also a good average rate. The assets look respectable
enough, the worst feature being that the co. holds
£35,452 of property as mortgagee in possession, as com-
pared with £104,046 of live mortgages. This is an ex-
ceedingly large proportion of the mortgaged properties
to be abandoned and left on the mortgagee’s hands,
though, as the rate of interest earned is reasonably high,
it is possible that full provision has been made for any
possible loss. Taken as a whole, the T. and G. Society
looks better than it did last year and much better than
the year before, though there is still much space for
improvement.

This is not exactly how the co. shows its accounts, but it
exhibits the real position as accurately as possible. In
1901 the co. carried £12,500 of profits to capital, making
the latter £50,000, and it called' its dividend (£4000) 8 per
cent, on £50,000. In 1902 it adds another £12,500 of pro-
fits to capital, and it calls its dividend (£5000) 8 per cent,
on £62,500. But the real position as it affects the original
shareholders is as shown above.
During the last eleven years the Colonial Mutual
Fire Insurance Co. has provided £10,295 to cover depre-
ciation of assets; has added £25,000 to its capital out of
profits; and has changed a deficit of £19,911 into a
reserve of £53,595. The shareholders’ funds have, there-
fore, increased during this period by £103,506. In 1891
the co.’s foreign business had reduced it to a very low
ebb. It had been practising the policy of “a wide
diffusion of risks,” which bogus insurance experts are so
fond of declaring to be essential ; when it is proposed to
start a State Fire Insurance Department, the busi-
ness of which will necessarily be confined to one
State, the contempt of certain experts for the ama-
teur persons who can’t see that a “ wide diffusion
of risks” is the only way to success is some-
thing appalling. Now, after 11 years of a non-diffusion
policy, the Colonial Mutual is one of the richest fire
offices in Australia. Its assets, too, are mostly good
liquid securities—State securities and bank deposits—
and the income from investments alone (£3873) is sufficient
in itself to pay 10 per- cent, on the original capital of
£37,500.
* jJ- » m *
Melbourne Age suggests that, considering all
the deficits, the Australian Government should make
advances to the hard-up States, as authorised by the Con-
stitution- But five States out of the six have deficits,
and the Australian Governmentcan’t raise enough from the
sixth State (Westralia) to help the five even if any sane
person would dream of thus robbing one Peter to help
five Pauls. And if the Australian Government borrows
money for the States to use as revenue—well, the States
are good enough at that themselves, and should do their
own dirty work. The Australian Government has before
it the task of taking over and consolidating and renewing
the State debts. It will have to borrow' for that purpose,
and to borrow cheaply it must keep its own credit clean.
To begin by raising loans for the States to use as revenue
would be killing the reputation on which its success
depends. The only thing the Australian Government can
do is to practise all reasonable economy itself, and to im-
pose some direct taxation so as to get the means to assist
the States which lack the courage to tax themselves suffi-
ciently. There is no other way, apart from the old
nefarious device of using loans as revenue.

The National Insurance Co. last year issued at a hand-
some premium 3925 unallotted shares, making its paid-up
capital £lOO,OOO. The year just closed has certainly been
a magnificently prosperous one ; yet even now the
National hasn’t quite made up its lost ground. In the
early eighties its reserves reached £200,000. In those
days the old familiar mania for a wide diffusion of busi-
ness seized upon it, and it spread itself to England,
India, and other remote places. In 1894 it had just
begun to recover from that disastrous experiment, in
which much good Maori legal tender was lost, and which
is not likely to be repeated. Very good progress is cer-
tainly being made with the repairing process, and the
National Fire and Marine Insurance Co. of Maoriland is
now a sounder concern than it ever was before. As a
side issue, last year’s income from interest and rents was
£lo,3o6—more than sufficient in itself to pay a 10 per
cent, dividend. Also, the net premium income is now
£109,476.

“Waddy”: The Cobar Chesney mine share-
holders evidently intend going on with work, and from
chairman Cain’s remarks the hope of smelting the ore is
not finally extinguished. Considering that the ore
carries 85 per cent, insoluble matter, and counting same
as silica—to make a 45 per cent, silica slag it would require
just about 2 tons of the 50 per cent, iron ore the co. has
developed to flux every 1 ton of ore. That is to say, the
furnace charge would contain about one-third ore. Allow-
ing 4 per cent, copper in the ore a metallurgist might be
expected to recover about three units of the four,, or
roughly, about 275. worth of material to .pay the cost of
smelting, refining and realisation. Some gold is supposed
to be in the ore, which would add to the value of the
product, and at the same time increase realisation costs.
Water-concentration has not been a howling success ;
crushing to about 2-inch ring and passing on to a
travelling belt to let boys pick it free from abso-
lutely clean rock, with concentration afterwards of
the enriched balance, might show' more favorably. The
Elmore Oil process ought to be worth trying if the oil-
ring in Australia isn’t too greedy, but magnetic separa-
tion for copper ores of this class has not reached the
point of absolute success, and, if it does so, the dust-
problem in connection with magnetic separation is a
difficult thing to overcome. Perhaps the crowd with the
option on Great Cobar has the best solution to offer—
viz., amalgamation with the Great Cobar scheme.
•' • * •
Cobar Chesney half-yearly report is as cheerful
a document as could be expected from a mine in which
little or no work has been done. Total expenditure on
mine-development was £247, exclusive of salaries ; and
“ to exercise supervision,” as Chairman Cain puts it, over
this expenditure, £363 had to be paid in directors’ fees
and travelling expenses, and £258 to “ Melbourne office,”
in addition to which there is the customary Chesney item
of “ reports and sampling,” £247. It would be interesting
to have a record of the total amount which has been
paid by this unfortunate co. for “expert” reports,
and still more- interesting to know how much
more of added wisdom there is as the result.
Exception was taken at the meeting to the seemingly ex-
cessive amounts for directors’ fees and office expenses, but
Mr. Cain satisfied the discontented u’ith the statement
that, “ as far as the duties of the directors are concerned,
it doesn’t make any difference whether the mine is closed
down or not; they still had to exercise supervision ;it is
the same whether cheques for £5O or £5OO have to be
signed.” Chairman notified that it would be necessary
to make a call of ljd. to meet debenture interest, due in
January, and, he might have added, directors’ fees and
office expenses. The most encouraging feature of the
meeting was the speech made by Manager Woolooek, a
gentleman whom no one would accuse of being tainted
with a suspicion of optimism. He spoke in glowing
terms of the prospects generally, and strongly urged
shareholders to stick to their interests, which re-
mark revealed the M‘Eacharn in a new character—
that of a hflinorist—as he retorted :“ We must—we can’t
sell them.” Rumors have been current for some time that
co. was developing what was likely*fco prove a very valu-
able iron flux deposit, but no official intimation on the
subject has been forthcoming until now. The deposit is
about four miles from the mine, and for 50ft. deep it is
described as massive ironstone, assaying over 50 per cent,
metallic iron. The width at surface is 40ft. for several
chains in length. It is said that “ locally the indications
are regarded as being highly favorable to the occurrence
of copper or other metallic bodies at depth.” Should
further development prove the existence of an exten-
sive body of this class of ironstone, it will mean the
salvation of the Chesney co. It will leave no
doubt as to how and where to treat the immense
quantities of ore which are in sight —it will
hardly be necessary’ even for the directors to have
another “expert” report. The question which they
will have to face will be how to raise the money for the
requisite smelting equipment, which, on a scale'commen-
surate with the capacity of the mine, will cost not less
than £40,000, so that with the amount owing on deben-
tures £60,000 will be wanted. Reconstruction or increase
of capital will be inevitable as the uncalled capital is only
£7875. Additional capital to the amount of 4s. per share
might meet the case.
The explanation, or defence, or whatever it is,
published by Alex. Stewart and Geo. Smith, formerly
general and mining managers of the Chillagoe, amounts
to about this : Some bad men got hold of them, and used
them, and then threw them away and said that it was all
their fault. They say that they didn’t know they were
being used—that they didn’t think the men for whom
they were working were capable of such things. Yet,
though the directors’ “ peculiar ” ways of doing business
were from the outset noted by Mr. Stewart, he
doesn’t seem to have taken proper trouble to guard
against being “used.” On the contrary he boomed the
concern by plunging into a huge scheme of development
without finding out for sure that there was the money
to carry it through ; and, as the public assumed that
a general manager who drew £lO,OOO or so a year, and
whose name was Alexander Stewart into the bargain,
wouldn’t start work where there wasn’t the funds to finish,
the public bought shares, and fell in. Mr. Stewart blames
the directors for stopping work before it was half finished ;
but the public will blame Stewart for starting work at all
without having found out something about co.’s resources.
Afterwards, he made a still worse blunder; and again,
curiously enough, the result was to drag the public in
still deeper. It was just when some of the plunging
crowd were beginning to smell rats, and when it was,
therefore, very necessary to “ restore confidence ’’—there
were still many shares unsold. Mr. Stewart was there-
fore asked to estimate the quantity and value of ore
exposed in the 16 months he had been at work. He had
made an estimate in June, 1898—had reckoned 500,000
tons as having been as good as proved above the 100 ft.
level in the co.’s properties. Now, after 16 months’
more, he still reported 500,000 tons as proved.
The directors evidently thought that wasn’t good enough
to restore confidence with, and invited Mr. Stewart
to say that it was another 500,000 tons altogether. But
he could not say that; what he did say was that it was a
“distinct estimate.” The other really was not proved
ore at all; this was—“ proven by work'on portions only ”
of the ground covered in 1898 ; that is, on the portion
to which work had been confined. And in another
wire he said that this portion was only a small
part of the whole. That, as a means of getting
over the rats, was better; and no doubt somebody
sold some more shares. Yet now Mr. Stewart says that
the estimate, instead of being of ore proved on only a
small part of the co.’s property, was “ a general estimate
of the whole field 1 ” Surely some friend ought to have
taken Mr. Stewart aside before this was printed and told
him that, of all the things that have been said about him,
nothing worse has been said than he here proposed to say
about himself. And, worse still, he in a way defends
what he did (though he admits he ought to have
been more careful) by saying that “ absolutely no expen-
diture depended upon the estimate.” That might be
so as far as the co. was concerned, but it is quite
possible that a lot of people expended their money
on the strength of the estimate. What a de-
fence to make for the man who, reckoning by posi-
tion and salary, was at the head of Australian mining
engineers 1 It will be poor satisfaction for the public who
fell in to know that Mr. Stewart most likely fell in, too.
He didn’t get their money—that went in other directions
altogether. And it was not a fair arrangement, because
it was he who (for the most part, if not altogether,
unwittingly) made the transfer of cash, so easy and big.
* * * ■ m
Mr. Smith makes out a stronger case. If his
statements are true, the directors are chargeable with
concealing the true state of affairs for over five months,
and the surprise expressed by them in June, 1901, when,
after a visit to the mine, they shocked the speculative
world with a declaration that there was only a small
supply of ore available for smelting, was simulated,
because in the January previous Mr. Smith says he
submitted a detailed statement which closely corre-
sponded with the estimate subsequently made by
Mr. Weinberg. The circular discloses that there
was considerable friction between the managers and
the Board almost from the start. The managers, on
their own showing, were all anxiety to rush on mine-
development work while the directors were equally
anxious that the main effort should be to complete the
railway and reduction works. The explanation offered
by Messrs. Stewart and Smith for the long delay in
publishing their statement is that they had no wish to
say anything which would influence the shareholders
“ either in favor of or against the proposals of the Board to
save the Company from the position into which they had
brought it.” That sort of whitewash won’t stick.
* * * •
Chillagoe itself is in slightly better feather of
late, but a correspondent writes that the once well-
thought-of Ruddygore has proved disappointing and is
shut up. No winding plant of any size is on any of the
shows that are working, and of the latter Zillmanton,
Boomerang, and Redcap groups are the most promising.
None of them are proved below 250 ft. The furnace has
to go cautiously; it wouldn’t do to outrun the ore supply.
• * • •
The Einasleigh Freehold (N.Q.), having appar-
ently failed to get its £150,000 for another railway, all
work has ceased there except that of two men left in
charge. No one in London at ,this date will look at any-
thing in the neighborhood of Chillagoe. A tremendous
sensation was caused in Georgetown (N.Q.) by two
speculators announcing that the Einasleigh has the coin
for the tram and every miner in the country who owned
a reef immediately saw a>epetitioq of the golden shower
of English money that once poured into Georgetown in
the days when miners slept on night shift, worked five
shifts a week and were paid for six shifts at 13s. 4d. a
shift. Georgetown got drunk on anything that had a bite
in it, and it is probably still-drunk on its disappointment.
David Lindsay is reported to be taking up mines in the
district, and if report can be trusted there are one or two
shows near Georgetown worth investigation, the Durham
and Lord Byron, to wit.
* • • •
Mt. Lyell report for past half-year is satisfactory
enough so far as it relates to the operations of that period.
It shows that 159,450 tons of ore were smelted, or 22,651
tons more than in any other six months. The product
was 3608 tons copper, 341,3460 z. silver, and 11,6810 z. gold.
The declared profit is £45,000, or about £7500 less than
that of the previous half-year, Which is more than
accounted for by the reduced value of copper, the
averages for the two terms being £56 and £53 per ton.
The cost of producing blister coppfer has been reduced from
21s. 3d. to 19s. Bd. per ton of ore.' Total production of-the
mine to Sept. 30 was 43,306 tons copper, 3,609,3620 z. silver,
and 137,1790 z. gold, and £896,886 has been paid in divs.
This is a great record, but the end of the mine would
seem within measurable distance. The report states that
considerable exploration work has been done in the
deeper ground by diamond drill and otherwise, “but
these operations have not disclosed anything of import-
ance.” Although the rich ore worked in the upper level
was met with at No. 8 level, “ it unfortunately did not
prove to be of any large extent.” The candor of this
statement is specially significant. The hope of the future
of the co. will lie in the acquisition of other mines, par-
ticularly N. Lyell.

North Mt. Lyell mine : Whispered in Queens-
town that the smelters will not take anything less than
14 per cent, ore, to the extent of 350 tons a day. To get
350 tons, the eyes, also the mouth, ears, and brains
of the mine must be picked out. Pity so good a mine
should be done to death by ignorance ! Copper, too, is
down to the £5O mark again, and while silver stays at
Is. 10Jd., copper can’t rise, nor can lead or tin. There’s
an economic fact lurking in that paragraph.

Curtin Davis Co.’s report is a sorry document,
English shareholders, most of whom gave 40s. for their
shares, won’t send in their proxies to permit the directors
to wind up the co. There is £9197 still on hand, a
remarkable survival from the ancient flotation, which the
directors can’t distribute and so must Spend. This is
how they are doing it. Forty-two mines were under con-
sideration during the previous six months, of which 11
were inspected, 5 were being considered, and 26 did not
warrant a visit. The examination of properties cost £l5O
and the wealthy directors took £loo—for supervising a
mme-watchman working for 20s. a week for six months 1
No wonder the British shareholder declines to entrust
such a Board even with his proxy 1

After months of goading from Stock Exchange
and press, the Briseis directors have submitted a state-
ment as to the position of affairs at the mine. The new
manager thinks it will be possible to remove the whole of
.?. overburden in 18 months, and meanwhile enough tin
will be won from the southern portion of the workings to
PuV.uT ex P ens es- The directors assure shareholders
that they have every confidence in the property paying
divs. This official statement has not had the effect of
reassuring shareholders, who regard with dismay the
prospect of having to wait for profits for nearly two
years. Profits will depend on the value of tin, and it
will be unprecedented if that metal maintains anythin)?
like its present price for the period named.

Great Fingall continues to open out satisfac-
torily at the lower levels, despite all assertions to the
contrary. Unbiassed experts, who have recently in-
spected the mine, are quite enthusiastic over the develop.
ments,and are satisfied that the co. has a very great
future before it, and that ere long it will head the gold
?o 0 0,o cers , 0f A « straJla - During Oct. the mine produced
12,2380 z. from 7643 tons—value, £41,754. As a good deal
has been made by bears and pessimists of the recent fall-
mg on in the value of the gold, it is satisfactory to note
that, on this occasion, the value is £3 Bs. 3d. per oz., the
highest realised since the new mill started. The value
per ton of ore is £5 9s. 3d., which is also a record. The

* * * *
Boulder (W.A.) continues to show a supposed
increased yield of gold from month to month, but the
mcieasem quantity is gained at the expense of qu dity.
The yield for Oct. of 14,5170 z. was valued at £44,535, or
only £3 Is. 4d. an ounce. This means that the bullion
contained practically 28 per cent, of alloy. Nearly two
years ago a furnace to refine the bullion was erected, but,
probably because it would reduce the monthly output by
over 40000 z., it has not been used. No development work
is yet being done at the 1600 ft. level, but a diamond drill
bore is being put in to test the ground to the east. In
the upper levels nothing payable has been found in this
part of the property. It is stated that the Merton
furnaces will reduce the cost of roasting by nearly 25 per
cent., or about a shilling a ton.
• • * *
Associated Northern (W.A.) is still shipping
rich sulpho-telluride ore to Dapto, in addition to treating
oxidised ore at Lakeside battery. During Oct. 1264 tons
were milled for 12210 z., and 1,082 tons smelted for 84600 z.
The gold was valued at £31,900, which leaves a large
margin of profit. The sinking of the main shaft has been
resumed. Nothing further has been heard of the 20-head
mill, which, it was stated, was shortly to be erected on
this property. Co/s second div. of ss. per share means a
distribution of £87,500, a splendid showing for a co. which
has spent no money on treatment plant. The original
£lOO,OOO working capital of the co. is still intact.
* * * *
Hannan’s Reward and Mt. Charlotte (W.A.)
showed a better result for their initial crushing than
anticipated, the average panning out at 15dwts. per ton.
The co.’s exchequer is again in a bad way, the £15,000
provided by the reconstruction in Jan. having already
been expended in patching up old machinery and getting
the mine into working order. To raise the wind tributors
are again to chip in and work the rich narrow veins,
which are a feature of the property. The mine is
absolutely worthless for a Bull co. and will never .yield
any return to shareholders, though it may provide em-
ployment for miners and directors for a long time to come.
* » • *
The Ivanhoe (W.A.) is gradually reducing its
working costs, the figures for Oct. being 355. Bd., the
lowest yet recorded. Kalgurli gives its total at 38s. 10d.,
but this only includes 2s. lOd. for development, against
6s. lOd. in the case of the Ivanhoe, and the latter mine
contains a far more regular formation than the former.
Lake View Consols publishes its costs as 345. 9d, but this
does not include cost of development, repairs or royalty,
and is, consequently, wholly misleading.
The D Arcy Wentworth mine, Lucknow (N S
Wales), was recently auctioned on account of debenture’-
holders, and was bought by Frank Gardner’s representa-
tive for £12,000. It is understood that the successful
Gardner will shortly visit Sydney with his French bride.

Dr. J. A. Beattie, Medical Superintendent
of the N.S.W. Govt. Tubercule Farm, alias
Liverpool Asylum, is off for a long holiday to
Delhi Durbar. It is only quite recently that
this lucky official had a trip to England on
“leave” for some nine months; this second
excursion is to repair his health. The many
unfortunates doomed to death at the deadly
institution over which he presided have no Delhi
in front of them. No one can blame the Medical
Super, for escaping from the sights and sounds at
Liverpool Asylum, but surely the unspeakable
horrors of that institution can only be added to by
the frequent absences of the principal permanent
medical officer.
* * * ' *
Victoria also has its pitiable phthisis
problem. There is no adequate hospital accom-
modation for consumptives, and the small sana-
torium that is provided is always overcrowded.
A little while ago a few beds were by some chance
available, and letters were sent to persons who
had applied before for admission and were “ wait-
ing their turn.” Extracts from some of the
replies :
I received your note, and am sorry to say my son died
two months ago.
The applicant, Frank O’Donoghue, is dead since July.
I am very sorry to state that my daughter Lizzie will
not require the papers you so kindly sent, as she is so
very weak that she is unable to get up from bed, and the
doctor says it is only a matter of a few days.
I thank you and the committee for your kind offer. I
am too weak to travel.
You will, no doubt, be surprised to learn that my dear
sister has passed away since she made her application.
I write to thank you for your kindness in forwarding
the application papers to my sister, but am sorry to state
that she is too ill to think of going to the Sanatorium.
After these six mournful dirges of “Too late”
had been read at a meeting of the committee of
the sanatorium, the secretary was asked “not to

A shuddersome cut from the report of the
S. Aus. stock-inspector :
486 cattle were reported with tuberculosis. If in use
for the dairy they were withdrawn from milking and

And still Adelaide calls itself ‘ ‘ the sanitary city ” !
Carnegie threatens to demoralise Australia.
Hardly a week passes without it being mentioned
in the papers that some person or corporation has
written to the big ironmaster asking for some of
the blood-money he made at Homestead. So far,
N.S.W. Works Minister Owe’Sullivan hasn’t ap-
pealed to him for a £5,000,000 loan, but that may
be coining. Rand wick (Sydney) is a wealthy
suburb, inhabited chiefly by M’sP,, horse-owners,
and bookmakers, but even Rand wick is cadging
for the coin wherewith to start a library of yellow-
backed fiction. Carnegie was a hard, brutal suc-
cess in his middle age ; in his declining years he
threatens to be a failure. His pile is going to
found libraries where Mrs. Henry Wood and
Marie Corelli will be poured on the rising genera-
tion ; and to universities of the old mouldering
type, where they teach the bones and skeletons of
dead languages, and the defunct gridirons and
fowls and semaphores that used to be alphabets,
and the dust of the Greek particle, and the old
useless bog of the Latin conjunction. With the
money he has given away, he might have regene-
rated a country like Ireland or Portugal—a coun-
try that wasn’t too large for one man to handle,
and whose people had possibilities. He might
have cleaned out its big landlords, created a
peasant proprietary, established schools of indus-
try where trades, not old metaphysics, would be
taught, and been the founder of a new nation.
And, instead, he has helped to increase the
demand for spectacles, and many blear-eyed gene-
rations will pour over old tomes, and wipe their
green goggles, and call him accursed.
* * * •
When Attorney-General Deakin has a
little spare time he might establish an Australian
Registry of Notaries. Appointments as notary
are at present for sale on application to the Arch-
bishop of Canterbury, Eng., and applications (not
necessarily successful) cost a matter of £OO, part
fees to English law-agents, part fees to the Arch-
bishop’s Surrogate. The regulation is that appli-
cant must hold a certificate from two Notaries of
his town, affirming that he is a “person of
known probity, sober life, and conversation con-
formable to the doctrine of the Church of England
as by law established ” and well affected to King
Ned. Melb. Society of Notaries has formed a
close corporation in order to monopolise the half-
guinea a signature fees, and no one can hope to
buy the Archbishop’s warrant unless he pay hand-
somely to the society at this end as well as to the
Surrogate at t’other side.
• * * •
The W.C.T.U. will fight to the death the
proposal of N.S.W. Liquor Bill to allow beer-
licenses to oyster saloons. There is something
mysteriously salacious about the oyster, some
smack of Cupid, some suggestion of giddiness.
Why? Who knows ? But the W.C.T.U. recog-
nises the fact, and, if it can prevent such a disas-
ter, the immoral midnight oyster won’t be further
buttressed by a beer license. Yet why should
oyster saloons, which are already licensed to sell
Australian wine, not also be licensed to sell Aus-
tralian beer ?
* * * *
Mrs. Sangal was attended at her confine-
ment by two doctors, for fear that Nature should
cheat the hangman. Iceberg Irvine apparently
intends “to take no notice ” of petitions on her
behalf coming from outside Victoria.
Ethel Herringe, a comely housemaid at
the Club House Hotel, Cowra, N.S.W., one night
last week arranged with the local minister, Rev.
R. Seymour-Smith, to attend the hotel after
closing hours to marry her to the proprietor,
M. J. Lee. When the parson arrived at
the kitchen, attended by two witnesses, the pros-
pective bridegroom baulked at the ceremony,
whereupon the girl drew a revolver and wounded
him twice. He is now likely to die. The girl,
when arrested, stated that Lee had sworn “ by his
mother in Heaven ” that if he did not marry her
that night she could shoot him. This Lee said, in
his dying depositions, was correct. Lee was, how-
ever, in total ignorance of the proposed visit of the
parson. Ethel Herringe was in mourning—sup-
posedly for a lover of hers who died at St.
Vincent’s Hospital, Sydney, where he had been
removed from the Drayton Grange. Lee also had
been a contingenter; and there is a strongly
martial flavor about the whole affair—from which,
by the way, it is evident that lovely woman
doesn’t look upon the receipt of the franchise as a
reason why she should abandon the little privileges
of her sex.

‘‘Alex. M.” to The Bulletin :
Discoursing of the recent electrical happenings in
Victoria, N.S.W. Government Astronomer Russell appears,
as reported, to have blundered egregiously in confusing
globular lightning with St. Elmo’s Fire ; and in asserting
that the latter phenomenon “is hardly witnessed once in
10 lifetimes.” St. Elmo’s Fire— nautici, the “ corposant ”
(corposdnto)— is explained by electricians as due to a
continuous but gentle discharge of atmospheric elec-
tricity on to the extremity of a conducting vehicle.
There is certainly nothing violent about the manifesta-
tion ; whereas the "fire-ball” is a very destructive form
of true lightning, occurring only during severe actual
electric disturbance. In 1862, during a memorable
thunderstorm in the North of Ireland, I was one of many
who saw a fiery sphere, apparently about 2ft. in diameter,
travel horizontally near the ground for about 300yds.
before it “ exploded,” or, at all events, disappeared with
a loud detonation. The corposant I have seen twice ;
once in ’69, off the coast of Antigua (West Indies), and
again in ’B3, in the Straits of Sunda, not long before the
terrific Krakatoa convulsion.

The notable divorce-case so long pending
in Adelaide, has been put through at last in a
curiously confidential manner. First there was a
motion to take off the record the name of the co-
respondent and proceed without one. Then the
case came into Court undefended. Petitioners
evidence consisted of a letter from his wife, saying
that she had not gone wrong “with the gentle-
man you have dragged into it,” but that she had
gone wrong. Also, he admitted to his Honor
that she had mentioned a name to him. “ Alle-
gations reported proved.” But what a chance for
a strong Judge to insist that the thing should be
dealt with straightforwardly, and that while he
sat on That Bench rich and poor were alike, and
no one should tamper with the Majesty of Law !
• * * *
S. Australia’s pending murder trial is
characteristically “respectable.” Husband and
wife couldn’t “hit it”; so, in a series of quite
loving letters on the subject, they agreed to live
apart. They got as far as the division of the fur-
niture, when the hot word led to the taunt, and
a too-convenient gun did the rest. The most
pathetic part of what altogether is an inexpres-
sibly sad affair—the quarrel was all over a photo,
of one of the children. Neither would part with
it to the other. And thus, by a malignant freak
of fortune, what might so easily have been the
instrument of reconciliation —what any self-
respecting novelist would certainly have used for
the purpose—turned, instead, one to the grave
and the other to the cell.
This from Mackay (Q.) Chronicle ;
The cook on one of our coasting boats sent for a ss.
ticket in “ Tattersall’s ” sweep on Caulfield Cup. To the
stewardess he said, “ I’ll give you half of whatever I win.”
On the arrival of the vessel at Townsville the other day a
wire was waiting for the cook, and its contents advised
him that he had drawn first prize—about £6750 net. He
immediately sought the stewardess and advised her of
his luck, at the same time saying, “ I have to give you
£3376. What do you say to taking me and the lot?”
The offer w 7 as accepted, and on the arrival of the vessel
in Brisbane the pair are to be married.
THE OLD SLIP- RAILS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
rrrHEN the mopoke sounds the knock-off for the
KV day,
And I take the twilight track beneath the trees,
Though I’m tired enough to stagger all the way,
And there seems to be no stiffening in the knees,
I am calmly, soundly happy with a joy that never
fails,
’Cos Eliza comes to meet me at the old slip-rails!
It was spring-time when I brought her to the hut,
On the strength of fifty acres under wheat;
And the rails were then a prime pair, newly-cut,
Flinging forth the scent of blue-gum sweet,
And a jackass laughed above us when I tore aside
her veils
And kissed, and bade her welcome o’er the flung-
down rails.
v.
There’s a house-full now of youngsters, large and
small,
And I toil from early dawn till late at night;
There are evenings when the lonely curlew's call
Seems saying to my heart, “ Give up the fight.
But the great heart of Eliza is a thing that never
fails,
And I leave my woes behind me at the old slip-
rails.
When the deeper shadows round me later fall,
And I’ve paid the last instalment up to life,
When to me there sounds the final mopohe call—
A signal for my soul to cease its strife—
Then I’ll count it worth the struggle if, in one of
Heaven’s dales,
I stoop to hiss Eliza o’er a set of blue-gum rails.

“F.H.” returns to the charge, re “slinks.”
“ Theta ” (B. 15/11/’O2) makes rather amusing reading.
The fact that he was “ sitting within a stone’s-throw ” of
the railway station he mentions doesn’t make it any more
probable that from said station was despatched in one
day half of Melbourne’s veal-supply for one week-, and,
in short. I’m ready to bet “ Theta” a fiver, either that no
forwarding-agent in Victoria ever in one day sent 700
“ slinks ” (i.e., calves whose dead weight with head and
feet was only 401b5.), or that no such consignment was
ever sent from any Victorian station. Money talks !
As for “ the big well-fed western district cows,” I did
not deny that a new-born “slink’’could weigh 401bs. ;
but what I contend is, that, as the Meat Act prohibits
the sale of any calf whose weight (without head, feet or
offal) is under) 401b5., the alleged “slinks” must have
weighed, alive, between 60 and 701bs.—about the weight
of a three or four weeks-old calf. I agree with “ Theta,”
that the slink-business is loathsome and disreputable
(“Theta’s” dairyman-friend being evidently a good
representative thereof), and nothing would give me more
pleasure than to see the killer, the seller and the buyer of
any calf under four weeks old, get six months “ hard
very hard.

“Via ” to The Bulletin :
“Galvo ” (8., 15/11/’O2) makes light of the statement
in B. of 11/10/’O2 that the Brisbane ’bus-driver was
gambling with death when he tried to push aside a
“live” wire with his bare hand. (The par, as written
had nothing about his foot being on the brake—that was
an editorial addition). As a matter of fact, I saw the
occurrence, and it was a wet day, and man, ’bus and
ground were wet. Wonder how “ Galvo ” would like to
try it on under those conditions? If so, I hope he will
make over his insurance policy to me. As regards shocks,
it is considered impossible to positively say what will kill
a human. In New York they electrocute with a current
equal to 1300 volts, and in a recent case they had to give
three shocks of 40sec. duration before the deed was done.
Have myself received a shock from a 425-cell Meidenger
battery on short circuit, the sensations being first, that
of a fearful blow in the stomach, then sweating and
violent vomiting Years ago an American electrician fell
between two contact-points in a power-house, and a
current of 650 volts traversed his body for at least
s'uin. before he was rescued ; yet he is alive to-day but
bald as an egg, and it is alleged that even now his body
will deflect a galvanometer if placed on his bare skin.
Also, Campbell, the Q. railway-swindler, was “operated”
upon by a Govt, medico for two hours every night for a
week to see if he was really paralysed, and Campbell
stood it without a tremor. What would kill him ? (If
anybody wants to know what Campbell stood let him get a
small portable battery, either frictional or acid, put a wet
sponge in one handle and hold it, put the other handle
m a basin of water, drop a silver coin therein, and with
the other hand dip into the water and recover the coin
without grimacing or jumping.) Campbell would be an
ideal line-repairer for “ Galvo.” Most men I know prefer
a voltometer, it’s cheaper than a doctor’s bill and handier
than a coffin.
A FAIR EXCHANGE.
“ Say, mate, what about swappin' pants?"
Children placed out by the State in the
various provinces have been notably freakish—
witness the “stone-throwing ” mysteries now and
then reported. The latest yarn is of a “ placed -
out ” girl who was at service in a sparsely-
populated district, and, when the master of the
house was absent, terrified his wife with an
“attempted burglary ”of the premises. Muddy
foot-marks were found on the verandah, window-
bolts had been forced open, and in the garden
were marks as of a heavy man rushing along.
Night after night such little things happened,
until the mistress sent for a local black tracker,
who failed to find the midnight prowler. Finally,
the sergeant of police took a hand, and immediately
suspected the girl. The mistress scouted the idea,
but a simple trap set by the sergeant soon settled
the point. The young lady, just to liven up
things, had borrowed a pair of the master's boots
to stump round the house in, and had duly
worked in all the other little burglarial circum-
stances. _ Lucky that her desire for excitement
took this form. The usual hysterical female
directs her mendacities to more dangerous ends. “
* * * *
Victorian State Children’s Department has
just acquired a new ward in the person of a child
of four, who was lately discovered asleep on the
pavemen tof a Richmond thoroughfare. Asked as
to the why and the wherefore of this unusual pro-
ceeding, the infant explained that she was out
walking with “Auntie,” when the latter com-
manded her to lie down and go to sleep, which
she promptly did. Then Auntie faded away.
The damsel gives her name as Eileen James, but
as nobody knows any James who has lost a small
girl, and as no one has claimed her, it seems to be
another exposition of the tired feeling that
characterises the great tribe of Bill-Jim, alias
James.
The result of the action against the
N.S.W. Govt, by the returned swaddies claiming
colonial as well as Imperial pay, is a big surprise
to the soldiers themselves. Most of them never
for a moment dreamed of a win. Moss and Cohen
would not lend the ’tingenters a cent on their
chances of winning, and told intending borrowers
who offered to mortgage £2O and £3O chances for a
fiver, that they hadn’t a ghost of a show. But the
men have now won in two courts; the decision
affects the second contingent, and possibly some
of the “A” Battery men and a portion of that
huge joke,_ the Citizens’ Bushmen. The win was
on a ‘‘point” (Mr. Justice Owen remarked that
the claim would be utterly scouted anywhere out-
side a court of law) which arose from the lax
method in which the papers in connection with
the enrolment of the troops were drawn up.
For that, some gilded military officers are en-
tirely responsible. N.S.W. can hug the consola-
tion that she has probably paid the biggest prices
for soldiers since the days of the Roman Pre-
torian guard.
* * * *
It is the baldest nonsense the cohen-
tingenters (or most of them) trying to get work on
the patriotic racket. Many of their number had
never done a day’s work in their lives before they
enlisted ; and most of the rest went because “five
bob a day ” and their keep was a very much better
wage than they had been getting. If “ their coun-
try ” had needed them at one shilling a day, ninety
out of a hundred of them would have stayed at
home and left their country in the lurch. Now
that they are back and stone-broke they cry for
preference on the strength of their patriotism and
self-denial. Naturally an employer prefers a
steady man who won’t go roving at the first chance.
Employers are not to be blamed if they have given
to other men the jobs which the cohentingenters
left to earn better pay.
* • * *
A distinctly unusual charge is levelled
against a Perth (W.A.) dentist. He reported to
the police that a young woman had died on his
premises while having a tooth drawn. The police,
however, have a theory that she died as the result
of an illegal operation, and that the tooth was
drawn after death by way of providing corrobora-
tion. At all events they have arrested the tooth-
smith.
m m m *
“ Petty ” thieving has become so common
lately that the big Sydney retailers are taking
drastic steps to suppress the customer who, after
turning out half the shop and buying a few hair-
pins, departs with a seven-and-sixpenny trifle as
a keepsake. A well-to-do-looking person, who
proved to be wife of a professional man, was
lately caught with some 15s. worth of things in
her parasol. She was proceeded against and fined
£5. Some of the big places have been victimised
to an incredible extent, mainly when the cus-
tomer and an assistant are in collusion.
* ■ • *
Apropos the recent case in which an
English peeress exhibited herself in her corona-
tion finery, on her own lawn, to the country
people, at 3d. a head, for the benefit of the local
hospital. In North Queensland, 15 or 20 years
ago, a certain young English lordling, finding
himself stranded in one of the coastal towns,
had a sort of horse-box arrangement made with a
small window and a spring blind. Over the
window, in large letters, was :
THIS IS A LORD.
3d. a look.
PULL THE STRING AND THE SHOW BEGINS.
A slot in the box received the coin, and the
blind ran up and exhibited the youth in evening
dress. He would then indulge in a little chaff
with the crowd, give a short account of his for-
bears and describe the family place. Then he
would ring down on that lot, wait for the tent
to fill up again, and repeat the performance.
What he made isn’t known, but it was usually
enough to take him to bed in his boots.
This resourceful chappie is now a respected repre-
sentative of England’s aristocracy—married to the
daughter of a hundred earls —but the wild oats he
sowed in N.Q. have not yet been all harvested.
* * * *
A strange, crazy kind of horror, which
looks as if it might have had its origin in the
penny dreadful variety of literature, is reported
from Melbourne. It began with a casual acquaint-
ance between a school-boy and a skinny young
man of about 19, who said he was a “junior
detective.” They met frequently, and the skinny
young man told huge, boundless tales about how
he traced the footprints of the assassin, and ran
down the pirate, the bushranger and the bunyip.
Finally, he offered the dazzled boy 4s. anight with
all the thrill thrown in, and the boy paid his own
fare to a lonely place, where he understood he
was to watch desperate criminals through a key-
hole and bring them to justice. But all
that happened was that he was shut up
in a lonesome house for some hours without his
clothes, and then received an appalling flogging
from the alleged detective on general principles.
Then he was shut up again, and later on he
received another flogging on the same prin-
ciples. He was also compelled to give a written
receipt for the flogging—the document acknow-
ledged that the undersigned had received 320 cuts
on various parts of the body ; also he was ordered
to deliver a parcel of eggs at a certain address,
and received 2d. and two postage stamps to pay
his fare part of the way home, being ordered to
walk the rest of the distance. The miserable youth
is in a shocking state as the result of his night’s
detecting. When the police raided the scene of
the episode they found the alleged detective,
also the receipt for the flogging, and other corro-
boration. There is an air of gruesome and
crazy economy about the whole business. The
boy paid his own fare to the place where he was
to be whacked. The assailant hadn’t a cane
ready, but went out and cut branches for the
purpose ; and he used his victim as a cheap egg-
deliverer when the affair was over. The fact that
accused is described as “a young man of good
character ” lends further support to the theory
that he was a victim of penny dreadfuls.
The first trouble that arose in Worthing
ton’s Christchurch (M.L.) career was
when three of his “students” ob-
served him leaving his front gate
about midnight. Determined
to pursue the truth, they
foxed their brilliant
teacher till he entered
the home of a lady. A
light shone in the bal-
cony-room window, and,
still intent on the pursuit
of truth, the most agile of
the party shinned up the
verandah - post. Presently
he returned with a lot of
interesting information about
black silk stockings, choice
embroidery, and other things.
His vivid description so de-
moralised the party that it
abandoned the pursuit of
truth right there, and
when it arrived home
in the morning it was
wearingcorks in its hair.
Ructions ensued; meet-
ings were held; the
papers teemed with let-
ters condemning peep-
ing Toms, prying
Josephs, etc., till one
last great meeting
burst forth with a
wildly original reso-
lution, to the effect
that it deplored the
fact that Mr. Worth-
ington’s indiscretion
should have placed
the three young men in such an unenviable posi-
tion. Then the public removed its pipe to the
other side of its face and was satisfied.
• » * •
“Index” writes concerning a recent
Bulletin paragraph:—
As an ex-teacher, I know something of N.S.W. Educa-
tion Department. Several female teachers assure me
that they are worse off, in many respects, than laundry
slaves. Not only have they to sweat in a musty class-
room all day, and prepare or correct exercises afterwards,
but their Saturdays (and often Sundays) are demanded to
give attention to this or that school “ display.” And
what does it all mean ? Simply the glorification and
aggrandisement of Minister Perry and Chief Inspector
Bridges—especially Bridges. Mr. Perry is the gentle
Minister who, during the war fever, threatened to make
it warm for any teacher running counter to his jingo
ideas in the matter of cheering the flag ; hence the
Education Dept, employees tamely submitted to un-
warrantable official levies on their slender salaries for
this or the other fund. I personally know many teachers
who could ill afford the sums wrenched out of them
during the war craze. All these amounts are
drained from what is admitted to be the hardest-
worked and poorest-paid branch of the public service.
The female teachers complain bitterly of the exactions
made on their health and pockets. Hunting up cheap
drapers to supply material for fancy hats and sashes, and
many hours’ work as seamstresses, is not calculated to
improve either the temper of the staff or the standard of
the schools. As to the children, a good number like
the displays, but many don’t. To the Commonwealth
display, at least one death, by sunstroke, is traceable.
To give healthy open-air exercise as part of the curriculum
is a good and desirable thing, but to spasmodically drag
the poor youngsters here and there, ostensibly for some
fund or alleged patriotic purpose, is unnecessary and
cruel, more particularly if one notices the disgraceful lack
of shelter and refreshment accorded to the children who
actually provide the show.

* » * *
At the outset of the Fox v. Trenwith libel
case barrister Duffy put the law of libel in a truly
beautiful light. He reduced the issue to a solemn
financial question— “Was Trenwith to be seriously
embarrassed for the greater part of his life by
winning the action, or be crushed by losing it ? ”
The audacious candour of Duffy might have moved
the foreman of the jury to ask him what he
thought of the law as a profession, but no jury-
man 'oie to the occasion.
A BREATH OF AIR.
HE (an adventurous stranger seating himself
beside her uninvited): “How balmy the
day is!”
SHE : “Are you sure it’s only the day?”
Premier John See, speaking at the opening
of an Inebriates’ retreat founded by one of the
N.S.W. churches said that “he thought the
Inebriates Act one of the best passed by his
Government.” Curious, then, that he should allow
it to remain practically a dead Act for so long !
* •* • *
One result of the Mt. Lyell (Tas.) hospital
row is that the miner contributors, disagreeing
with the view of the committee that a doctor
should not sit on a nurse’s bed, are inclined to
stop their payments to the institution ; but Mt.
Lyell Co. insists on deducting them from the
miners’ wages—an injustice which would be
illegal if there were a Truck Act in the Flyspeck.
* * * *
The Adelaide crematorium will soon be
ready for Service, and arrangements are in pro-
gress for a proper inauguration of the process of
incineration. None of the members of tne Crema-
tion Society, however, show any desire to attain
to the distinction which will attach to the first
corpse furnaced. One of the most enthusiastic
advocates of the system was Alderman Tomkin-
son, M.L.C., who pushed the Enabling Bill
through Parliament, but, though he lived until
84, he died ere he had induced Government to
grant a site for the oven.

Two cemeteries (the C. of England and
R.C.) at Lower Mittagong are allowed to exist on
the Sydney water supply catchment area ! What
does Dill-Macky say to this ? It is surely grossly
unfair to the large number of “ other ” religions
that there is no dead Wesleyan or Presbyterian
flavor in the water.

Bishop Harmer, of Adelaide, is in luck.
The cathedral towers, for the construction of
which millionaire Barr Smith gave £lO,OOO, are
almost completed, and now Mrs. Simms
(widow oh the late Brewer Simms’son) has given
several thousands to build a Lady Chapel (a
ladies’ chapel, the Register calls it) and other
accessories. For a wonder, the Anglicans, and
not the Catholics, have secured the most con-
spicuous site in Adelaide, and the new cathedral
shows up splendidly.
THE UPPER YARRA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Summer is here on flashing wings,
The joyous day is musical;
And the exultant river sings
A clear, unending madrigal.
The river is as yet so young,
And has no thought beyond to-day;
What though its waters be far flung,
Its end all sorrowful and grey ?
It weaves rose fancies maidenwise, •
The sun’s warm tenderness a part,
And dreams not that the city lies
So close, with a licentious heart.
MELB.
THOROLD DALRYMPLE.
OBLIVION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When back to our mother’s bosom
Dust unto dust we go,
But little the world shall fret us,
However the winds may blow.
All one to the silent sleepers
Is babble of glory or shame ;
And Shakespeare’s rest is no sweeter
For all his trumpeted fame.
M.J.T.
UNTRUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I kissed her red, passionate lips,
Deep, deep, I drew
Red love from her sweet nectary. . .
She was untrue !
Yea ! the love on her lips was a lie,
And I—l knew !
I kissed her more wildly to think
Her love untrue.

Part of the result of “ the triumph of the
New Testament ” in the B’African war (cable to
London papers) :
Shocking revelations have been made concerning the
trade in women and girls who are recruited from Great
Britain and the Continent by agents representing a syndi-
cate which practically controls this infamous trade. The
profits are enormous, and the victims are veritable slaves,
who are enticed by advertisements offering them good
positions in S’Africa. On landing they discover the
deception. Some escape, but the majority are ruined.
Another railway job to the credit of the
Philpocracy. A few weeks ago Railway-Commis-
sioner Thallon was asked to report on the high-
level and low-level proposed extensions from
Gladstone to Rockhampton. Thallon utterly
damned the low-level scheme, on account of its
liability to flood (just as previous Commissioners
had done), but Minister Leahy, who openly con-
fesses to an ambition to represent Rockhampton
—the town which favors the low-level scheme as
terminating close to Rockhampton—pushed the
matter through. The high-level line might have
helped Gladstone along. Fancy paying a Com-
missioner £2OOO per annum to give advice, and
then refusing to take his counsel every time !
