A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 25, 1902.
My Dear Arini—
For three days the sumptuous and haughty vice-
regal court of Sydney has been doing the magni-
ficent at Newcastle. The Cranbroolc potentate*
wife and daughter, rose at 5.30 a.m. on Friday to
get a special train to the coaly place. With them
were their suite, the Sees, a bunch of Horderns,
five gentlemen in holy orders and a couple of
politicians. The billy was boiled on the train,
and the corned-beef sandwiches eaten, and im-
mediately upon arrival, at 11 o’clock, they drove
straight to a dedicatory service at the new
cathedral. Afterwards, levees, fetes and sports.
“A wind to puff our idol-fires.” His Majesty
the King has been pleased to confer the title of
Lord Mayor upon the Mayors of Sydney and
Melbourne, and, as the D.t. has it, “ the lady
Mayoress (Mrs. Hughes) bore the good news to
her husband whilst he was on his sick bed.” Rush-
ing to the telephone to congratulate Alderman
Thomas Hughes, quite a number of people asked
for “ M’lud ! ” The Hughes are a popular
pair, and decorative withal, and their syntax
hasn’t to lean up against things ; so, if they are
not offended at the King’s little joke, no one else
should be.
“We are threatened with a revival of tight
lacing,” says a woman’s paper, but amongst a
certain class tight-lacing has never really died
out. An English trade sheet says that in some
business houses women employees are compelled
to have very small waists, and in some cases a
locked corset, which has to be worn day and
night, is made use of, the manageress keeping the
key ! A girl at North Manchester died of this
kind of corset; the impression of her ribs was
imbedded upon her liver. Aristocracy only may
afford the waist of 26in. and upwards. The shop-
girl is not genteel if hers is over 21 in. In the days
of our grandmothers 19in. was the apex of ele-
gance. At some ultra-fashionable schools the
noble damsels used to be hung up in a “neck-swing”
whilst the governesses or maids laced them. In
such a position they could offer the least muscular
resistance to haulage power. Fancy studying for
a matric. with your ribs pressing into your liver
and other places—but they had no women matrics.
then.
A fashionable Darling Point woman, who has,
all along, in the face of water famine, persistently
hosed her garden, had her house water cut off last
week just as her servants were preparing for a
dinner-party she was to give. There was con-
siderable anger and borrowing of water from the
nearest mansions. The Water and Sewerage
Board has doubled its staff of inspectors, and a
number of persons have had their tea and coffee
water cut off for having used the precious stuff on
roses and lawns. Extraordinary efforts are made
by certain garden lovers to evade the no-watering
ordinance, even to the getting up just after mid-
night to give the plants a spray.
N.S.W. National Council of Women does not
profess to compete with places of amusement, but
there is no reason why it should not enter the
list. The founders must have had some gleam of
its possibilities in this direction, for amongst the
rules is one forbidding the presence of man. At
the half-yearly meeting last week, presided over
by the ever-smiling Governoress, husbands were
seen humbly waiting on the mat outside the
Royal Society’s Hall until the flow of feminine
eloquence had subsided. Plump Miss Windeyer
took nearly an hour to tell what a charming billet
for a woman that of librarian is, but omitted all
hints as to how it might be obtained. Mrs. C.M.
Parkes discoursed of the joy of having a “dinner-
cart ” call with the family dinner, thereby giving
wives more time to devote to their husbands “and
to keep them from other companions.” Oh, the
watching these husbands want! Then a lady,
with her eye steadily on the vice-regal presence,
mentioned that she kept several “maids” but
sometimes they all left at once, and that her boys
liked stewed fruit for breakfast. The audience
refused to be thrilled by these items and got up
to leave. Lady Rawson and the placid daughter
came down from the pedestal, shook any hand
that looked as if it ought to be shaken, and passed
unattended “ out into the night.”
A good speaker, who is spending herself on
small, but perhaps, fit audiences, is Miss Susannah
Wells, 8.A., a London Quakeress at present
visiting one of the several cocoa-Quaker families
of Sydney. The Friend lecturess discourses of
George Fox, of the “inward light,” and kindred
themes, but the impression gained from her
addresses is that being a Quaker is like being
a Theosophist—one can be anything else with it.
Anti-war seems now the only definite tenet of the
creed. Miss Wells touched smilingly on the old
belief in the necessity for distinctive dress, but
the thought occurred that the lady herself would
be a much more picturesque platform figure in a
dove-grey cloak and soft round bonnet than in the
E resent ugly uniform of non-related skirt and
louse and uncompromising hat of straw.
Constance Brandon Usher, who left Sydney
with the perilous reputation of an infant musical
phenomenon, has returned after four years, a tall,
nearly-grown-up girl, with her phenomenal-
ism trained and crystallised and attested by a
trunk-full of academic musical parchment. On
Saturday night Sydneysiders went to the Cen-
tenary Hall to her “first appearance,” carrying
large quantities of expensive flowers, and quite
ready to endorse all that Herr von Heiligestadt,
of Leipzig, and other harmonious Herren had put
on sheepskin about her. Miss Usher took a Con-
servatorium prize, which for 38 years has not gone
to a British student. The presentation of an im-
posing basket of blooms was made by Mr.
Lawrence Campbell, on behalf of the ladies’ com-
mittee of welcome. Miss Usher’s first item, a
formidable concerto by Bach, roused the wonder
and admiration of her listeners for her executive
ability, then, with a Lisztian “Love Dream,” she
reached their hearts, and stopped there to the end
of the programme—and after.
Constance B.U. was dressed in pink with pink
infant roses in her very fair head. She has a
fetching platform manner full of little bobs and
smiles and a gleeful way of looking at her friends
with an air of saying: “Just wait till this is over
and we’ll have a doll’s tea-party.” Her memory
must be exceptional. She gave all her pieces
without a note of music.
A correspondent tells me that the fashion of
wearing a big ribbon or velvet bow, wide as a sash
ribbon, on the top of the head is very general in
America. I had already seen some photos, in
which the decoration was worn, and had thought
it a cap, so obvious was the bow.
Crimson Rambler rose is in splendid form just
now, rambling up 15ft. or so over balconies and
garden houses, a swarm of the small red blossoms
on every stalk. Flannel flowers and Xmas bells
are in the street wild-flower baskets. In a flower
shop I saw, last week, a quaint and charming
noose—you couldn’t call it a wreath—fashioned of
white water-lilies, their long, red-brown stalks
brought round to the flower-heads and tied with a
pink ribbon. Looked the kind of loop with which
to catch a nymph.
In a recent Sydney divorce case an application
was made that it be placed in the first batch to be
dealt with. It was “urgent,” and wanted by
wire, as the petitioner had received an offer of
marriage and desired to be free to accept it.
The committee of Hamilton (Vic.) hospital has
stopped Sunday visiting by children of the
patients. The youngsters used to take flowers,
and sometimes eggs and fruit, to the sick, then
wander all over the wards, staring and comment-
ing upon the cases. “The gentleman with the
red nose is here still, Muriel; see if you have a
drink-traot left!” “That bogie lady, with the
spotted shawl over her head, looks a lot worse,
doesn’t she, Ivy ? ” This was most exhilarating
hearing for patients who, perhaps, hadn’t got any
of the eggs or fruit.
Here is a delicious matrimonial advt. from the
Australian Star
“Pretty and Respectable French Lady (SO), speaks
and writes English, desires correspond gentleman,
bachelor (75 or more), view matrimony. Bound by vow,
she can only marry an old man (in preference a' sickly
one), that she may take care of him. Gentleman must
give references from Governor, or influential Members of
Parliament, and French Consul, attesting honourability
of self and family. Real name, age, occupation, address,
income, and photo, required. ‘Mademoiselle,’ G.P.0.,
Noumea, New Caledonia.”
“If I were King ”at the Royal: How Athol-
wood’s King Louis XI. recalls one’s childhood’s
reading of “ Quentin Durward ! ” For weeks after
reading that novel I wore images in my hat—tin
soldiers they had to be, as I couldn’t get hold of
Saints Louis, Denis or any of the other 12 apostles.
Audience, or some of it, made a great effort on the
opening night to put names to the images in
Atholwood’s hat. A man who had strong glasses
(no innuendo intended) protested that the decora-
tion was the Last Supper.
Cuyler Hastings’admirers—their eyes half out
of their sockets with fear lest Sherlock Holmes
should shadow the 15th century French poet—
awaited his Villon, and got the idealised hand-
some vagrom man, the man who “laughs while he
loves,” and writes verses as he rests in a warm
ditch, fights, wins the love of all the women, and
is constitutionally unable to put anything into the
Savings Bank. But no spook of the detective who
died the night before haunted Hastings’ Villon.
Miss Lane, somewhat nervous under the stress
of her doublet and hose, is a revelation of a
beautiful figure, not a bit too opulent. Miss
Chevalier, as Katherine, wears some of the most
beautiful clothes Sydney has ever seen. In the
first act she has a brown dress and a cloak lined
with mandarin yellow; in the second, when she
comes to the rose garden to meet her High Con-
stable lover, she wears a lettuce green and silver
brocade, in the old tapestry pattern, with chiffon
sleeves reaching to the hem of the Juliet-like
robe, which is worn over an underskirt of white
broche. Her last is a heavy white liberty satin,
embroidered with large butterflies.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent
“The discussion in Melbourne Herald as to
whether or not Mrs. Sangal should be hanged has
drawn some queer letters from the gentle sex.
Here is one:
Mrs. L. writes.—l and many a one are in favor of the
penalty of the law being carried out. Why should a
woman like her be spared ? If it wasn’t for her, those
poor children would have their father now. No, respec-
table life didn’t suit a fiend like her. If the law does not
hang that woman—well, they ought not to hang anyone.
Hang the fiend, by all means, or she will be killing some
other poor fellow. Hang her to death, the fiend she is.
This writer knows a woman who wants to have a
hand in pulling Mrs. Sangal to pieces because of
her brutality to her husband, and who quietly
read the morning paper in one room while her
husband coughed himself to death in the next.
There was not a soul handy but her to moisten
his phthisis-deadened lips. She had had a paltry
quarrel with him a few hours before.
‘‘ At the Presbyterian ‘ Festival ’ only sacred
songs were listed, and the audience woke up with
a start when Tenor Wood suddenly let loose as an
encore ‘ Oh stay with me, my darling, stay ! ’ The
black-coats on the platform were worth watching
during the interlude. They neither smirked in a
treacly way, nor self-consciously ‘perused the
matting ’ as Methodists might have done, but one
and all threw their heads far back and glared
sternly at the roof like hens drinking. Maybe
the canny ancients assumed this reverential atti-
tude as a sort of blind to bluff the audience into
interpreting the ‘ darling ’ referred to in the song
as a Scriptural metaphor, as the Kirk has hitherto
done with regard to the ‘ Song of Solomon.’ Prof.
Harper, in his new book about the ‘Song,’owns
up that it’s a secular love story, while he lets it
down gently as depicting ‘ human affection in the
purest, highest form ’ possible at that period.
‘ ‘ Mrs. Hickman Molesworth—wife of the rosy-
cheeked Judge—appealed to lovely woman through
the columns of the Argus, just before the Cup,
on the subject of economy in clothes, in con-
sideration of the dreadful drought conditions,
which made the sporting of fine feathers and
showy plumage among the fashionable a callous
proceeding. The letter read very well, but it
failed of its humanitarian object. Lovely woman
turned out in the loftiest style that she could get
draper’s credit for, and—saw herself eclipsed in
magnificence of lace-layers and chiffon-tiers by
that self-same philanthropist. Mrs. Molesworth
had advised her sisters to turn out to the Cup in
cotton frocks, and carried wealth of silk and
Solomon’s glory herself!
“The latest version of the female figure admits
a waist. You have to be right through the stages
of no corset, straight corset, pouched blouses, and
roomy, etc., etc., before the ‘ Gibson ’ shoulders
and the waist can be attempted. The idea is to
dig out the meridian, from elevations about the
hips and the drop-chest. One girl at some
fashionable sports, the other day—scored vvith
this —the latest vagary. Her waist gave the idea
of a hollow between the hip-hills. The jelly-bag
sleeves fell into line with the expansive parts and
threw the hollow more than ever into relief.
“Dolores’ dressing! How it pains her ad-
mirers ! At the Wednesday afternoon recital she
came out in a garish evening gown of gold brocade
of the brightest design, which made a sartorial
suicide with deadly doses of pink and blue shades.
A Melbourne globe-trotter from the Big Smoke
writes alarmingly of Australian artists there :
“ There’s the case of Miss , who landed here full of
hope, vocal strength, and letters to the big guns of the
great world. I hear, on good authority, that ‘ paper ’
filled all her little series of concerts. More recently I
was spending some time in a village in North Wales,
where I saw in a band of strolling singers Miss Y ,
who came Londonwards to make her fortune, and now
lives, like several others from the wallaby haunts, by
sending round the hat. Yet she has a good appearance
and a lovely voice, far too lovely to be reckoned among
the nomadic open-air sands singers. When will it be
brought home to the Australian artist that a creditable
platform name and a small income in Australia are pre-
ferable to oblivion and starvation in the Big Smoke—the
most merciless vampire in creation ?
“A Melbourne woman last week invented a
novel mode of committing suicide. She dressed
herself in her best, took all her jewellery in a
handbag, walked out on to the footpath, and
waited quietly for a lorry to come along. Just as
the vehicle was passing she jumped underneath
the wheels. The driver says he felt a jolt, and,
on looking round, saw the woman lying in his
tracks, crushed and bleeding. At the inquest it
turned out that she had been strange in her
manner for some time past. On the morning of
her death she went into the room where her
husband was lying on the bed, and struck him
over the head with a club. Under examination
the husband was asked whether she had ever
struck him before. He turned to the coroner and
remarked, bluntly, ‘We won’t go into that. The
poor old woman is dead and gone.’
“Australians were always yearning to hear
Melba ‘ in the prime of her power and the flower
of her fame.’ The wish was granted. The diva
came, and did her best to please as a singer, while
outside her Art she showed herself open-handed
and tender-hearted. So far as Melba herself
is concerned, Australia has no claim to a
legitimate grievance. But Human Nature is
queer stuff! At present, the crowd raves
about Dolores, lionizes her, as it never did
before, though not for lack of opportunity.
‘Why this sudden enthusiasm?’ scribe asked a
newly-converted Dolores devotee. ‘lt will do
Melba good to see that she hasn’t got all Austra-
lia by the wool,’ was the reply. In the same
spirit, people who have not heard Melba because
they would not, or could not, afford her prices,
now maintain that Dolores is the superior singer,
and downface people who, having conscientiously
sampled both artists, have taken, at least, the first
step towards qualifying themselves to make com-
parisons. The catch-word of these one-eared
critics is ‘Melba is cold, so different from
Dolores. ’ As a matter of fact, neither of these
richly-gifted women is a great emotional artist.
“Lord Dick Nevill is Melba’s social referee and
time-keeper. He heads V.R. or unofficial bevies
at all the shows the jolly Star patronises: At the
Opera-remnants’ rehearsal, the other day, the
Aide, who looks like a long, thin bottle of vinegar
when the official smile is not doing duty, headed
an excitable contingent of Melba’s women friends,
little Miss Stonington Clarke among them. Dur-
ing most of the rehearsal Melba was accompanied
by a gamp, which she drummed on the stage
whenever Slapoffski or his underlings ran amok.
Slap, seemed,to be revelling in real music again
after the foolish pantomimic penny whistlings,
and to feel the pride of batoning to Melba.
“Melba’s visit to the Maternity Home in the
Holy City had its exciting results. Just after the
departure of the diva an inmate gave birth to a
daughter.. The proud mother at once greeted
her offspring as Melba, and the news was wired to
the Illustrious One. She immediately ordered a
wardrobe of long clothes and complete parapher-
nalia for the infant, and probably thanked
Heaven it wasn’t twins.
“ Mrs. Charlie Ryan, at the C. of E. Grammar
School Sports on Friday, welcomed Melba and
Miss Mignonette Clarke. It was a fresh role for
the capsrish diva, patting freckle-faced boys on
the cheeks and pointing out the lolly-stick legs of
the runners. Melba winks pronouncedly ; the
world of fashion grins delightedly, but daren’t
follow suit. Her dress for the sports was a white
silk, with a white chiffon over-dress, splashed with
roses.
“ Mr. Pince-Nez Tyson had the most fluffy box
party of young fledglings at Melba’s Opera Night.
It is a refreshing sight to see the seasoned bachelor
wearing his thoughtful glasses amid a fluttering
brood of debutantes. Among his charges this
night was a clever young heiress whose fortune
springs from a great ‘ run ’ in the woolliest quarter
of the Western District. A dozen drought
seasons wouldn’t affect her income appreciably.
“At the Philharmonic concert, 17th Nov.,
Dolores was the great attraction. Emilie Kef-
ford, a young contralto singer, showed the
makings of a good voice. She should shine in
oratorio, if heredity goes for anything, seeing that
her grandfather is ‘the Messiah of Nunawading,’
a much-talked-of patriarch, prominent in the
Faith-healing line of business.
“The tennis-girl had her innings all last week.
She is, without doubt, the chirpiest, prettiest
sport follower we can boast this side. Her style
is distinctive. She is thin, almost meagre, with a
flattish chest, and the daintiest chiffon-furnishings
to be had. Her complexion is that of the lily, just
healthily and artistically tinted in the proper
places. Her hat is her crowning glory, roses
under the brim, a softening lace edge above, a
guileless tarantula bow of velvet on the retreating
crown. She was repeated to a monotonous
extent during the Tournament. On all sides
the same crisp, clean, pearl-neckleted, dainty, in-
souciant siren.
“ Of the Court champions, none had the dash
and the takingness of Miss Waters who topped a
clean-cut rig out with a Panama, piped and
banded with russet-brown silk—the sort of thing
to win a wandering eye. A French girl ran away
with all the traditions of Gallic taste in her re-
trenched skirt, archaic blouse, and a pilot’s cap.
She was spare of form, but volleyed and served at
top express speed. A weather-beaten South
Australian, whose deeply-tanned skin told of many
a long summer in the Courts, racketted like a man.
“ The Tennysons did the tennis contest on
Friday, the G.G. in a small ‘boxer’ that looked
like a blister covered with court-plaster on his
head. Her Ex. and the three small Tennyson
editions in calf binding looked in at the C. of E.
sports, where she gave out the prizes, a hand-
shake, and a smile. She wore blue—quite a
sprightly blue—and one of her bleak-looking hats
owning sparse plots of strange-hued poppies.
“ Society rallied in force to the final ‘Rippon
Lea’ concert on Tuesday. The most notable frocker
was a large lady, whose expansive chest, well visible
through an open-air yoke, showed the turkey-
egg speckle known as heat-rash. No one cuts
such a extra-pretty figure at concerts nowadays as
Mr. Charles Rose. It is his turn to be taken up,
now that the tenor with the chestnut name finds
that ‘the winds sits in the shoulder of his sail.’
At the Sargood concert Rose gave his languishing
manners full play as he tootled his serenade. But
it is a tactical error for a male singer with a sweet,
well-educated voice to ogle an audience of women
and emphasise tender passages with facial play.
Apollo needs to be —well, certainly Apollo, before
it is wise for him to coquet before the eyes of a
petticoated assemblage. An audience of one is
quite enough to flirt with in song, or in anything
else.
“The wisdom of girls striking out for them-
selves is again shown by a recent wealthy mar-
riage. The average girl has a far better marriage
chance by starting a millinery, massage, or petti-
coat business, and prospering thereat, than by
‘ keeping up appearances ’ at home, and suffering
all the pangs of envy, covetousness, and vanity.
The girls who have gone into business are landing
all the big fish lately.
“ At the ‘ Mikado ’ first night, a bride caused a
sensation in the circle. She was large and florid,
and had encased her ample form in a scaly
sequin gown cut dla Polonaise. From a distance
her upper figure resembled an illuminated globe.
Florence Young, in her Katisha role, goes the
whole length of an advanced and blue-mouldy
Chinese widow’s make-up. She doesn’t even save
her big, white, robust molars from the sacrifice.
They are blackened in the traditional manner, so
that when she extends the dimples into a smile
the frightful peanut-face merely shows a large,
dark chasm where the mouth should be.
“ The biggest masculine figure duriifg the race
season in these parts was the Brandy King who
won big wagers so often. He did more—he ‘ laid-
off,’ with considerable disadvantage to himself in
many instances, so that several fair flutterers are
basking in Cup affluence just now, and the gentle-
man with the full-flavored Hibernian bouquet to his
name was the petted darling of the season.
“ A nice point of honor was discussed heatedly
at a swagger hostelry on Cup eve with the
result that much gore stained the polished oak
floor of the dining-room, and one fine Adonis is
laid up for repairs in Melbourne’s most expensive
private court-plastery.
“ The Westralian bachelor has a high market
value these times. The distant hill is always
green, and the flutterers of this under-manned
State reckon every man from the West a magnate
and a Midas. The Western bachelor is conjured
up as a scion of a noble family, who, having sown
his wild oats and then his wild cats, and reaped
the reward of manly enterprise, now stalks the
land, rich, and looking for a wife. But, alas, often
he isn’t rich, and often he is dodging a wife.
“ The pony cart with its bell chimes is becoming
a pest. The members of the Waxellency’s family
were first on the road with a tinkling pony cart.
Other smart girls followed suit, and now one’s
quiet walk through Toorak, St. Kilda, or Malvern
is broken in upon by frequent bells. It’s a nasty
little nursery jingle, and, after awhile, gets on the
nerves.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
It is considered probable that one of the
Kenniffs will confess before the fatal day arrives.
Dr. Mitchell, father of book-lover Mitchell
of the present day, was, while holding an official
position under N.S.W. Government, censured for
a point-blank refusal to superintend the flogging
of convicts at Hyde Park barracks
J.P.-ism in N.S.W. was thus reflected on
by one-time Premier Parkes : “ When in office, I
never made myself a J.P. ; when out of office, I
never had a good enough character in the eyes of
the Government to fit me for the position.”
History repeats itself, but it is often a
long time about it. The last Persian ambassador
left Athens 2393 years ago to report to Darius
Hystaspes that war was inevitable. A last week’s
cable tells that his successor has just been
appointed.
Vic. Supreme Court prize (£4O) at the
recent final examination for solicitors went to
William Ah Ket, an Australian-born full blood
Chinese. Ah Ket will be Victoria’s second
Chinese solicitor, the other being F. I. V. Ni Gan,
of Bendigo.
It is a notable coincidence that E. W.
Cole, of Book Arcade fame, the late George
Robertson, and the late Samuel Mullen, leading
Victorian booksellers, allfirst landed in Melbourne
on 12th Nov., 1852, the first mentioned sailing
from Capetown, and the other two from Liverpool.
The Commonwealth makes gubernatorial
history fast —two Governors-General in less than
two years. At the start the G. -G. was an almost
Sacred Personage ; now, he is a comfortable, com-
monplace, inexperienced anybody. By and bye,
when Australia gets sense, he will have as pay
£l5OO a year at most, and possibly a free bunga-
low.
Sec. J. R. Martin, of Sydney Harbor
Trust, resigns office “on account of ill-health.”
Students of “ form ” tip him for a Federal prize
when he gets better. Mr. Martin was Minister
Lyne’s right-hand man when that cute gentleman
was N.S.W. Premier, and perhaps Bill is feeling
the need of a right-hand man now in Federal
politics.
Kirton, one of the honorary Ministers in
Irvine’s crew, once got off this fine figure of
speech when supporting the McShiels-Lean Govt.:
“The Opposition has got the ear of the con-
stituencies and is endeavouring to throw dust in
their eyes.” Kirton (erstwhile a postman in
Ballarat) belongs to the worst type of A.N.A.
platitudinarians.
NOT HIS FAUE
“ If I am a gaol-bird it’s not my fault I can’t fly."
An incident which has caused much talk
is the “pilling” of a certain senator by the
members of a very fashionable and haughty
Victorian club. That senator’s colleague (on the
same side of politics) is a member of the club in
question, and the world has never heard a breath
against the rejected candidate, one of Australia’s
foremost lawyers.
Ex-Premier Peacock’s hobby is photo.-
collecting; his house at Creswick is crammed with
autographed pictures of more or less important
people. The fifteen-shillings-a-dozen portrait that
little “York” gave him figures cheek by jowl
with that of an up-country municipal light who
would be justified in feeling very much annoyed
at the juxtaposition.
With the Pacific cable comes, in a sense,
the realisation of his idea to the veteran Mr.
Audley Coote, who, for three decades almost, has
agitated for some such project. “Cable CoOte”
started on the track of a Pacific line about 1873,
and, though others claim the credit of having
brought the plan to fruition, his, at any rate, is
the merit of its origination.
Hughes, M. H.R., than whom there is not
a better speaker in the whole Federal Parliament,
is just now manoeuvring Sydney Wharf-Laborers
case through N.S.W. Arbitration Court. Mr.
Hughes is in active training for the final exam,
for the Bar, which explains why he has not been
quite so prominent in the Federal Parliament de-
bates last session as was expected.
Sydney's “Lord Mayor,” Tom Hughes, is
just now prostrated with nervous depression. It
is necessary to explain that his lordship fell ill
before the news came that Sydney Mayors are in
future to be temporary Lords, and that his lord-
ship’s illnesss is not, therefore, due to thinking
over some of the probable Lord Mayors and Lady
Mayoresses of the immediate future.
“The Students of Truth” in Melbourne
have disbanded, and the furniture of their rooms
is to be sold for the benefit of Mrs. Worthington.
Worthington’s library is being preserved for him
by a faitliful student; but it is said that the fallen
apostle, who was threatened with eye-trouble
before his trial, is now rapidly losing the sight of
one eye, and is feeling gaol life keenly.
During its 66 years of existence S.A. has
only given the affix of J.P. to two clergyman, and
both were Congregationalists. One was W. R.
Newland (father of ex-Treasurer Newland, the
author of “having the Way”), and the other S.
L. Both lived in out-of-the-way country
towns, ancl they fined “drunks” with so much
discretion that no one ever had nything unkind
to say concerning them.
Despite all the public enthusiasm, Scotch-
byterianism looks askance at the play-acting
Melba. The annual report of the Ladies’ College
submitted to Vie. Presbyterian Assembly con-
tained no reference whatever to the return of the
diva. Also the report was adopted by the
Assembly without a single member drawing
attention to the omission or putting in a word
about the College’s famous pupil.
Frederic Dudley North, C.M.G.’d on
King’s birthday, is a brother-in-law of big John
Forrest. North is a curly-headed, florid-faced
Britisher, who came out to Westralia and joined
the civil service. He was a very ordinary clerk
when he married Forrest’s sister-in-law, but then
Big John took him in hand, and he was pushed
into the position of clerk to the Executive, and
afterwards into that of Under Secretary.
Sydney University mathematical professor,
Gurney, retires on a pension. It can at least be
said to the credit of the Scotch clique who run the
institution that their financial administration is
admirable in its details. The professors are paid
only a part of their salaries: the rest is devoted
to the purchase of annuities. Resignation at a
certain age is compulsory, and the learned man’s
declining days are cheered by a substantial
pension.
Treasurer Butler (S.A.) will be 52 on
Wednesday (Dec. 3). Senator Zeal will be 72 on
the following Friday, on which day John Wats-
ford (born at Parramatta), the oldest Methodist
minister still in active work in the Commonwealth,
will be 82. Also, “Jack” Creswell, sec. of the
S.A. cricketing association, agricultural society,
chamber of commerce, and half-a-score other
things—the most energetic personage in Adelaide
—will be 44, Dec. 8.
J. L. Parsons, M.L.C., brother-in-law of
S.A.’s boss squatter and millionaire J. H. Angas,
has been chief promoter of the iniquitous Cen-
tralian land-grant railway scheme. Parsons was
originally a Bible Christian cleric, then went into
business and Parliament, and became N.T.
Government Resident. He is an orator of the
impractical flowery type, and it is generally
believed that he will go to England with Premier
Jenkins to exploit the scheme.
Speaker Holder is comparatively poor and
has a large family (about a dozen). This was
what mainly induced him to decline the offer of a
commission to represent the Commonwealth at
Delhi Durbar. Besides, he would rather preach
the sermons at the anniversary of some little
Methodist chapel than invest in a Windsor uni-
form to swell round at a gaudy function, for he
neither smokes, drinks, nor relates “funny”
stories, and his tastes generally are of the very
simplest.
Francis Fox, the Age reporter who has been
suing ex-Railway-Minister Trenwith for libel,
based on remarks about the alleged unwholesome
appearance of his bald head and other matters,
achieved a remarkable distinction in the course of
the case. Premier Irvine and ex-Premier Peacock
both got into the box to express the opinion that
Fox, in his journalistic capacity, gave unfair and
misleading reports. As these reports appeared in
the Age as its political views, David Syme may
consider his paper widely disrespected by both
parties.
J. E. Mackey, one of the new members
for Gippsland in Vic. Assembly, was originally a
compositor. He has studied hard, brilliantly
gassed all sorts of law examinations, and made
imself the best local authority on synthetic
philosophy. He is saturated with Spencerisms,
but that has not been able to save him from
“ running cunning ” in politics. For years Mackey
was an ardent land-taxer and as such stood for a
city constituency. Now that he sits for a
country district he denounces the land tax as in-
expedient.
Peters, the 19-year-old individual accused
of inflicting a hideously cruel flogging on a small
boy whom he enticed to his house for the express
purpose, is a very ordinary-looking person, whose
only distinctive feature is a pair of those peculiar
blue-grey eyes that seem to gaze rather than look.
He wept copiously during the police-court pro-
ceedings, and disclaimed any recollection of the
matter. At present he is being medically observed
in the gaol hospital—which he seems to be much
in need of. Mel Dour ne has at times provided
some queer sensations and criminals, but she will
take long to raise a more abnormal one than this.
Phrase-making is quite a lost art in Vic.
Parliament since Deakin and Hancock are no
longer about, Treasurer Shiels being unable to
give the requisite air of spontaniety to his
written-down impromptus. Perhaps the neatest
phrase ever let off in the stolid House was
Deakin’s retort when Patterson chaffed him
about talking frivolously. Quoth Alfred : “ When
I see a bladder of air painted like a bomb, I don’t
get an axe to smash it. I prick it with my
finger ” —and he darted a long middle digit in the
direction of the butcher-politician. It was Han-
cock who described a Govt.’s policy as “one of
laissez faire, but more lazy than fair ”; and when
Murray Smith rose on one occasion to discourse
on Freetrade, exclaimed: “ Now for the two
great authorities, Murray and Adam Smith ! ”

“ Come and have a drink, old fellow! ”
"No, thanksh, ole shap, jusht had one!"
Richard H. Harnett, of Chatswood, near
Sydney, lately died, at 82. The Harnetts of Cork
were a strong clan over 100 years ago, and Richard
carried the family vigor with him to Australia.
He started Mosman ; built the first ferry-boats
for local service; set up a line of ’busses; was
crack yachtsman and enterprising pastoralist; and
leaves behind him a widow and 12 children, with
“ Larry,” his picturesque kinsman, the perennial
ornament of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly.
Donald Mackinnon, Yictorian M.L.A.,
is that rare bird —a radical squatter.. He owns,
with his brothers, a fine Western district. (Vic.)
station, and (a doubtful possession these times) a
station in Queensland. But he had the good for-
tune, while studying for the Bar and during his
early barristerial years, to be under the tutelage
of George Higinbotham, a fact which accountsfor
the absence in him of the average squatter's
Toryism. He refused to join in the Kyabrama-
pootra cackle, and he and Mr. Andrews are now
the most awkward of the Irvine Government’s
opponents.
The State Govt. (S.A.) has again failed to
get a knighthood for Millionaire Angas, who is
now within a year or two of 80, and who is but
slowly recovering from a very severe illness. Mr.
Angas has liberally helped almost every Ade-
laide educational and philanthropic institution
worthy of assistance, while his private benefac-
tions —concerning which nothing is ever heard
publicly—are continuous and extensive. But he
is not popular. Even his charities are conducted
in a businesslike way, and he never gives until he
has thoroughly inquired into the merits of a case
of hardship.
English paper item :
General Booth left London on Saturday morning, for a
five months’ tour through the United States and Canada.
A large crowd of sympathisers assembled at the station to
bid good-bye to the leader. The General, who was
attired in a regulation Salvation Army uniform under a
long, heavily-braided overcoat, seemed in the best of
spirits. He discarded the familiar Army peaked cap for
a glossy silk hat, which he repeatedly raised in acknow-
ledgment of the salutes of his friends.
Thus does the dry-rot of affluence eat away , the
props of all successful new religions. The insi-
dious bell-topper disease has crept into the Army,
and the General wears braid, or catskin, or such-
like carnal gauds on his coat. And the bell-topper
religion is Lost.
In “ A Research on the Eucalypts, espe-
cially in regard to their essential oils,” Messrs.
Baker (curator) and Smith (sub-curator), of the
Sydney Technological Museum, have produced a
work embodying the results of immense labor,
and promising to be of great scientific and
economic value. Nearly 500 distillations were
made from about 40 tons of leaves obtained from
110 distinct species of eucalypti, and the book
(300 royal-quarto pages, liberally illustrated) con-
tains a vast amount of collateral botanical infor-
mation.
“Yolt” : On various electric-tram sys-
tems :
Re “ Galvo,” B. 25/10/’O2. In Kalgoorlie the track is
laid very solid ; 921 b. rails on jarrah sleepers ; and the
overhead work is of the very latest style. The “ special
work” mentioned by “Galvo” in describing the Auck-
land system may also be seen at the intersection of
Hannan’s and Mari tana sts., Kalgoorlie. Two tracks
intersect at right angles, and have curves in all four
directions. This complicated piece of work was put
down in about 10 days. The overhead trolley-wires,
feeder-cables, electric-light cables, and telephone wires at
this point form quite a shade about midday.
Yeteran Dr. Bernstein, the benevolent
Lismore (N.S.W.) citizen who, during the
recent S’African war, was howled at as a “pro-
Boer,” and who was even subjected to an official
inquiry for his reprehensible “disloyalty,” has
just been presented by the Lismore people (now
returned to sense) with a handsome testimonial
and a public address bearing the signatures of
many of his former enemies.
It is remarkable, a recent writer points
out, that in the many obituaries of Zola, “no
mention is made of his constant fear that death
would come to him suddenly. All his life he was
haunted by a constant dread of heart-failure; when-
ever he began a book he was convinced that he (
would never live to finish it, and when he made a
speech that he would fall dead before completing
the sentence. He was always watching for bad
omens, and would not enter a cab or a shop unless
the number was a multiple of three or seven. He
confessed that on retiring at night he opened his
eyes seven times, never more nor less, to satisfy
himself that he was not dying.”
Mr. Law, who has imported a little ex-
citement into N.S.W. politics by resigning from
the Labor party, and consequently from the
Assembly (to which he seeks election again as an
“independent”), has long been uncomfortable as
a solidarity member. When, some years ago, he
joined an Orange Lodge, and began to take an
active part in its proceedings, this caused dissatis-
faction, it not being considered desirable that a
Labor member should be mixed up with the sec-
tarian row. Since then Labor has been pulling
Mr. Law one way and sectarianism another;
and he finally goes clean out of the Labor camp.
His chance of re-election is pretty good, for his
electorate, Balmain South, is very Dill-Mackyish ;
but the Labor party will do all it can to.beat him
in the interests of discipline.

only to have beasts like that thrown at you ?
NEARLY.
POLITE DRIVER OF HEARSE (to hot lady walking
to railway-station four miles off): “ Better
take a lift, mum—you look near dead.”
Though the smaller Gippsland farmer has
been ready enough to help his distressed Mallee
brother, the rich squatters and graziers of that fat)
and profitable district have sheered off with re-,
markable unanimity. Some of them have been
simply coining money by taking advantage of the
meat market, and averaging in the neighborhood
of £lB a head for cattle at the rate of anything up
to 100 a week, and others have let portions of
their broad acres for grazing at phenomenal rentals.
Several of the big Gippslanders could easily spare
£lOOO each out of the last six months’ profits, but
so far only two of them figure in the donation list
for trifles. And the Western district, which has
also been raking in money hand-over-fist, has
behaved quite as meanly. It is the hated
metropolis, vilified by; every little shire council,
and set upon by organisations of country members
in Parliament, that has at all times to stand Sam.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — ln the ease of ethnological and
natural history items , etc., always give date and
place of experience ;or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value
Scene : Bookuokookera Court-house in the
remote back-blocks. District Court Judge (address-
ing jury): “ You can find the prisoner guilty of
theft; or if you consider the evidence that he stole
not conclusive, but are satisfied that the stolen goods
were found in his possession, you may find him
guilty of receiving stolen property. For instance,
a woman charged with infanticide may not be
found guilty of child murder, but the jury may
find her guilty of concealment of birth.” The
jury retires and deliberates for two hours. Then
it re appears, and the prisoner, a hulking, un-
shaven man of list., is replaced in the dock.
Foreman of jury (addressing the Judge): “We
find the prisoner guilty of concealment of birth,
yer ’Onner.” Then the jackass on the tree out-
side Bookookookera Court-house opened his bill
and laughed for the space of three days.
♦ * * »
Told in the Adelaide papers by one of the
survivors of a Licensed Victuallers’ picnic that
“a snake sft. Din. long, at Happy valley, was
seen to stand up on the tip of its tail in front of
the horses in a most threatening manner ; but the
driver killed it with his whip.” Ah, these are
dull days ! In the old brisk times the snakes that
yon saw coming home from a publicans’ picnic
didn’t simply stand on their tails waiting to be
killed. They had wings and flew at you, and with
their fins they swam at you, and when you woke
up a day or two afterwards you still felt the fire
and brimstone they had breathed into you.

The camel is just about as sacred to the Afghan
driver as a bullock is to the average bull-puncher.
Also, it’s only the male camel (when in season)
that is fierce, and, even then, he’s a lot more
likely to attack the inoffensive stranger, who

cruelly abused him. It’s common here (Westra-

on.
“A.C.”: Re hybrids :
Have been rabbiting for nearly 20 years and have
caught hundreds of the hare-rabbit cross. They were a,
little bigger than the ordinary rabbit and with the hare’s
brown-red fur had the rabbit’s white bob-tail. I have
also caught the cat-rabbit hybrid, but it was very young
and I failed to rear it from lack of proper food. About
eight years ago the keeper of a boarding-house in North
Deniliquin had an undoubted hybrid of this kind. It had
the rabbit’s head and white short tail, with the cat’s fur,
and had a rolling walk or wobble very unlike the stealthy
movements of the cat. It was omnivorous, and I have
seen it eat meat and grass in one day. Three hybrids [of
what kind?] were presented to the Melbourne Zoo about
seven years ago.
The above description is quite valueless. The
teeth and claws would have been the important
parts to investigate.

Re Mallee (Vic.) drought, a business
resident:—
Have just been through the Mallee, from Warrackna-
beal to Hopetoun and Rainbow. Should say the whole
district will average three bushels —some more, some
nothing. One farmer near Hopetoun has 3000 acres pro-
mising three bags an acre. Old wheat is now being sent
from Beulah ; 14,000 bags stacked at Rosebery. Mallee
fanners require from 30 to 401 b. of wheat an acre for seed.
Wimmera district is worse than the Mallee.

“Gibo": Re “Dargin’s grey" (B.
15/11/’O2), “Ajax” errs in stating that the horse
has thrown every self-styled champion rider that
mounted him, with the exception of “ The
Breaker.” He was successfully ridden by W*
Wright at Sunny Corner Exhibition Sports, in
’95, and again at Bathurst Show in ’96, when the
rider was presented with £8 by Lord Hampden,
who said the latter was the best man he’d
ever seen in the saddle. Also, the famous buck-
jumper has since—in his decadence —been success-
fully ridden by Tom Sloan, for a side wager, at
Bathurst Show, ’9B, and by Harry Mantle at
Lithgow, ’99—the latter with a hold.
“Quirinch”: Be the buckjumper “Dargin’s
Grey, (8/11/’O2). Not long ago the horse was
travelling with Martini’s show, and at Gunnedah
(N.S.W.) was ridden twice by aboriginal Abel
Woods, of Gunnible station, and never moved
him in the saddle. The terror was also ridden by
one “ Combo Jack ” (connected with the show)
but, owing either to Dargin’s temperament or
“ Combo’s ” foresight, the horse, in this case,
would do nothing more than “root.” [No more
Dargin’s Grey.—Ed. B.].

“Alone”: Not generally known that
bunny is a oapital swimmer. Have had dogs that
could catch him ashore, but as soon as he took to
the river the dogs had no chance. Head and
shoulders well out of water, Brer Rabbit swims
straight and fast. [So perhaps in Australia he
picks up this swimming accomplishment too.]
“Alex. M.” : I have frequently (in
Britain) observed the hare and the rabbit swim,
and found both poor performers, because
they trim “down-by-the-head,” the nos-
trils being barely above water, while the
rump is raised absurdly high and the hindlegs
waste their force on the suface, like the screw of
an over-light steamer. It would be a poor dog
that couldn’t overhaul either of them in the
water.

“Buttle”: Re Judge Docker’s dog-de-
cision (B. 8/11/’01). The very worst dog to sool
on to horses is a eattle-dog who is a good heeler,
and a horse knows this quite well. A sheep-dog
or a mongrel will only rush up and bark, and a
horse simply presents his tail and his compli-
ments and shakes hands with the dog if he is not
a bit fly.
Things are in a strangely ab-
original state at White Cliffs (N.H.
Wales). The lockup contains only
two cells, in one of which a femals
prisoner is working out her sen-
tence, while a dangerous lunatid
howls in the other. A wife-beater,
under remand, camps in the open
yard, and a policeman has to sit up
at night and watch him. A cranky
Salvationist, who wore an inverted
beer-pot design on his jersey by way
of showing that he had given up
drink, is in custody at an hotel on a
charge of attempting suicide, with
another weary officer looking after
him. Drunks and disorderlies,
unless they are violent to the verge
of homicide, aren’t arrested now,
but only summoned. Timber is
scarce at White Cliffs, and there
isn’t enough of it to tie prisoners
to, and, besides, the police would
have to go round and pour water
over them in the heat of the day.

“ Alone ” : Let our Aus-
tralian Pasteurs and Kochs con-
sider the little busy flea, how he
lives, and how he dies. It may
be well worth while, for it looks
as if the little tormentors are sub-
ject to some disease that occasion-
ally clears them right out of the
whole town or district. Avenel
(Vic.), for instance, was, a few
years ago, simply swarming with
them. They bred in millions in
every sand-patch about town, and
every house was wondrously in-
fested. They abounded even in
the churches and thoroughly en-
joyed their Sunday’s carouse upon
the worshippers. Quite suddenly,
however, the pest died right out,
and since then a flea in Avenel
has been a rare bird indeed. An occasional speci-
men is discovered on the local dog when he re-
turns from an excursion to a flea-infested locality ;
but they never make any headway.
* • • *
“Frank S.” re milking-machines:—
Your correspondent (B. 15/11/’O2), who states that the
milking-machines are proving a costly failure to the Vic.
dairyman, is right, as far as he goes. The machine
alluded to is a Scotch-Yankee invention, which draws off
the milk by an alternating vacuum pressure, the pulsa-
tion being managed by a pretty mechanism that lets a
little air in once a second. The defect of the machine is
that it has but one tube for both vacuum-pressure and
milk, and they get badly mixed when the cow is in full
milk. There is another machine- a Vic. invention—
which gets the milk away, but it is slow and awkward,
and has other drawbacks. But dairymen feel that the
milking-machine is bound tocome all right— like the shear-
ing-machine ; and some practical dairymen are putting
their wits to work to overcome the present defects. To-
day I saw an improved machine, just patented by a Terang
(Vic.) farmer, which looks like a big step forward. It has
a double tube, one for the milk, the other for the vacuum
pressure on the teat. The teat cups are double, so that
the vacuum does not, as in the Scotch-Yankee machine,
act direct on the teat and hurt the cow. The new
machine did its work well, and, best point of all, the
cows seemed to like it. Milk-machine inventors should
always bear in mind that the key to the successful
machine is the fact that the cow has the power to let
down or retain her milk at pleasure. If she makes up
her mind not to let it down no machine known will get it
from hen She can’t keep the milk up for ever, but she
can hold it for over an hour, and that’s long enough to
burst up the most beautiful machine. She started doing
this with the S.-Y. machine ; hence the trouble. One of
the S.-Y. inventors is on his way out here to investigate
the trouble ; and the chances are that he will arrive in
nice time to congratulate a smart Victorian on having
fixed it up.

“Is that young man gone,
Mary?"

covered with white ashes, and with hands slightly burnt.

track of a root that had burned for about 30ft. from the
stump, and had left about 3in. of burnt soil over the
place where it had been. I had run right along to where
the stump was still burning, about 3ft. down.

23 HUNTER STREET.
That tremendous storm, a week back, when
iuost of the top-dressing of the Great Australian
desert was blown over Victoria, brought to pass
some curious effects. Besides the showers of mud,
brought down by the subsequent rain, the trains
On Swan Hill line were blocked by accumulations
of sand—in some places a foot deep. At Ultima,
one of Bent’s “ over-manned and under-worked ”
engines broke down through being choked with
dust; while, at Charlton, a baby of three was
blown off a verandah, and was only found next
morning, four miles away. It is all very well to
talk about placing the people on the land, but
when the land thus comes and places itself on the
people, it makes a difference.

“ Bobrid ” : Be “Boko’s” comparison
(8., 15/11/’O2) of the fruit-hawker with his sore-
backed moke and the station-owner with his
starving sheep. Fora “sprat” the “poor devil
of a fruit-hawker ” can fix up his horse, and if he
doesn’t, he is a callous wretch, deserving of
punishment. The station-owner has starving
sheep, but is prepared to spend any sum in reason
in fodder to keep them alive ; and if his money runs
out, he has done his best and is a victim of circum-
stances. “Over-stocking” is the parrot-cry of
the man who doesn’t know. Wouldn’t the grazier
be a fool not to stock his holding to its full
carrying capacity ? True, a drought may make
that over-stocking, but he can’t predict a drought.
If he could, there’d be no over-stocking.

The Bulletin has received from Johan-
nesburg a photo, of a mule “ with foal at foot.”
Writes the sender:—
They came from Bloemfontein Remount Station, and
have been presented to the Zoological Gardens in Pre-
toria. The dam is about 15J hands in height, and grey
in color, and the •foal (female) is a sturdy youngster of
about eight months old. It will be interesting to know
whether this foal m turn will breed. There are on record
but fcWo other cafes of mules breeding, one of which
occurred in Australia. The dam in that instance was
from a mare that had had several mule foals, but had, in
this case, bred to a horse, evidently, however, “ throwing
back,” with the result that the foal was a mule to all
appearances.

“Jungle”: The closed-road curse is
making itself keenly felt in Victoria. South of
the Dividing Range, where “starvers” ate being
travelled in thousands, the big landholder is
mostly boss of the lanes. One estate alone com-
prises 800 acres of closed roads, the grass on
which, and on miles of other appropriated roads
and reserves, could be mown. A Wimmera man,
who is paying 2s. fid. a head per week for 40
horses, says there is feed enough thus locked up to
last all the mallee stock for months.

The latest discovery re cane cultivation in
Queensland. A Burdekin sugar-grower, who was
also a sheep-owner, turned a number of his
starving sheep into the young cane to keep them
alive. The sheep went through and through the
stuff, never touching the cane or shoots at all. On
subsequent examination, however, the owner
found that the juinbucks had eaten every weed
out of the young stuff, and left it much cleaner
than ever the Kanakas had done previously. The
plan is also in vogue now on the Burnett plan-
tations.
* * * *
A stand-up fight between a ’roo and a man
named Fallis happened lately at Southport, near
Brisbane. The animal was bailed up by some
dogs, one of which it hugged to death. Fallis
went to help his dog, when the fight began, and
he was getting considerably the worst of the deal,
when two extra dogs came to the rescue and killed
the ’roo.
“C.H.T.”: Be leprosy— “a disease of
which he has seen much in various parts of the
world ”: —
Not long ago, in the northern part of New Caledonia, I
had occasion to accept the hospitality of a French settler
and his half-caste wife, and whilst strolling round the
premises I noticed, in a hutch, a pair of tame white and
brown rabbits, from the nostrils of which protruded horn-
like masses of scab, the rims of their ears being also sore
and unsightly. Remarking upon this to my hostess, she
explained that, in the locality whence they had come,
the rabbits were nearly all in a similar state—but she
scouted the idea of “une maladie.” “ Non, non, M’sieur;
presque tous les lapins b, sont comme ga. Je vous
assure c’est la race.” But, three weeks later, when I re-
visited the place, the protuberant scab had disappeared,
and so had the greater part of the animals’ noses and
upper lips, and the ears had become mere torn rags.
But what chiefly strengthened my first impression that
they were suffering from some form of leprosy was the
fact that their feet had been attacked, several toes being
missing, and the rest apparently about to follow. Now,
supposing that this affection was leprosy, and that all the
rabbits in a certain locality were, as stated, similarly
affected, might not investigation throw valuable light
upon the pathology of this dread disease ?

“Yimmang”: Be Tom Coward and the
bottle-tree (B. 30/8/’02). In Queensland, years
ago, the pioneer housewives made excellent jelly
from the sap of this tree mixed with water,
sweetened, boiled, and put in a basin to “set.”
The long slits seen in many of the trees may have
been cut by the blacks as an outlet for the sap,
which they were fond of.
• » • »
“ Spinifex ” : Be distinction between ’roo-
rat and bandicoot. The b. is slightly larger than
the ’r-r, and more thick set about the front parts.
The former, also, is of a delicate grey, with a
black stripe down the back and another across the
shoulders; while the latter is of a tawny fawn
color flecked with white. Both animals are white
on throat and belly. Another distinction is that,
when hopping, the bandicoot always carries his
right paw, held stiffly across the chest, as though
in a sling.
• * * *
Several children and one or two adults
have lately been afflicted with total blindness
between Cairns and Townsville (Q.), from (the
doctors aver) eating an indigenous fruit known
as “ finger cherries.” One man was ad-
mitted to Cairns Hospital on 3rd Nov. whose
right eye had been affected, and soon after
admission he lost the sight cf the other eye. The
blindness is caused by the destruction of the in-
ternal nerves of the eye, and is pronounced
incurable. The cherries are just now receiving
the attention of the Govt. Botanist.

Anew disease has broken out among
horses at Goombungee, Queensland. The tongue
swells so —filling the entire cavity of the mouth—
that the animal is unable to eat. Shortly before
death the eyes burst, and the lips and head become
swollen. The rest of the body is not affected.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabetii-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, vvhich will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.

At Wilcannia (N.S. W.) the Darling
“River” has vanished. For several miles the
bed is absolutely dry.
Seven hundred emigrants are stranded at
Capetown, in a bankrupt country where a man
can’t even fall back on pick-and-snovel work—for
the nigger does all that. Distant hills look green,
but how bare and rocky they are on close inspec-
tion !
“ Saltpetre”: Be that monkey-rope-weight
Sroblem. About a year ago a number of S.A.
chool of Mines students decided the point prac-
tically. I was the monkey; and when, after
much sweating, I arrived at the pulley, the weight
was there too. The result was confirmed as the
logical and expected one by the Lecturer on
Physics at the University. Stated briefly, the
weight and the monkey—neglecting the weight of
the rope—travel upward at the same rate.
NELSON ILLINGWORTH'S BUST OF J. F.
SHERIDAN AS THE WIDOW O’BRIEN.
Photo, by Talma, Sydney.
The infamous female Drake (of Wellington,
M.L.), who, with the assistance of her two elder
children, flogged her little daughter so dreadfully
that the child died shortly afterwards, is not to
escape. The jury found her guilty of manslaughter,
and an appeal was lodged on the ground that evi-
dence showing the mother had always entertained
a dislike for the dead child should not have been
admitted. The appeal was dismissed, and the
child-flogger will be sentenced at next criminal
sessions. According to the evidence, when she
had beaten the child till she was utterly weary,
she made her two daughters, aged 13 or 14, flog
her in turn, and it was a few minutes after they
had finished that the little girl died. As an utterly
gruesome female Mrs. Drake has few equals.
Australia badly wants a Drapers’ Defence
Association with an Assassination Department
attached. The most down-trodden animal in the
world is the draper’s assistant. That is the male.
The female is always likely to get married, and
that makes her less dependent; but the man, to
whom marringe means only that he won’t have so
much independence as he had before (which was
none) is a hopeless case. The trade is full of
stories of tyranny practised by arrogant calico-
jimmies. One of the tribe used to make the whole
staff stand to attention in their places before the
big front doors were opened, and then he would
stalk down between the counters, his thumbs in
his armpits, muttering loudly, “You —
sons of , nice-looking set of —— cows
you are ; wonder how much you robbed me of
yesterday, damn you!” And the drapers stood
by meekly and took it all. The potentate enjoyed
it so much that one morning he had the carters
ranged up likewise. The carters were made of
sterner stuff, and some of them guffawed when
his preliminary remarks shed a lurid tint through
the atmosphere. But when he reached the rob-
bery part the man nearest him landed “one”
violently on his jaw. The head bookkeeper
fainted at the dreadful sight, but the carter de-
manded an instant retractation, failing which lie
would kick the boss while he was down. The
capitalist apologised with alacrity, and the carter
went off to look for another job. The morning
greeting is now confined rigidly to the drapers.

Building up after
Hemorrhage
The happiest results attended the
use of Scott’s Emulsion
Nurses naturally have more extended
opportunities of watching cases than
physicians. When a nurse has a story to
tell, therefore, it is based on observations
made hour by hour. And with nurses the
note of triumph is very pleasant when the
fight is won : as for example :
“ John Street, Kilmallock,
“ 00. Limerick, Ireland.
“January 17th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs, —I had a little patient, aged
five years, who suffered from a very delicate
chest and lung disease, and was subject to
hemorrhage of the lungs, accompanied by a
bad cough. I recommended your valuable
Scott’s Emulsion for her, and I am pleased
to say the happiest results have attended its
use. My little patient has now become a
strong and healthy child, and her lungs are
quite healed. The little one took the
Emulsion well from the first, her appetite
o‘h V/
%
NURSE KATHLEEN GIBSON.
(From a photograph.)
quickly improved, and the ultimate results
have given the greatest satisfaction. I con-
sider Scott’s Emulsion invaluable for delicate
children, and always feel pleasure in recom-
mending it, knowing that good results are
certain to follow its use. Very many of my
patients have taken Scott’s Emulsion upon
my recommendation, and the results are
invariably satisfactory.
“ Yours sincerely,
“ (Signed) Nurse Kathleen Gibson."
There are a number of points in Nurse
Gibson’s letter that apply in every home
where there is a delicate child, in every
home even where the children are “ not
quite right.’’
To begin with, “ the little one took the
Emulsion well ” —this proves Scott’s Emul-
sion has none of the nasty taste or smell of
common cod-livei oil. Then: “ good results
are certain to follow its use ” —mothers
therefore need never hesitate to give it.
Finally: 11 My little patient has now become
a strong and healthy child ” —this is the
result which Scott’s Emulsion always
secures—each mother of a delicate child
most earnestly desires.
You may perhaps wonder at this; you will
not if you consider a moment what Scott’s
E mulsion is. It contains pure cod-liver oil.
the standard medicinal food known to
physicians, blended with hypophosphites of
lime and soda, the two leading elements in
bone and tissue building. The process of
its manufacture is the result of the ex-
perience of thirty years. Many things have
been offered in its place from time to time,
but Scott’s Emulsion remains the standard
remedy of the British people.
Therefore there was nothing to wonder at
in the cure of Nurse Gibson’s little patient.
Hemorrhage of the lungs, bad cough, re-
tarded development, and all the painful
weakness after hemorrhage disappear in
taking Scott’s Emulsion; it calls back
appetite, health and strength, and these
bring happiness.
Look for the man carrying a big codfish
over his shoulder—unless he and his fish
are on the wrapper you are not buying
Scott’s Emulsion.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.
483 Kent street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

The Woman at the Washtub.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Woman at the Washtuh,
She works till fall of night;
With soap, and suds, and soda
Her hands are wrinkled white.
Her diamonds are the sparkle*
The copper-fire supplies;
Her opals are the bubbles
That from the suds arise.
The Woman at the Washtub
Has lost the charm of youth $
Her hair is rough and homely,
Her figure is uncouth ;
Her temper is like thunder,
With no one she agrees—
The children of the alley
They cling around her knees.
The Woman at the Washtub,
She too had her romance;
There was a time when lightly
Her feet flew in the dance.
Her feet were silver swallows,
Her lips were flowers of fire J
Then she was Bright and Early,
The Blossom of Desire.
O Woman at the Washtub,
And do you ever dream
Of all your days gone by in
Your aureole of steam ?
From birth till we are dying
You wash our sordid duds,
O Woman of the Washtub I
O Sister of the Suds 1
One night I saw a vision
That filled my soul with dread,
I saw a Woman washing
The grave-clothes of the dead ;
The dead were all the living,
And dry were lakes and meres,
The Woman at the Washtub
She washed them with her tears.
I saw a line with banners
Hung forth in proud array—
The banners of all battles
From Cain to Judgment Day.
And they were stiff with slaughter
And blood, from hem to hem,
And they were red with glory,
And she was washing them.
“ Who comes forth to the Judgment,
And who will doubt my plan ? ”
“I come forth to the Judgment
And for the Race of Man.
I rocked him in his cradle,
I washed him for his tomb,
I claim his soul and body.
And I will share his doom.”
VICTOR J. DALEY.
A Territorial Question.
The admitted area of N.S.W. is really 200,0005 q.
miles less than it rightfully should be. That’s a
fact which might be remembered just now, since
the area in question forms part of the Northern
Territory which S. Australia wishes to barter
away. N.S.W. was proclaimed with the 135th
deg. of E. longitude as her western boundary,
thus dividing Terra Aus-
tralia into two parts,
“ New Holland ” and
“New South Wales,” as
shown in Eig. 1 :
Things remained so for
41 years, when “ Wes-
tern Australia ” was pro-
claimed, embracing all
Australian territory not
included in N.S.W.
Fig. I.
This left the boundary of N.S.W. untouched,
until the formation of the province of S. Australia,
when the boundary of W.A. was shifted west to
the 129th deg. of E. longitude, and that of
N.S.W., from the south coast, as far north as the
Fig. II.
26th parallel of south
latitude, was marched
east to the 141st deg.
This left the political
boundaries of Australia
as in fig. 2.
Victoria’s separation did
not affect the territory
in question. The part-
ing of Queensland in 1859
brought the muddle.
Up to that date the continent consisted of
W.A., S.A., Vic., and N.S.W., and the
shape of the last-named was as shown in dia-
gram 2. West of the northern portion of the
western boundary of N.S.W., north of S.A., and
east of W.A., existed a country without name or
family. When Queensland was born, its western
boundary was fixed at
the 138th deg., neither
on the old boundary of
N.S.W. nor on a line con-
tinuous with her pre-
sent one. This left the
shaded strip of country
shown in diagram 3 to
N.S.W. and unless form-
ally abandoned, to that
State it belongs.
Fig. III.
The Northern Territory has never been pro-
claimed a separate colony, nor its boundaries
defined as such. It was simply handed over as a
piece of waste continent to S.A. in 1863 to look
after, without any reference to N.S.W. The
evidence points to the fact that the strip in
question is N.S.W. territory ; it is by far the best
third of the N.T. It contains two available
harbors in the Gulf of Carpentaria (the mouth of
the Roper river, which is navigable for ocean-
going vessels for over 60 miles; and the Mac-
arthur river, navigable for launches and lighters
for 30 miles). Off the immediate mouth of the
Macarthur lies the Sir Edward Pellew group of
islets, which affords good shelter in any wind.
The coast eountry is good cattle land and well-
watered ; there is an easy road up the Macarthur
River on to the tableland, where there is some of
the best-grassed downs country in Australia—in
ordinary years sharing fully in the copious wet
seasons of the tropics. That it once belonged to
N.S.W. is evident. The whole question seems
to hinge upon whether N.S.W. ever gave up her
right to it. It looks, anyhow, as if N.S.W. could
make trouble over the title.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The existence of national debt is a nega-
tion of national freedom.
Franchised women must keep a weather-
eye on the political deceiver. Many a man is
just now buying brilliantine to lubricate his track
to Parliament.
Last year Maoriland realised £19,040 out
of totalisator percentages. The tote might be the
saving of Australia yet if the Government
legalised it, and enacted that the. percentage
should go to a sinking fund to pay off the public
debt.
When one Watt came out from England
some years ago with a big N.T. land-grant rail-
way scheme in his breast pocket, the sturdy
Kingston, then S.A. Premier, kicked him out of
his office. Watt should try again: things have
changed in Adelaide
The way of the Q. State civil servant is to
be rough again, next year. Philp and co. say
tliey are going over the estimates in January.
The Q. civil servants would get much more sym-
pathy were it not an open secret that they con-
tinue to vote at every election for the Philp crowd
and its followers.
It is at least twelve months to next Federal
elections, but already the Boodlewraith is putting
its tenacles forth throughout Queensland. There
will be no overlapping of candidates this time if
it can be helped, and no effort will be spared to
feb a reversal of the White Australia verdict,
low they will miss the defunct Bulcock!
WHAT HAPPENED TO JONES.
No. 1.
JONES: “H'm ! Think I’ll have five lessons.”
No. 2.
JONES (one hour later): “Great Scott I How am
I going to get home? ”
N.S. W. Assembly has practically passed
the Land Boilers Inspection Bill and the Fisheries
Bill, undereach of which more Govt, billets are
provided. Under the Fisheries Bill the Com-
missioner is to receive £BOO a year, and his five
assistants fees of 15s. a sitting. The big billet
goes, of course, to an M.L.A., if he will take it at
£BOO. Both Bills are necessary, especially the
Land Boilers, but at the rate things are going,
half of N.S.W. will soon be busily engaged in-
specting the other half at the State’s expense.
Another result of Bent’s curtailment of
the working hours of Vic. railway employees,
which even he might have foreseen, is that while
the lower-paid “wages” men work five days a
week (or more) for five days’ pay, the salaried
officers get six days’ pay for five days’ work. So
what little grease is lying about is spread on the
fat pig, as usual.
Even in jingoistic Maoriland the returned
cohentingenter who has spent all his blood-money
and dissipated in riotous living the loot obtained
from plundered Boer farms, has now to rub
shoulders with the decrepit Chinaman when
applying for the food of charity to keep himself
alive. To be a returned warrior when Jingoism
is dead is a cold job.
If Mr. Barton abandons the Premiership
—and that seems very likely—there will b< a
joyful row as to the succession. Deakin,
R. E. O’Connor, Turner, Lyne, Kingston,
and Forrest are understood to “have claims.”
Nothing further is heard, by the way, of Turner’s
threatened resignation ; he seems to be at peace
again with his position and his colleagues.
Federal Postal Dept, is making an on-
slaught on some alleged newspapers. Several
circular-advertising affairs have been asked to
show cause why their names should not be re-
moved from the register, owing to their not ful-
filling the requirement of the 1901 Act, while
others are being assailed on the extraordinary
nature of the so-called “supplements ” which they
issue.
In reply to Federal postal official Scott’s
contention that it was only the morning news-
papers in Sydney which were agitating for the
free postage and free railway carriage for news-
papers, S.M. Herald manager Cook solemnly
pleaded that it was not so : among others, the
Sydney Mail proprietary (i.e., the S.M. Herald
people) also joined in the agitation. What a
humorist 1
Australia has to be grateful to the Federal
Labor Party for lopping no less than £250,000 off
the military estimates —so Minister Forrest lets
us know, though he doesn’t quite put it in that
way. And the retrenchment has been in the
direction of reducing the frill, not the efficiency
of the forces; it has come out of Hutton’s gold
lace and Jawbone Neild’s rooster-feathers and
'possum hats.
Let the Commonwealth be-
ware. The abolition of postal frank-
ing privileges is declared by Tasma-
nia’s 600 road-trusts and codlin-moth
committees to be “a shameful and
unwarranted interference with State
Rights! Moreover, it is protested
that it is a new tax on local bodies, as
a 2d. stamp must now be used when-
ever the year’s rates of £2 14s.
are remitted from Spud Hollow to
the departmental head in Hobart.
Even money-orders and postal-notes
must be paid for by the sender. This
is entirely opposed to Flyspeck tradi-
tions and practice, and Hollow Spud
feels bound to protest. Hell knows
no fury like a disappointed dead-
head.
Tom Mann's generalisation about the
Australian democracy, so far as he has been
able to understand it, is that, though on some
lines it has gained more material successes than
the old-world democracy, yet its mainspring isn’t
of such good material —its motives not so good,
its ideals not so high.
It looks bad to see in a Parliamentary
return that, of 533 workmen employed by Sydney
Harbor Trust, 379 are non-unionists and only 154
unionists. Since N.S.W., by a special Act, gives
its courts power to enforce preference to union
labor on private employers, Govt, departments
should have the grace to set a good example.
THE IDEAL PARLIAMENT.
Good old constitutional variety, on strictly safe and commercial lines,
and the one and all-the-time question of debate —“HOW CAN WE
MAKE HIM PAY?”
So far, S. Australia’s land purchases for
closer settlement have reached 98,355 acres, for
which £233,175 has been paid. Formerly the
land was in 30 holdings ; now the part already
disposed of (mostly on right-of-purchase lease) is
in 3219 holdings. Of 17 subdivisions under the
present Act 15 are returning from 3| to 5 per cent.
Perth (W.A.) Morning Herald says that
“ the continued influx of almost destitute unem-
ployed from the eastern States” is beginning to
create difficulties, but that “a vigorous policy of
public works” based on loan money will alleviate
the difficulty. What a pathetic faith this country
has in a vigorous policy of public works based on
loan-money !
N.S.W. Government has expressed its
willingness to abolish fees in public schools (it is
said the innovation will cost £60,000 annually) the
year after next. The Civil Service Superannua-
tion Fund will be exhausted by then, and about
£BO,OOO for pensions will have to be found the
year after next. That makes £140,000 extra to
pay the year after next. But what matter ? The
distant year looks green.
Writes “ Boh R.” :
More departmental idiocy. In N.S.W., annual leases
are limited in area to 1920 acres each, but there is no
limit whatever to the number any one individual can
hold. Consequently the enterprising middleman steps
in, takes up all the available leases in a district at a
rental of about 2d. per acre, often less, and in this period
of drought and disaster sublets them for 9d. and Is.
Such a state of affairs was disclosed at a sitting of the
Land Board at Tumberumba t’other day.
Defence Minister John Forrest is an
example of the effects of evil communications.
When he first took office he announced :
I am convinced that the present policy of mere pay-
ments of money to the Imperial Government in return for
naval defence furnished entirely by that Government will
not satisfy the people of Australia.
Now he barracks eagerly for the tribute scheme
by which Australia is to pay much more and get
no naval defence.
From Melb. Age :
To tell Australians, as Mr. Barton does, that it is
an act of wisdom to emasculate Australian sentiment
and tie this Commonwealth down in its Defence policy to
follow helplessly in the leading strings of the British
Admiralty, is repugnant at once to common sense, to the
teachings of history, and to that national self-respect and
independence which are in themselves of priceless value
in the evolution of racial character.
The Bulletin hopes yet to see the Age going for
an all-wool Australian Republic.
Of the 13 village settlements which fol-
lowed the “ back-to-the-land ” war-cry in South
Australian politics 11 years ago, six have been
abandoned wholly. The other seven, nearly all
with a River Murray frontage, have a total popu-
lation of 448. The happy dream of communistic
bliss and blessedness with which they started is
now buried Jeep under ten seasons of sand and
sweat. But they know a lot more about chopping
wood and growing oranges and hay than they did,
and that more than compensates.
The sweaters’ representatives in Yic. Leg.
Council have successfully delayed the re-enact-
ment of the Factories and Shops Act for another
fortnight. As soon as it was informed that the
Assembly had disagreed with its amendments in
the Continuance Bill to wipe out all wages boards
constituted since July last—nine boards in all—
the Council promptly reasserted its right to said
amendments, and adjourned for a week. This
was done notwithstanding that there was a
great amount of work on the business paper.
It was done so that the sweaters could squeeze
a few more pence out of the factory operative. On
receipt of the message from the Council intimat-
ing that it had insisted on its former amendments,
Premier Irvine, whose Ministers do not want to
see the Factories Act reinstated any more than
the sweaters’ representatives in the Council, made
a few cold, feeble remarks and moved that a free
conference between the two Houses be held to
consider the situation. The conference will pro-
bably occupy another week. No signs of ‘ ‘ hasty
legislation” about these proceedings. A cater-
pillar which started level with them would be out
of sight by now.

importers are
seeking to work-

similar to the one
which recently
