is Buckley’s—or it deserves to be.
When the Factories and Shops Bill was
before Westralian Assembly there were actually
five votes cast for an amendment to the effect that
“No married woman whose husband is able to
work shall be allowed to work in any factory.”
The husband might be out of a billet, or he might
have deserted his wife years before, but it was
seriously proposed that so long as he was a live
and able-bodied citizen his wife should be
practically denied any employment in W.A.
except domestic service! She would even be
shut out of office-work, for “factory,” in the
W.A. legal sense, includes offices.
The blatant Imperialism, and general be-
littling of Australia’s national aspirations by Vic.
Governor Clarke, Admiral Beaumont and others,
at Mayor Gillott’s banquet recently, has had the
welcome effect of stirring up a strong resentment.
There has been a representative meeting in Mel-
bourne to form an Australian Defence League,
which, among other things, will “ keep a watch-
ful eye on Mr. Barton and other public men, ” on
naval matters. One speaker pertinently pointed
out that, with the £200,000 per year, which is the
proposed new naval tribute, Australia could, in 20
years, build five first-class battle-ships and man
and maintain them as they came to hand.
Thp Presbyterian conspiracy to secure the
oulk of the N.S.W. Public Service billets for the
soured and embittered race that believes in the
damnation of infants (if there is any con-
spiracy it is certainly a Presbyterian one)
is still being vigorously pursued by Parson
Dill-Macky and his clan. The information
laid before Parliament shows that, at grabbing
more than its share of public offices, the sect with
the Mac before its name and the whisky-bottle
concealed in its kilt has no rival. And Parson
Dill’s implied demand for a wholesale sacking of
Catholics from the service seems to imply that
there are still some good Calvinists unprovided for.
There was big effort on the part of a section
of the Freetrade press to organise a dingo-howl
when Premier Barton went to Maitland to speak.
But it was hard work. As Luck would have it,
Maitland constituency has scored tremendously of
late ; flourishing coal-mines have been opened up,
and the lucerne paddocks have given really golden
returns. The Maitlanders, illogically, perhaps,
but none the less naturally, put a lot of this down
to Barton, and the indications are that even if
Brunker, who has bought or sold a cow for almost
every elector in the constituency, were to stand
against him now, Barton would win. In the past
it has always been argued that Barton held his
seat only by the grace of cow-man Brunker.
When the Ballance Government intro-
duced the Lands for Settlement Act, giving
the State the power of expropriation, the
whole of the Maoriland Tory press rose to yell
“ Un-British ! ” Now, eight years after, the same
press applauds the decision of the Imperial
Government to introduce practically the same
Act into the Orange Free State and the Transvaal
in order to settle British farmers on the soil. The
moral of which is that loyal subjects shouldn’t be
worried as to whether anything is un-British or
not. If it pays it will darned soon be British. A
large portion of history is taken up with the rela-
tion of the way about a sixth of the earth, which
was at one time un-British, has become British.
The British Secretary of State for India,
it is cabled, has indignantly repudiated, as a
“ gross libel,” the assertion that the prosperity of
India has declined under British rule, and points
proudly to the fact that during the last three
years, despite the famine, the surplus revenue has
been over £8,000,000, Yet, considering that the
chief source of revenue, the land-tax, is now about
five times as heavy as it was under the old native
rulers, even a poor country might yield a surplus.
Lord George Hamilton added that though it was
a “ gross libel ” to state that the prosperity of the
Indian masses had decreased, he regretted to say
that the cultivators of the soil were sinking more
deeply into debt. Seeing that the cultivators
represent something like three-fourths of the
people, aren’t they the masses ?

S. A. democrats are wondering whether the
next act of the Jenkins Ministry will be the
abolition of free education. Education Minister
Gordon has called upon his officers to suggest how
savings amounting to several thousands can be
effected. Gordon says he “ fears that the growing
cost of the system may lead to its breakdown”—
which means that if the Tory crowd in S.A.
Assembly repeat last session’s demand for abolition
Jenkins-Iscariot will humbly submit.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
When Carbine won Melbourne Cup in 1890 the
prize for the first horse was worth £10,230, with £2OOO for
the second and £lOOO for the third—total £13,230. That
was the highest figure ever reached. When The Grafter
won the Cup in ’9B his owner only collected £2774 • and
£5OO for the second horse and £250 for the third made
the total £3524. Things have improved since then, and
The Victory’s win was worth £4588, while the three
placed horses gathered in £6OBB among them. When
Banker won the Cup in ’63 his win only brought the
owner £490, while the prize for the second horse was £2O.
The owner of the third horse, in those days, only got the
honor of the thing for his reward
* * * .

withheld the 6d. for each railway ticket, which used to

Champion two-year-old display of the season
was at the Tyro Stakes, Randwick, Saturday. Cashier
won through the skilful riding of jockey Barden. This
horse was bred by S. Hordern at Wilton Park, by Gossoon
(imp.) from Hebe (imp.) by Pursebearer. Other hand-
some ones were Brumlo by Bill of Portland from Cooya
by Nordenfeldt, Cake Walk, Cherson and Carmenita.
• • • .
Those who were associated with the Sydney nag
Merton Steel, who broke his leg in Melbourne Cup and
nad to be shot, thought him an absolute “cert.” for the
race. I* or months he had been specially prepared, and
was never sick or sorry or suffered the slightest set back,
.trainer, Albert Cornwall, says in his 35 years’ experience,
bar Cap-a-pie, Merton Steel was the only horse he ever
had that properly stood a thorough Melbourne Cup
preparation. Had he come off Mo. would have got a
staggerer, for he was backed to win a big fortune
mainly because most of the bejewelled ones wouldn’t
stand hnn at any price, and many overlaid the nag in
consequence. Cornwall worked his colt into the required
hx by running him in races less than his distance and
over lengthy journeys on the quiet. A dav or two
before the race Merton Steel showed a ljjm. gallop that
equalled Revenue’s last year, and made him an even-
money chance. Also said that Merton Steel could get
over the Caulfield stretch every bit as fast as Wakeful.
“1.3 T*” ® florfc ln company was with Acrasia, who re-
ceived 151bs. over and above their Cup weights, and was
beaten easily. Yet Acrasia ran fourth in the race.

Judged by the opening showofN.S.W. Trotting
Club, that sport has come to stay in Sydney. Despite
big counter attraction of Kensington pony meet, a large
crowd turned up andprovided a profit, which, considering
heavy preliminary expenditure and the fact that cham-
pion trotter britz had been specially brought down from
the country to exhibit, augurs promisingly. Among the
winners was Mr. “ Bob ” Beckett’s Cast-Off—a nag bred
in Bathurst) district, and raced there as Green Stuff
so named through having previously pulled a vegetable-
cart along for months. Contrast the game in M.L.,
where the N.Z. Metropolitan Club, a week or so since,
gave awiiy 63os°vs. for an afternoon’s sport, and then
raked in a big surplus.
* * • •
A queer paragraph, sent to The Bulletin by a
woman Had a pleasant afternoon outside Moorefield
(Sydney) racecourse. Mingled with the unwashed, and
with another woman saw the races through the fence
We hadn t any wealth, therefore backed no winners in-
dulged in no curry (threepence a plate, vinegar free) nor
in a lovely, amber-colored drink, exhibited in large glass
jars with floating* lumps of ice ; but contented ourselves
with studying humanity. One ancient lady was the
proud possessor of one buck tooth, which she medita-
tively tapped with a florin, staring intently the while at
a bookmaker (one of many carrying on their business
with a fence for a background). At last bookie wearied
of the insistent stare—‘Gim’me that two bob,’ he said •
so she backed something. When last I saw her she was
sitting in the gutter regretting. It was a blazing after-
noon, so we took refuge between races in the R.C. Church
opposite. A portly priest came from the vestry. ‘Good
afternoon; do you ladies wish to confess?’ ‘Not this
afternoon, Father,’ I answered, trying to look as if I were
sometimes taken that way. Then the saddling bell rang
and I rushed again to the fence.” b
• • • *
’ s „ Melbourne Cup sweeps having proved
that the Postal Act hampers without suppressing, there
is a cry for “ further measures.” As usual, the pious folk
voice the demand, though it is the book-making interest
which formulates it. As a matter of cold fact the
amount of money now passing through the hands of
Moses of the Book is not sufficient to keep him in the
style to which he has accustomed himself ; and, to main-
tain the champagne and the diamonds, and the truffled
turkey, and the motor car—to say nothing of the lady in
sealskin the bookies nowadays have to prey upon one
another as well as upon the general public. That’s bad
business. If the money that is devoted to other
forms of gambling—“Tatt.’s,” chief of all—could be
diverted into the bookies’ hands (and levied on to the
extent of 50 or 60 per cent., not 10 per cent, as “Tatt ”
chiirges)—and that would be the inevitable result of
effective suppression of the sweeps—there would be a fat
time for Ikey Mo. The public will gamble. If it can
gamble at a general loss of 10 percent, with “Tatt.” it
will do so ; if it is forced to gamble at a general loss of
50 per cent, with the-“ books,” it will do so. Therein is
the reason why the Birds of Prey invoke the Birds of
Pray against “Tattersall.” ’

* * • *
The jockey Barden boom continues. At Rand-
wick on Saturday he scored three wins ; his beating of
First Consul, on Lady Grafton, in the Salisbury Stakes
was a fine display of horsemanship. Although the crowd
gave him a great reception in recognition of his skill it
had a sore grin on its face at the time, as Lady Grafton’s
win was a good thing for Ikey Mo.
* * * *
Maoriland bred Nelson-Moonga six-year-old nag
Seahorse 11. won the 6 furlong November Handicap at
Warwick (Eng,). Spencer Gollan, the owner, bought the
horse in Maoriland after he’d scored some successes. In
11 starts as a two-year-old Seahorse gathered in Auckland
Handicap,. Criterion Handicap, and Snorts Handicap. At
three years, of nine starts, Hawk ns Bay Guineas, Spring
Handicap, M.L. Cup, Canterbury Derby and Cup and
Auckland Plate fell to him. In Australia he was disap-
pointing ; second to Kinglike in Caulfield Eclipse Stakes
was his best performance.

Sydney City Tatts born of a rupture between
big Sydney Tatts. and Kensington people, reports a big
profit on the year, despite the fact that many of its
opulent Ikeys were drawn away to the bigger concern bv
the reduced admission fee. The City crowd has secured
premises right opposite the other for £6500, and the rival
gangs will now be able to sit at the front windows
and make noses at each other over the whisky.
Owner (Mr. G. 0. Stead) gave 325g5. for
Cruciform as a yearling, and that nag has since won
£5630 in prize money alone—£lsls came when she was
2yrs. old, £3765 at 3yrs., and the balance subsequently.
* * * •
“Ciro”: With all the talk about Sandow,
Pagel and the rest, not one professional strong man has
ever laid claim to the authenticated feats of Melb.
amateur Tom Waldron, the red-headed Irish policeman
who died at Carlton a couple of years back. On one
occasion, while stationed at Port Melbourne, Waldron
went aboard a vessel berthed at the pier, and, for the
amusement of the crew, picked up two large pieces of
machinery lying on the deck and carried them, one in
each hand, to the opposite taffrail. An interested spec-
tator had the masses of iron weighed, and each tipped
the beam at scwt. Can either Sandow or Pagel
show a feat to compare with this? Veteran Prof. Miller
once expressed the opinion that Waldron was absolutely
the strongest man in the world, and it would seem as if
the Prof.’s opinion was well-based.
• * * *
‘‘ Jones ”: The three-card swindle described by
‘ Bosco” (B. 8/11/’O2) may be new to this unsophisticated
country, but it is none the less very ancient elsewhere. I
remember it because it was the first and last time I was
‘ had ”by the three-card man. It was at Aintree (Eng.),
in the early ’Bo’s, and I had “ wagged ” it to see Liverpool
Unprun. I had spotted a horse bearing my own name,
and the coincidence moved me to put my only half-crown
thereon. I was looking round for the longest price when
I stumbled across aforesaid spieler, and the thing seemed
a dead cert. ; although he professed to be unwilling
to wager less than £l, he condescended to accept my
half-crown—and that was the last I saw of it. Naturally
as I had then no money to put on him, John Jones must
needs go and win the Cup-at 7 to 1, I think. I can
never understand why a thoroughly accomplished card
juggler wants to swindle. He never offers more than
even money, and, as the theoretical odds are 2 to 1 he
makes 50 per cent, profit on the deal. Isn’t that enough ?
M.L. is thb x,nly section of tiiis continent that
won t stand the slightest attempt at a serious boxing
match, and yet that country was the birth-place of some
of the best fighters the world has known—Bob Fitz-
simmons, Billy Murphy, Han Creedon, Tom Tracev,
Harry Laing and co. to wit. Recently a couple of small
fights were brought off without interference, and then
the clever Sydney lightweight, Bob Turner, and Ross were
matched. The police swooped down and arrested them
both, after letting matters go till the eleventh hour almost,
with the result that the pair were “ bound over ” M L
has changed much since Paddy Slavin fought Maori Laing
and Richardson there, and since Jem Mace toured the
islands, and Billy Murphy engaged in those several fierce
struggles that opened the door for him in Sydney and the
rest of the world. The most, and best, of Fitzsimmons’
early battles took place in Sydney.
* * * *
Bob Turner-Jack M'Gowan fight at Sydney
Gaiety A. 0., Monday night, for Australasian lightweight
supremacy, drew 1600 people, and a substantial margin
over the £l5O purse given. Two better-prepared pugs
never faced each other, nor two more evenly matched in
point of poundage. Turner in his pelt, M’Gowan in a
flannel sweater, each scaled 9.3£. Turner, yet only 19
showed remarkably good condition, and splendid develop-
ment about the shoulders and arms, all acquired since
his last ring appearance. His pink-tinted flesh, which
S f' Ved M<^' th . health > was striking
contrast to M’Gowan’s pallid, though not un-
healthy - looking, body. Turner forced the game,
assuming the offensive, a new experience for M’Gowan
who mostly has to chase his men. With right-hand glove
well up over the left jaw as a fend for M’Gowan’s dan-
gerous right, Turner jabbed and hooked magnificently on
IfXn of/’ u " der the hea *t. and in the stomach with

The most notable of the various attempts on
the British Channel swimming-record this year was that
of a Sicilian, who got to the scene of action by natural
means. That is, he first swam across to Italy, and then
walked the rest of the way across Europe 1 He deserved
better luck than to fail at the Calais to Dover swim.
• * * *
At Pyrmont, Saturday, Dick Cavill, using his
famous “crawl” stroke, covered 100 yards in what four
watches made from 58 3-5 to 59 3-ssecs., the greatest
speed known in the world till the same man did it in
68 3-ssec. in London a few months ago. N.S.W. 300yds.
premiership to be decided at Bondi to-day (Saturday),
looks a moral for Dick unless some unknown David brings
along new world’s figures.
* • ■ *
Winner (W. H. Cooper) of Australasian single-
handed bowling premiership in Melbourne t’other day is
the old Vic. cricketer, who was one of the 1884 Australian
combination in England. Noteworthy that one or two
Victorian bowls scribes very early in the tourney tipped
Cooper to win, which says much for his superiority, as
the element of fluke is with bowls far more than any
other pastime.
* * • •
“ B.B.” : There seems to be only one idea as to
what cricket consists of —judging by the way the batting
department is being bolstered up. Therein will come
great trouble for the N.S.W. selection committee in the
forthcoming “big” matches. Last season there were
many bowlers who made an effort to hold up that end of
the game ; but, this year, everyone of them seems to
have taken to Sandowism or some other department of
the game, and others have relinquished trundling for
batting, in which they are ignominious failures. Marsh,
the Sydney bowler, who trundled brilliantly last season,
and who was excluded from “big” cricket on the color
line and sensational reports, seems to have gone “ off,”
and as no new men have shown “ form ” worth speaking
about, there is every possibility that recruits will have to
come in from the country districts—which is so objec-
tionable to city cricketers. The selection committee
seems to lose sight of the fact that N.S.W.’s best bowlers
—M‘Kibbin, Turner, Evans, Howell, etc.-were men
brought to Sydney and who showed such brilliancy that
their selection was inevitable.
* » • *
Something like a record was put up last
season in England by a, professional cricketer named
White, when he knocked up 2000 runs, took 250 wickets,
made 12 centuries (including two 200’s), got all 10
wickets twice, and performed the hat-trick six times 1
Whew ! 1
• * • •
The inter-State cricket fixture difficulty between
N.S.W. and S.A. has again been patched up, and the
matches will be played. The new terms are that N.S.W.
offers to make good any loss S.A. may incur over the two
matches during the season. A definite agreement is
needed between the two associations, which will hold
good for a term of years ; as it is, S.A. is mostly dependent
on N.S.W. chanty, since if the Wheatfielders failed to
send a team in rep. inter-State cricket they would pro-
bably be debarred from selection for an Australian
Eleven. Then there would be a rumpus !
* * • •
N.S.W. Cricket Association, in the matter of
umpires for inter-State matches, proposes that Vic. and S.A.
Associations submit three names, and the visiting cap-
tain will choose one and the opposing team another It
is an original idea, well worth a trial.
* * • *
Yet another departure ! Clem. Hill, the S.A.
“ star ” batsman, intends to go to S’Africa at the end of
the present season—unless Australia makes it worth his
while to stay.
* * * •
Hugh Trumble, back in Melbourne, says that
the ram that fell towards the finish of the fourth test-
match (at Manchester) made the wicket easy for the last
two English batsmen, but the waiting whilst the rain
cleared off had destroyed the nerve of Tate. He hit
wildly at the only two balls he got, fluked the one for a
fourer,” and missed the other by the proverbial mile
whilst it turned into his stumps. Then Tate stared
pathetically at his devastated wicket, and the Australians
threw up their hats.
• » * * /
Fresh rounds were entered upon in Sydney
first grade cricket, Saturday. Wickets and weather were
good, but scores were not high, and Sydney’s 299 for 6
wickets against Central Cumberland was the best. Of
these, 19-stone H. Deery made 72, Duff 63, and Cuffe 60.
Orlebe lost 5 for 276 to Paddington, Duggan making 92 not
out, and Buckle, also not out, 62. Waverley made 252
against ’Varsity, 100 of which were contributed by Charlie
Gregory. At close, ’Varsity had 3 down for 61. Nine of
North Sydney’s wickets brought 139, to Burwood’s 114
Redfern-Leichhardt match is still very doubtful, with
Redfern’s total at 188, and latter 4 for 72. Of Redfern’s
runs, 71 were made by Foster, and 8 of 9 of that side’s
wickets which fell to bowlers were secured by Young at
a cost of 76—which was easily the best howling feat of
the day. B
* • * *
Sydney Homing Pigeon Society’s race from
Ararat (Vic.), a bee-line of 520 miles, was the longest yet
traversed by Australian homers. The birds were liberated
at 5 a.m., Friday morning, and at 1.15 next day, 22 hours
later, John Ward’s pigeon reached Balmain. If the bird
spent the whole 22 hours on the wing, it meant a speed of
685 yards per minute. It was the same bird which last
year did the 384 miles.between Arcadia (Vic.) and Sydney
The new record is well up with those of Germany and
England, where pigeon-homing is very popular.
Scheps, winner of*Sydney Wheel Race, himself
built the machine that won the brilliantly-contested mile.
He is the son of an Adelaide gunsmith, and has only been
wheeling about a year.

Don Walker’s appropriation of the Inter-
national Half-mile Scratch Race at Melbourne bike sports
Saturday, was generally anticipated, though the big
smash, through Jackson touching Lewis’ wheel made it
easier. Apparently Walker is going to scoop all the
scratch races of the Australian season. Since Oct 17
every one contested in N.S.W., S.A., and Victoria has
gone to him. In five weeks Walker has won nearly £2OO
prize money, and there’s a big harvest ahead during the
next three Saturdays. The odds are Walker will garner
them all, despite that others-—notably Walne —apveav to
be getting closer to him. Increasing age has made
Walker more brilliant; nine years ago he was on scratch
in front-rank company, but did not show anything like
the form he exhibits now.
• • * *
Don Walker’s sensational sprints call for grit of
no mean order, yet his friends tell that though he might
swim all day if he felt (or fancied he did) a supporting
hand under his. chest, he would sink straight away did ha
know or think it was not there : they’ve tried him.
* * * «
The success of men mounted on Dunlop Tyres
at the recent Cycling Carnival was really phenomenal, as
practically every race was won with their aid. “ Donlop ”
Walker, Seheps, Walne, Megson, Gordon, all demonstrated
the speed qualities of them ; and the competition record
made in the Sydney Wheel Race, of one mile in 1.54
will take a deal of shifting. The Dunlop co. is now
experimenting with a new vulcanised racing tyre, which,
as regards lightness, will eclipse anything hitherto seen
on this market, and speed-merchants are anxiously await-
ing its issue, which, however, will not take place until
the tests of durability are quite completed.*

After a long tussle, a 601 b. cod was landed,
t’other day, from Yanko Creek, Wangaratta, Yic. The
“ old man ” had been seen feeding along the shallows for
weeks before, and, though far from being the largest,
was the gamest fish hooked in the vicinity of late years 1
Melbourne Age journalist, Fox, who was
humorless enough to carry his personal appear-
ance into a Victorian law court and ask a jury to
say whether he really-had “a face like dirty fat ”
(as politician Trenwith had, more or less seriously,
alleged in the course of excited polemics), was
awarded one shilling damages on Tuesday.
Rev. J. Kinghorn (Pres.), of Bathurst, in
a sermon on the N.S. W. sectarian row:—
He did not know who was to blame, but he would say
that the thing: chat had cursed the Church was its
dabbling- in politics. He did not think that any Christian
preacher or teacher should be dragged into politics. The
Church of God should never be allowed to enter political
matters. It has been stated that the Rev. Dill-Macky
would visit Bathurst in the interest of the Protestant
Defence Association. He (the preacher) had been asked
to assist in the matter, but had refused to have anything
to do with it. . . . He would rather see every priest and
clergyman banished from the land, and every church
razed to the ground, than see religious battle.
Sydney and Melbourne have now got a
“Lord ” Mayor apiece, just as The Bulletin may
have a Lord Office Boy instead of a plain boy.
The only probable result is that there will be a
greater tendency than before to elect a rich and
probably useless person to the office on the ground
that the title has to be lived up to. Living up to
a title is mostly a matter of vittles and drink.
Glasgow contrives to be a go-ahead municipality
despite its “ Lord ” Provost, but it is an exception.
Postmaster-General Drake had a good
reception last week from the Laborites and
Radicals of Brisbane. Drake’s happiest sally on
the evening of his meeting was a quotation from
his chief enemy, the Brisbane Telegraph. The
Telegraph, when denouncing Drake for his firm-
ness in dealing with the attempted imposition of
Messrs. Cobb and Co., had said that if Cobb and
Co. had approached the State Govt, with a re-
quest for a subsidy in their alleged trouble, there
would simply have been “a meeting between
Cobb and Co.’s manager, the Treasurer, and a
bank manager—and the whole thing would have
been arranged without more ado.” This state-
ment was so characteristic of Philpocrat methods,
and so ludicrous in' the face of the subsequent
Cobb and Co. fiasco, that the audience simply
roared with laughter. -t
Melbourne discovered a leper t’other day.
Ah Fong, a Chinese gardener. The patient had
only arrived from the Flowery Land about two
years ago. Lepers have been rather rare of late,
except in speckled Queensland.
Rush of cases to N.S.W. Arbitration
Court is so great that the formation of a second
court for a couple of years is suggested ; after two
years, it is thought, industrial relations will be in
a more settled state' and the great pressure of
work will cease.
In Victoria the Government has once more
decided to make advances to farmers to buy seed-
wheat. And yet Victoria is the State in which
Chief Justice Madden is allowed to go to agricul-
tural shows and orate on the absolute and mani-
fest necessity of leaving the poor farmer alone.
According to Madden, C.J., on one memorable
occasion, the farmers only wanted the State to
stand out of their daylight. That is but a few
months ago, and thousands of pounds have since
been spent in making roads, railways, and water
works for the farmers who want to be left alone.
All these things have been done at the urgent
request of said farmers. Now, Government is to
spend a few more thousands in providing seed-
wheat for the farmers, which is another act of
fussy State interference. In 1896 the State made
similar advances to the farmers, and lost heavily.

The Federal Govt, has received word that
Mr. Chamberlain has declined to “advise” His
Majesty to veto the Pacific Islands Laborers Act,
by which Tommy Tanna is to be cleared out of
Queensland by the end of 1907. Chamberlain
declines on the ground that the Act doesn’t involve
any Imperial interest, and that the British Govt,
is not prepared to take upon itself the functions of
a Court of Appeal from the Parliament of a self-
governing State. Which, besides being a polite
snub for Premier Philp; shows that Mr. Chamber-
lain begins to know something of Australian
sentiment.
The compiling of the rolls of N.S. W. female
voters has brought some interesting facts to light.
In the Sydney district, for instance, the rolls
show a total number of 225,153 votes out of which
only 102,424 are males. The females are in
the majority by 20,305. In some individual
electorates this excess of female voters over male
is quite astonishing; in one case—that of the
suburb of Burwood—out of a total 5554 votes, the
females are in a majority of 728. Where is that
alleged female who refused to be put on the rolls ?
“Malle”: Be appointment of a Public
Defender, with control of detectives, etc., to hunt
up evidence for accused persons:
A very real grievance exists in the country districts,
where a man may be committed for trial to a town a
couple of hundred miles away. The police will see he
gets to the court all right, but if he be acquitted, he is
left to get home as best he may. Knew a case of a
bullocky, who was arrested over a cattle-matter, and
committed to a town 90 miles off. The case fell
through, and accused, who had been prevented from
going for his usual wool-carting, and had sold his saddle-
horse to meet expenses, was dead-broke, and had to
stump the 90 miles home.
There is a widespread idea that govern-
ments were made to hand out large sums of
money, and people bring actions against them
that they would hardly dream of bringing against
private individuals on similar grounds. For in-
stance, a lady last week started to sue the Vic-
torian Railway Department for £lOOO as compen-
sation for the loss of an eye “ caused by severe
physical strain incurred in opening a door” at
Flinders-street (Melbourne) station when she
quitted the train on the morning of the 23rd
April. She dropped the case when it was half
through, having remembered that on the morning
of 23rd April she went straight into town on
leaving the train, without waiting to open any
door, therefore she must have lost her eye opening
that door at a different day or hour. Still it was
a curious case.
The discovery of Dolores by Sydney and
Melbourne after the advent of Melba shows that
the judgment of the discerning public occasion-
ally requires the illumination of a Guiding Light.
No such thing, however, is required to convince
any man with a thirst that D.C.L. is the best
brand of whisky. Also that popular spirit
strengthens the judgment in all mundane affairs.
Little wonder then, that D.C.L. is swallowed
with, enthusiasm by the Wise in business, in
politics and in poetry—especially politics and
poetry.*

A Decaying Profession.
Some burglars having broken into the house of
a poor man were diligently engaged ransacking
his shelves and drawers when the owner suddenly
awoke and sat upright in bed. “ Hunt away, my
men, hunt away,” he said. “I don’t know what
you expect to find here by this dim light. For my
Eart, I can find nothing of value in the day.” And
aving made these remarks he resumed his
slumbers and the disgusted burglars withdrew.
The fact is, the profession of which Mr. Bill Sikes
was so conspicuous an ornament is a decaying one,
and the art of birglary is in a fair way to be lost.
There are more reasons for this than we have time
now to inquire into, but possibly one of them may
be the counter attraction of selling something
warranted to do that which it will not do. Even
as there are no friends like old friends, so there is
no remedy the equal of Mother Seigel’s Curative
Syrup with its thirty-three years of world-wide
renown. Just give two minutes’ attention to the
testimony of Mr. J. Armitage, the licensed
carrier, of Schrader-street, Adelaide, S.A., and
his lt deserves it. Mr. Armitage writes,
on the 25th of March, 1902 : —“As both myself
and my wife have derived great benefit from the
use of Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, it is only
just that I should thank you for it and give a few
particulars which may direct others, now suffering
as we formerly did, where to look for relief and
cure. My wife was afflicted for years with indiges-
tion. She tried a variety of mixtures, pills, &c.,
and at different times was treated by several
doctors, but without avail. Frequently she was
quite unable to attend to her household affairs.
About three years ago a neighbour recommended
her to try Mother Seigel’s Curative Syrup, with
the result that she was cured within six weeks.
As for myself, I was subject to asthma and
rheumatism for a long period. Often have I lain
in bed for days suffering torture through the latter
complaint. The asthma, too, was very distressing,
especially in damp or cold weather. I spent
much money, and swallowed a lot of physic, in the
endeavour to free myself of these ailments; but
it was all to no purpose. At last it occurred to
me that possibly the medicine which had cured
my wife might benefit me; and, having resolved to
make the experiment, I discovered that it was
just what I required. It speedily enabled me to
breathe with ease.and comfort, as well as subdu-
ing the rheumatism. About six bottles of the
Syrup cured me. My wife and I always have a
bottle of it in the house, and by taking a few doses
of it every week our health is never a source of
anxiety to us.”

The “Electric Post” idea is making the
British P.0, officials move uneasily in their sleep.
Berlin has admittedly the most efficient post and
telegraph service in the world, and there they have
a system of quarter-hour pneumatic tube services
(for letters) from every suburb within five miles of
the central office and an hourly delivery from
7 a.m. to 6 p.m. The letters are posted in the
ordinary pillar-box and thence by the tube which
runs underground, thus avoiding weather-difficul-
ties. The total cost of transmission is slightly
over one penny. Imagine a resident of Bondi or
Coogee knowing that a letter posted in those
suburbs at, say, 4.15 p.m. would be delivered in
George-st. city at 5.10 p.m. ! Also in every
German town of importance Hughes’ type-writing
telegraph instrument is employed and it is, as
regards rapidity and accuracy, as far ahead of the
Morse system as the Morse is of the old single-
needle affair.
“J.A.C.”: He absurd inconsistencies in
Vic. Licensing Act (B., 15/ll/’02). It is quito
true that while the law compels any publican to
supply boarders and bona-fide travellers with
whatever refreshment they may require on Sun-
days, it at the same time debars him from opening
his bar for the purpose. I remember, some years
ago, a Bendigo hotel-keeper being fined for going
into his bar on a Sunday to turn on his gas-meter,
although at the time there was nobody else in the
house but his wife.

A Word to Women.
With the advent of Summer comes that weary,
worn-out feeling. • Women more particularly are
sufferers in this respect, owing partly to their
confinement in the house, and not getting plenty
of fresh air, and owing a great deal to the delicate
constitution of the female organs, which give way
under the strain of work and worry. To all
women who are feeling “done up” at this time
of the year, a course of Bile Beans will prove of
immense benefit. They brace up and give tone to
the various organs, thus strengthening the whole
system.
As an illustration of this, Mrs. S. A. Masters,
of 18 Jarrett-st., Leichhardt, Sydney, says:—“l
have found your Bile Beans are an excellent
remedy for a disordered liver. At times I have
been so bad with indigestion, that I did not know
what to eat. My appetite was considerably im-
paired and although at night I have felt like
having a good sleep, I could not do so, and in
consequence, felt completely done up during the
day, and suffered agony with sick and nervous
headaches. I was induced to take your medicine
by seeing an advertisement wherein Bile Beans
had cured a fellow sufferer, and can now say from
my own experience, that Bile Beans are a perfect
remedy for sick headache, loss of appetite, sleep-
lessness and those innumerable symptoms of a
sick liver. I would now use no other medicine,
and always keep a box at hand for an emergency.”
Bile Beans have gained universal praise as an
undoubted specific for biliousness, indigestion,
constipation, piles, bad blood, pimples and all
skin eruptions, debility, nervousness, anaemia,
female weaknesses, sleeplessness, headache, and a
host of other ailments that owe their origin to
defective bile flow, assimilation and digestion.
Bile Beans are obtainable from all medicine
vendors, price Is. l£d or 2s. 9d large box (contains
three times small size) or direct from the Head
Australasian depot, the Bile Beans Manufacturing
Co., 39 Pitt-street, Sydney.*
Martin’s Apiol and Steel Pills are sold by
all chemists throughout Australasia, price 4s. 6d.
Agents: Sayers, Allport, and Potter, Sydney.
Sole Proprietor, W. Martin, Pharmaceutical
Chemist, Southampton, England.*

Prom Melbourne Age— one of the reasons
why the Victorian railways don’t pay:—
In a certain office at the Flinders-st. railway-station
there may be seen by the curious a very long list of
names of ladies who have at various times begged the
booking clerks to let them have, without payment,
tickets to various places—chiefly Flemington and Sun-
° Ul T' They have forgotten their purses, or lost them, or
spent their last penny on anew pair of gloves, and various
other reasons. They will call the very next day, and re-
fund the money without fail. But there the names and
addresses lie, with the amount of the borrowed money
written opposite ; and there appears no prospect of these
small debts ever being wiped out. The authorities are
considering the advisability of publishing this list in the
newspapers at an early date.
Probably, in many cases, the ladies have given
wrong names, and if the Government publishes
them its reward will be a crop of libel actions.
The best thing Minister Bent can do is to write the
lot off as a bad debt, and ukase that in future the
lady who has forgotten her purse shall be shown
into a private room, and shall there leave a suffi-
oitmt amount of underclothing as security.

for
Indigestion
Chamberlain’s
Stomach and
Liver Tablets.
Pt*lee, 1/6
Indigestion results from taking too much
food or some article of food of difficult
digestion. There is a feeling of distress and
sometimes severe cramps of the stomach,
nausea, vomiting, and sometimes purging,
but most often constipation.
The vomiting is beneficial as it relieves, the
stomach of the indigestible food, and when
the bowels remain normal and there is no
Cramping pains of the stomach, no medicine
whatever is required. All that is needed is
to let the stomach rest, take no food until
hungry, and then only a small amount of
milk toast, until the stomach is strong enough
to digest other food which should be taken
sparingly at first. When there is constipa-
tion, two of Chamberlain’s Stomach
and Liver Tablets should be taken to
move the bowels, and one tablet each follow-
ing day for three or four days until the
bowels become regular. If Chamberlain’s
Stomach and Liver Tablets act two freely
on the bowels, take only one tablet every
other day.

At Poverty Point.
Geo. Mitsgrove’s mistake in asking three and two
guineas for first and second choice of seats for the scrappy
Melba opera performances was made much bigger by the
unwisdom of his booking arrangements. He fixed the
box-plan at the Princess’s Theatre, instead of at Allan’s
or Glen’s, as is usual, and he allotted three days to the
“ rush ” for 3-guinea seats, and another three days for the
2- “ rush.” The result was that, at the end of six
days, everybody knew that the demand for Melt a
tickets had been very mild and casual, consequently the
public didn’t rush madly for 1-guinea tickets.
Then came the announcement that the back-stalls
as well- as amphitheatre seats would be sold for
10s. 6d. and reserved. The managerial tactics
had proved a failure. Now, if Musgrove had been
content to fix his maximum at 2 guineas and allot only
one day each to the “ first choosers ” he might have had a
fairly excited demand for 1-guinea seats on the second
day and thereafter. To quote 3 guineas as the top-
price for a programme of operatic selections—scenes
without a chorus—and to set apart a full half-week for
the sale of seats at that figure was far too big a “bluff”
for a public that had already put up its guineas for six
Melba concerts. A well-chosen “ bluff” deserves success.
Managers have to grow rich by business management
rather than by the mere virtues of their show. But this
3- bluff was ill-chosen.
The Fitzgeralds got out a very toney programme— i.e.,
J. J. Miller produced it for them—as a souvenir of their
Melba night at the Circus. It had an introductory note
which pleased the star visitor, though the adjectives
might have been drawn somewhat milder: “ Two
thoroughbred Australians welcome and honor the greatest
of all living singers, and feel proud that she is a native of
the greatest, most glorious, and grandest country under
the sun.” For this unrivalled land of milk and honey its
“ Greatest Daughter” recently made a private appeal for
financial'assistance, whereupon its grandest politicians
upraised their glorious hands in horror, crying “Hus-s-sh!
Not a word ! We are about to borrow more money in the
usual national, patriotic way, and you mustn’t spoil our
credit. Thank you so much—but, really, you mustn’t
spoil our credit.” - ’
Strange, coincidence that a revival of “Little Lord
Fauntleroy ” should be talked about only a week or two
after the death of Ada Willoughby. Given the necessary
cash and encouragement luckless Ada would have pro-
duced the play what time she was running the little
Queen’s Theatre ( nei Victoria Hall) in Melbourne. It was
advertised at the bottom of her play-bill as a forthcoming
event. Previous to that “Little Lord Fauntleroy” had
been a dead letter for many years.
Cuyler Hastings’ Sherlock Holmes is an excellent stage
conception, but it is by no means Gonan Doyle’s Sherlock
Holmes. The original was impassive, reticent, imperturb-
able ; our American visitor shows us a man given to
garrulity and outbursts of passion. The Moriarty organ-
isation is,/Of course, taken from Gaboriau’s “Slaves of
Paris”—Moriarty himself being a resurrected Mascarot-
Tantaine-Martin-Rigal. The, geist of the powerful
Uaboriau sensation is felt throughout the play. When
Prince, the genial burglar, dropping his tools, exclaims,
in consternation, “Wot! Is Sherlock ’Olmes in this?
Then let me out! ”we are reminded of Dr. Hortebize, one
of the blackmailing gang in “ The Slaves,” who, on hear-
ing Lecoq’s name, in a choked voice says, “Stop! If
that Lecoq’s mixed up in this, 1 withdraw Yes,
really, I withdraw.” Sherlock Holmes, who stands for
virtue, is successful as his prototype was successful, but
Alascarot is drawn with more brains than Lecoq, just
as Moriarty, at Sydney Royal, could easily have run rings
round his bombastic rival.
Every now and then an imaginative paragraphist states
that old friend Louis Lohr has wearied of his breezy
ruralism down at Anderson’s Inlet (Vic.), and meditates
a return to theatrical life. But, as a matter of fact, the
genial L.J.L. has gone “ nap ” on his Gippsland hotel,
which is now a quite imposing structure with all the
comforts of civilisation laid on. Lohr’s new tar-room,
detached from the main building, is the biggest thing of
its kind in that quarter of Victoria, and his mobs of Sun-
day customers from the coal-mining townships and
thereabouts are bona-fide travellers who can drink with-
out the option of a fine. And the fishing in the Inlet!
The fish are so numerous and various that they tear one
another off the hook. Lohr never knows what he has
actually caught until it is landed in the yacht. If a
perch takes the bait in the first instance, it is more than
likely to be supplanted by a rock-cod, or a vast
flathead before the line is hauled up. The Inlet was
always a great place for fish stories, and Lohr is going to
publish some of the best in advt. book form. He writes
in a tone of settled contentment. The climate is good,
business is better, and quietude best of all.
For the first time in its life the watch-house in Little
Bourke (Lilly Buk) St., Melbourne, has gone through a
Saturday and Sunday without taking in a lodger, and the
policeman in charge thinks that the ancient terrors of
Little Bourke-street’s name are departing. But he can’t
account for the improvement. The reform is probably
due to Williamson’s conversion of Her Majesty’s into a
fashionable theatre. Thousands of decent people who
never thought of walking up “ Little Bourke ” in the old
days now make it their general thoroughfare to and fro
Her Majesty’s pit and amphitheatre doors. The street i 3
becoming respectable by force of example.
New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency Co.,
Ltd., reports for week ended 22nd Nov. having offered a
fair selection of wool, mostly small growers’ clips.
Buyers were plentiful and competition good. Good
merinos were very firm, while wasty and burry sorts sold
in sellers’ favor. Comebacks and crossbreds showed an
ndvance of 10 per cent, on the last sale. Highest price
for greasy was IOJd. At all sales during past week the
market showed good strength. Sixth and final series of
London sales for this year will open on 24th ; 101,000
bales are available, an exceptionally small quantity,
which indicates that both merinos and crossbreds will
rise materially. Sheepskins sold well. Although tallow
supplies show signs of decreasing the demand shrinks
fister, and quotations are now about ss. below last
week’s. Light-weight hides were not much in demand,
but medium and heavy-weights were taken decidedly In
sellers’ favor. All sundries were firm last week, with title
exception of horsehair, which was slightly weaker.

The End of the “Joint.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mick the Mule came out of Melbourne Gaol.
The busy hum of the streets buzzed in his ears,
accustomed for months past to the deadening
silence of prison. He walked along Russell-street
into'the heart of the city, staring wistfully at
those he passed. His brain heaved in exultation
at his freedom and the prospect of a new life.
• • • *
He had struggled for a sweat-earned crust; but
there are none so cruel as they who sin not; so
lack of references and the mention of his latest
master brought a snort of refusal from each
righteous employer.
Sjg,Mick hungered.
'When his pence reached the level of his pride,
he reluctantly turned to the old haunts in Little
Bourke-street. Afi Sing’s flabby, cauliflower face
stillhshowed behind the mouldy window of the
Quang Joint. But Ah Sing’s memory of past
favors was of the Parliamentary type.
“No sabbee you ! You likee workee longa me,
mejpay. You no likee workee, me no sabbee.”
Mike thought of the opium-den bludger and of
the river—and preferred the river. But first he
tried a last resource.
A strong heavily-barred door opened to his two
scratches and a knock. A game of skittles was
going on within. He walked to a staircase and
ascended carefully, avoiding the alternate steps,
which were “blinds.” He reached a room fitted
up as a gymnasium—the meeting-place of the
Young Men’s Club—according to a notice on the
wall. Mick crossed to the opposite wall and pressed
a hidden button. The panel moved and he stepped
into the Two-Up Joint.
A large, well-lit room, with neither window nor
door. In the middle a 20-foot padded ring,
round which were seats and the scum of the great
city. The language was a blend of a dozen
nations’ profanity. Crafty,' cat-like faces, glaring
at the man holding the “ kip.” “ Snowy’’Brown
stalked about the ring, watching for his com-
mission and crying “ Make your bets ! ” Money
showered on the padded floor, and was quickly
covered. “I’m on heads.” “Tails!” “who’ll
cover heads?” “ A dollar.” “ Make it a thick’un!”
“Bli-me, orlright’’—the “nobs” with their gold,
the “snobs ” with their silver.
“Let her go!” said Snowy, and there was a
deep silence of expectancy. The man with the
kip—with its two large discs, each gilded on one
side, and now lying, one shining, one black—
paused a second. Mike gazed at him, and saw
his own reincarnation. A mere boy, as he had
been; a well-set, well-dressed bank-clerk, as he
had been ; and soon to be a gaol-bird, as he had
been. f, v.-
Up went the coins to the roof, gleaming and
turning before a hundred pairs of greedy, crime-
sodden eyes. They , fell noiselessly, and a chorus
of filthy oaths mingled with Snowy’s “Heads.”
Mick touched one of the winners on the
shoulder. The man turned. “ What-ho, Merley!”
He came out of the crowd. “Out of quod,
blokey! ”
“Yes, Josser ! Lend us a few bob.”
“ Strike me blind ! Is that wot you take a
bloke from a winning seat for ? Y’ve got a blanky
face on yer ! But I’ll put you on to a crook ”
“I’m going square,” said Mick, weakly.
“Oh, Hell! Go to the bloomin’ Harmy ! I
ain’t got nothing for your sort. And you’ve
crooked my luck, too, blast yer ! ”
Mick slunk from the place as another roar of
oaths proclaimed a fourth successive “Heads,’
and Josser went back to the circle. But his lucky
seat had been jumped, and his late adversary,
eyed him suspiciously.
“ A nice put-up job.”
“’Struth, no! Didn’t you see Mick the Mule?”
“ Dicken ! ”
“ Straight wire ! He’s just out of quod, and he
tried a Joe Rail on me for some beans.”
. “D’yer give him any?” said Buck, with
interest.
“ My blanky oath—no ! ”
A light gleamed in Buck’s one eye as he jumped
to his feet. “ Blokes, here’s a sanguinary cow for
you ! You know, coves, how his cobber, Mick
the Mule, kept his trap shut he might have
got this ’ere crimson Josser two years. Mick
took his whack, and now he conies out without a
caser to get a beer—”
“ Make your bets, ” cried Snowy, scenting fight.
“Shut up, Snowy ! ” came a roar,
“And this blanky cow wouldn’t give him a bob.”
Then Josser did a foolish thing—he made for
the exit-panel. But either the latent chivalry of
the Push, or its dog-like delight in a hunt, was
aroused, and it went for Josser like a tornado. He
flew up into the air like a football, his clothes
were torn from him in three seconds, and, naked
but for his boots, he rushed round, swore, fought,
kicked, and raved.
“My God!” cried Snowy, trying to draw the
attention of the excited mob to the entrance of
certain stalwart and grim-looking strangers.
Then, from one of these—“ It’s all up, my lads !
fifty more policemen outside ” —and tall, thin,
sharp-eyed Dunkey smiled at his catch—and
winked at Buck Crow.
* * * «
But Mick chose the river.
GENUNG.
Colonial Experience.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I remember one day when I was a kid,” said
Parkinson, “the old man says to me, ‘Bill, let’s
see how far yer can walk backwards with yer eyes
shut.’ I shut me eyes, an’ walks backwards, an’
was gettin’ along smilin’ when all of a sudden I
sits flop into a big tub of water. The old man,
while I was splutterin’ an’ splashin’, roared fit to
bust. ’Bout a week after, we wur standin’ near
the same place, an’ he says to me, ‘ Bill, let’s see
how far yer can walk backwards with yer eyes
shut.’ I looks round very careful. The old chap
grinned, an’ patted me on the woolly butt. * That’s
experience, Bill,’ he says, ‘wot you an’ every other
chump’s got ter pick up. -Yer’ll find yerself
blunderin’ into many a tub afore you’ve picked up
enough of it to make a handy bushwhacker.’ That
was 40 years ago, an’ I’ve been meetitt* tubs an’
pickin’ up that there experience ever since.
“ rUiiHi'iHirrn into a head
where there was none afore. It’s yer compass
where there’s no tracks— yer divinin’*rod - when
yer want water. Some fellers get it by sheer grit
an’ doggedness, an’ some by seein* other people
break their necks. It doesn’t come to you with a
sudden jump. It’s an expensive acquirement
generally. Take a man that’s been all over the
world in ships an’ trains, an’ so forth ; turn him
adrift ’ere, an’ he’s lost in ten minutes. But the
man that’s knocked about the big sheep or cattle-
runs is at home in any sitchuwation, an’ can pick
himself out of any bush, doan’ matter where.
He’s swallowed the experience. It’s a fine thing
to ’ave in strange places where nobody lives ; but
’tain’t to be gathered up like sticks.
“ When you come to reckon-up, the knowledge
o’ little things in the bush costs a lot one way nil’
another. Not to speak of the annoyances an’ fears
you ’ave, the skin an’ hair yer lose, an’ the knocks
yer get; the learnin’ o’ little wrinkles yer’d think
nothin’ of sometimes costs a thumpin’ stack o’
money.
“I remember once I was paddin’ it along a
telegraph line with a line-repairer, an’ whenever
he saw two birds light on a wire he’d betrme that
a certain one would fly off fust. He was a gambling
monomaniac in that respect. I warn’t long losirr
a poun’. Kept on bettin’, in the hope o’ winnin’
it back, an’doubled it. I dropped to it at last.
The bird that lights fust is always the fust to fly
off. Travellin’ soon afterwards with another
bloke, I reckoned to profit by my experience.
* See them two crows ?’ says I. ‘ Bet yer ten bob
the far one flies off fust.’ 4 Done ! ’ Bidin’ close
to a metal telegraph-post he gives it a smart ' tap
with his whip. The nearest (beautiful) crow flies
off instanter. I paid for some more experience.
Wot sort o’ terbacca d’yer call this ? Tastes un-
common like old bags.”
A mob of cattle emerged from the timber and
momentarily interrupted the dissertation.
“Yer’d think there was no art in ridin’ after
stock, in musterin’or drivin’,” he resumed. 44 It
looks a simple thing to turn a mob o’ rughin’
cattle, or to steer ’em through a gateway when
they’re ringin’. There’s as much knack in it
though, as there is in ridin’ a buck-jumper, or
throwin’ a three-months old bull-calf.
44 Yer’d think there was nothin’, either, in sheep-
farmin’. By the time yer can draft off wet ewes,
an’ dry ewes, an’ maiden ewes, into three separate
pens, by lookin’ at their faces, from a runnin’ string
o’mixed sheep, without blockin’’em or making a
mistake, ’tween smoke-o an’ smoke-o, you’ll begin
to tumble there’s a lot more in it than wot shows
on the surface. That’s one o’ the things that yer
can’t be shown, an’ that can’t be described. Until
yer become familiar with the game, you can’t See
any difference in the ewes; an’ the man who
drafts ’em by the faces can’t explain wot he sees—
’cept that the face of a wet ewe is cleaner of h'&ir
an’ wool, an’ looks happier, than the others’ faces.
Yer’ve got to learn it by experience. See a straw,
anywheres ? Can’t stand a pipe wot sucks or
whistles ? Gives me the dunnowots.
“ There’s any gorsquantity o’ things yer come
ter know an’ thoroughly understand, an’ yet yer
can’t tell ’em or learn ’em to another. It’s
acquired by long familyeraraty. I heard a fellow
say once that it was the external he-quivalent to
instink. A miner would say it was the precious
gilt that was mined out of all sorts of hardships,
an’ a farmer would put it as it was the fruit that
growed on a bitter tree called blunders. If yb’re
long in the company of even a workin’ bullock
yer’ll see different expressions in that bullock’s
face. In the same way yer come to see plain
English written in yer dog’s face ; to know when
he thinks yer a hero, an’ when he reckons yer. a
flamin’ lunatic, which is purty often, an’ yer come
to understand the moods an’ emotions of even, a
goanna. Anybody that’s got a bit o’ gumption at
all can tell when a horse is pleased, or narked; or
frightened ; an’ when he’s tired or sick ; but the
man who handles an’ rides the prad anyways
reg’lar sees a lot more than a stranger couldn’t
twig with a telescope. It all comes natch’rel
when yer go through the proper course o’ tubs.
“ Years ago ‘Colonyal Experience’ was qqite
an institootion in its way. Swells uster send
their sons out to stations to learn it. They’d get
10 or 15 bob a week maybe, an’ ’ave to rough it in
the yards an’ out-camps, an’ the bush gen’ally,
same as any other knockabout. At first we uster
look up to them an’ call ’em ‘Mister So-an’-so,’
an’ think them lucky beggars to have plenty o’
money an’ be doin’ this sort o’ work just for the
fun o’ it. But when ive got more experience, we
found that their people were mostly ragged swells
who couldn’t afford to keep ’em, or else they were
good-for-nothin’s turned out of the old home to
shift for themselves. They weren’t men enough t*Q
own up as they had ter go an' graft for a livin’.
After that, we nicknamed each one accordin’ to
his pertickler perculyeraraty, an’ uster pitch ’em
fairies about how we wur learnin’ colonyal, an’
gettin’ remittances from ’Ome.
“Sometimes yer meet a bullock-puncher on the
track that’s not well-up to the game. Has trouble
in every bit o’ loose ground, an’ gets bogged to his
eyes in every worm-track of a crick. He’s hoarse
from shoutin’, an’ blessed near blind with sweat.
Even the beastly flies seem to know lie’s worried,
an’ injiy tormentin’ him. He makes a swipe at
Straggler—an’ knocks his hat off. Straggler looks
round to see wot’s ’appened. The others switch
their tails. Some coughs. That driver’s nearly
boilin’. He can’t get his cussology goin’, an’
makes ap awful jumble of. it.. Then Leopard
starts feedin’ ; an’ that makes him madder. He
makes a tremenjus swipe at him with the whip ;
but it gets round his own neck somehow, an’ darn
near jerks his blamin’ head off—an’ breaks the
handle.
“Then an old driver comes along. He takes
the damaged whip, straightens the team up with
a word or two, just gives a high-up flourish—an'
away they go. To look at him yer’d think bullock -
punchin’ was the simplest thing in the world !
It’s like everything else —just wants a little
experience.
44 1 s’pose I’ve had as much of it as anyone, ’ere
or there ; an’ it’s put a bit o’ money in me pocket,
too. You can make a lot o’ money in the bush
with experience. I put by a ’und’ed quid once in
less’n ten years. It was my fust taste o’ wealth,
an’ I reckoned I’d go to Sydney an’ ’ave some tun
with it. When I got there I takes a tram as the
easiest way out to a friend o’ mine. That blanky
conductor uster come for tickets every five
minutes. I didn’t cotton to the game yer know.
When the darn thing was stoppin’ I makes a
dinnyaiser to get out of it as I see others doin’.
But they didn’t land like me at all. The road
seemed to buck from under me, an’ the fust o’ me
to get there was the back o’ me head. I looked a
proper fool, Lean tell you, with dust all over me
new black suit, standin’ there waitifi’ for the
bloomin’ tram to get off me hat.
“Some spavmed-kneed hoodlum comes up an’
says I was foolish to jump front a movin’ tram
when I had no experience. ‘ Exper ’ I-gasped.
Then I went an’ had a long beer. I ’ad several of
him, in fact. But I didn’t get drunk. I’d been
in that tub
EDWARD S. SORENSON.

Everyone knows the purpose of
a water filter-—to filter the im-
purities from water.
Have, you ever reflected for a
moment on the fact that you have
filters in your system a thousand
times more intricate and delicate
than the water thousand
times more important, too 1
These filters are the kidneys.
It is their duty to filter from the
blood the impurities which cause disease,
and to pass them from the system.
But sometimes the kidneys get sick through a fall or
strain, exposure, excessive use of liquor, pei'liaps, or one
of many other causes. Then they become clogged.
It’s no use to try to filter water with a clogged filter ; before the water
can be purified the filter must be cleansed. It should always be kept clean.
The same, exactly, with your kidneys. When they arp clogged they
cannot filter the blood properly ; then certain impurities a,re left in the system
that poison every nerve and organ. These poisons, this unfiltered blood,
cause Anaemia, Jaundice, Eczema, Pimples, Boils, Blotches, Piles, A r aricose
Veins, Sallow Complexion, as well as Female Troubles, Neuralgia, Headaches,
Melancholia, and a hundred other ills. 1
A tonic or purgative medicine will often give temporary relief in cases of
this kind, but the trouble soon returns. It is NO USE to doctor the symp-
toms if you want a lasting cure-—you must go to the root of the trouble and
doctor that. • ■
A gardener who is weeding does not clip'off the body of the weed, but
pulls it out by the root." The kidneys are the root of troubles brought about
by impure blood, and ; , ;
Doan’s Backache Kidney pills
are a medicine specially prepared for Kidney Disorders. They will never
fail to gently aid and soothe the kidneys, and to cleanse them from'im-
purities. They cure the root of your trouble, not merely the symptoms.
Entirely vegetable in their composition, they do not injure the system, nor
purge. .
You are not asked to believe our statement alone, nor the word of a
stranger living in a distant town, but you are asked to place confidence
in the word of a fellow-resident, who frankly speaks out for the public
benefit.
SYDNEY PROOF.
Mr. W. Hibberd, of St. Paul’s-street, Randwick, this city, says : —“ For
years I have been a sufferer from backache. In my work I have to stoop a good
deal, and prolonged exertion of this kind'would double me up with pain. After
stooping I would be scarcely able to stand up straight again, but would have to
unbend gradually, and get the kinks out of my back. I used many sorts of
medicine for this ache, but with poor success. The doctors I saw were also
puzzled, I think. I procured a box of Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, and I'am
very pleased to tell you that these pills g-ave me almost immediate relief. They
went right to the root of my trouble. The pains diminished after further treat-
ment, and at length totally disappeared. I shall always recommend these pills
for backache trouble.” - .
Twelve months later Mrs. Hibberd states:—“lt is now more than a year
ago since my husband used Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills for backache. lam
now enabled to say that the pills wrought a permanent cure in his case. The
pains never retimed since. I can speak with conviction on the marvellous way
they acted in his case. He is very enthusiastic about the pills, and is always
recommending them for such ailments as the pills are meant for. He had been
bad over ten years, so you can see it is no common remedy that could cure him
the way Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills did.” '

DANTON.
Our New Selection.
(HOW DAD FELL OUT WITH DALY.)

Tears ran from Daly’s eyes.

“ Now, don’t get angry over it,” mother inter-

But Dad would never suffer a contradiction.
.“Here!” he shouted, violently displacing a
portion of the tablecloth and brandishing his

savagely scratched a tracing, something like a

Daly smiled and, when Dad glared round at

“■ There’s th’ river/”— raking the knife through

Daly, watching closely over Dad’s shoulder,

“ An’ that’s th’ bridge?” —gashing the river in
two.
“ Correct,” Daly said, grinning more.
“ Here’s Windmill Hill; here’s Rafferty’s pub.
in South Brisbane” (Dad dug a hole in the table
to mark the pub., and mother shuddered); “ and
here, just behind Rafferty’s, is a ridge, an’ a

water, an’a lot O’ scrub runnin’ right along this
way ’’—(hacking another channel in the cedar).

STEELE RUDD.
Some time ago The Bulletin a
(true enough) story of how grog was secretly
distilled, and enjoyed, by prisoners in Darling-
hurst Gaol out of material unwittingly provided
by the authorities to confinees. Here is a yarn to
match : *
Some dozen years ago I was a warder in Pentridge
(Vic.) prison, and one day self and mate came across a
bit of tin sticking out of a heap of spalls. “ Here’s a
plant, Jack,” he said, and we burrowed into the heap
until we found a pig excellently cured, encased in the tin
lining of a ling-fish box. The bacon-raising industry was,
as appeared at the subsequent inquiry, entirely intra-
mural ; the pig had been bred, fattened, killed and cured
inside the walls. First as to the abstraction of the pig
from the piggeries : The authorities keep a careful check
of the numbers of the pigs, but when a brood sow added
to the pig population, the prisoner in charge suppressed
one of the litter, and hid it in the straw until the official
count had been made. Then he put the little pig back
into stock and removed a big one for slaughtering pur-
poses. As to the curing, the prisoners in the scheme had
all been instructed to save their allowance of salt, and
thus sufficient for the purpose was collected. Then as to
fchecooking : A slush-lamp was improvised by means of a
jam-tin and a piece of moleskin tied round a stick and
saturated in grease, saved from the allowance for greasing
boots, and with a pieoe of sheet steel from the workshop
as a pan, the prisoners had been in the habit of enjoying
an occasional variation from the prison fare. For some
time before the discovery in the spall heap we had sniffed
the smell of cooking bacon, but-had not suspected the
truth.
The Eastern Extension -cum -S’African
Cable Co. is rpn principally on Chow, Malay, and
boy labor. On the .Australian stations lads pre-
dominate on the stalls. Those.young beginners
are quartered on the station, and have to -pay for
their mess at the same price as the officers
drawing greater salaries, a distinct injustice
where only £4O is the initial pay. On
the cable ship, which is manned at Singa-
pore, an Englishman generally does the
' splicing, assisted by about 40 Chows to turn the
cable. Should the Celestials pull it too tight,, or
vice-versa, the splicer will yell tQ_tho_Jiunion
nearest him, “Good Heavens! what are you up
to ? ” The leading Chow will yell, “ Goo’ ’evelins !
wha’ yar ooploo ? ” And the query, which is
wholly unintelligible to the yellow men, is passed
along the line —quite unconcernedly.

“T.D. ” : One cogent reason the police-
man has for refraining from “dealing it out”
to obstreperous drunks until they reach the
privacy of the lock-up cells, is that public
sympathy leans almost invariably towards the
'latter. Years ago, in Maoriland, T witnessed the
capture of a mad-drunk person who had run amok
with ahatchet, scared the wits out of numerous
citizens, and absolutely cleared several streets of
any semblance of life except a butcher’s pony
which was filching lunch’from-the overturned
baskets of a scared Chinaman.. . iDtffiedin is a hilly
place, and the roysterer was.eventually seized half
way up the most precipitous street to be found
even there. He fell with the' policeman on top of
him, and the two went rolling down the hill, one
over the other, accumulating thick layers of mud
and many excited spectators as they went. They
came to a stop outside the mansion of a well-to-do
old lady who hadn’t seen the earlier proceedings;
and who was positively horrified to observe a torn
and bleeding and muddy policeman sitting solidly
upon the stomach of his captive. She asked no
questions, but rushed out and made a spirited
effort to drag him away, vowing "meanwhile that
she would inform the proper authorities that he
was a cowardly brute, and ought to be dismissed
and gaoled for half murdering a small and-inoffen-
sive citizen, And the, crowd, which wouldn’t
have been there if it had se.en the victim’s blood-
.thirsty diversions with. the . hatchet, cheered the
‘old lady. vociferously, and would probably-ffiave
helped her I®.liberate that muddy object but for
the timely arrival of other policemen and a boy
who had picked up the culprit’s little axe.
